* a powerful backend with a weak user relationship
* a capable intelligence that still feels detached from the place where work actually happens

Lens exists to prevent that collapse.

It is the visible expression of:

* current presence
* current mode
* current scope
* current mission state
* current incidents
* current approvals
* current takeover authority
* current continuity state
* current receipts and handoffs

### 4.1.1 Why Francis needs a signature surface

A detached chat interface creates structural weakness.

It forces the user to:

* leave the work surface
* reconstruct context manually
* translate the current situation into prompts
* map answers back into the environment by hand
* remember what Francis can currently see or act on
* manually discover what is urgent, blocked, or staged

That may still be useful. But it is not the category Francis is trying to create.

Francis needs a signature surface because its promise is not merely to answer questions. Its promise is to participate lawfully in the actual flow of work.

The product must make that participation visible.

### 4.1.2 Lens is not an accessory

A weak build path would treat Lens as something that can be added later, after the “real intelligence” is done.

That is the wrong model.

Without Lens, the user experience of Francis will drift toward:

* prompt dependence
* detached interaction
* weak mode visibility
* poor scope legibility
* low operational presence
* shallow continuity feeling
* hidden or abstract governance

Lens is how governance, continuity, missions, control transfer, and in-place assistance become tangible.

### 4.1.3 Lens is part of the trust model

A powerful system that acts in the environment must expose itself in the environment.

Lens is therefore not only a usability layer.
It is also part of the trust contract.

The user should be able to feel through Lens:

* what Francis is aware of
* what Francis is doing
* what Francis is allowed to do
* what Francis is asking to do
* what Francis has done
* what remains pending
* how to stop or narrow authority

That makes Lens part of governance, not just presentation.

---

## 4.2 The Core Goal of Lens

The core goal of Lens is simple to say and difficult to execute:

**Francis should feel like it lives across the system, appears where relevant, disappears when not needed, and collaborates in place instead of constantly pulling the user into a separate AI tab.**

This goal has several layers.

### 4.2.1 Lens must make Francis feel resident

Francis should feel like a resident operator presence, not like a summoned consultant.

That means Lens must make the user feel:

* Francis is here
* Francis understands the local situation
* Francis can intervene at the point of need
* Francis is carrying continuity across surfaces
* Francis has a stable relationship to the environment
* Francis is not trapped in a separate conversation box

Residence is one of the main emotional thresholds in the product.

### 4.2.2 Lens must make Francis feel disciplined

A resident presence can become oppressive if poorly designed.

Lens must therefore also make Francis feel disciplined.

It should not feel like:

* pop-up spam
* attention theft
* ambient pressure
* omnipresent nagging
* theatrical overlay clutter
* system-wide interruption for trivial issues

The correct feeling is:

* calm
* available
* relevant
* bounded
* inspectable
* non-invasive

Residence without discipline becomes creepiness.
Residence with discipline becomes trust.

### 4.2.3 Lens must make Francis feel operational

Lens must not reduce Francis to “UI around chat.”

It must make the user feel that Francis is an operational system.

That means Lens should naturally expose:

* missions
* incidents
* approvals
* control modes
* scope
* takeover
* continuity
* receipts
* delegation
* return briefings

These are not side panels for aesthetics.
They are the working surfaces of an Operator Layer.

---

## 4.3 Lens Principles

Before defining specific primitives, Lens needs design law.

These principles should guide every panel, chip, indicator, queue, and interaction.

### 4.3.1 In-place over detached

When possible, Francis should assist where the work is already happening.

A suggestion attached to the actual error is stronger than a suggestion hidden in a side conversation.
An approval request near the relevant action is stronger than a generic queue with no context.
A mission hint adjacent to the current work surface is stronger than an isolated dashboard elsewhere.

Lens should bias toward in-place relevance.

### 4.3.2 Relevant over ambient

Not everything needs to be shown all the time.

Lens should favor what is meaningful now, not what is theoretically displayable.

A mature Lens does not try to prove that Francis is always active.
It proves that Francis is active where it matters.

### 4.3.3 Calm over dramatic

Lens should never feel frantic, game-like, or over-animated.

The product should not use motion, glow, interruption, or cinematic framing to simulate importance.

If something is important, the importance should come from reality:

* a real risk
* a real mission dependency
* a real approval requirement
* a real active takeover
* a real issue affecting continuity

Calm does not mean boring.
It means composed.

### 4.3.4 Visible power over hidden power

Lens must make authority legible.

If Francis is sensing, the user should know.
If Francis is in Pilot, the user should know.
If Francis is in Away, the user should know.
If Francis is waiting for approval, the user should know.
If Francis is acting within a scoped mission, the user should know.

Lens is one of the primary places where hidden power is prevented.

### 4.3.5 Silence is a feature

A weak Lens tries to fill the screen.
A mature Lens respects silence.

Francis should know when not to intrude.

That means Lens should often:

* remain minimal
* defer to focus
* compress low-priority items
* avoid interrupting ongoing flow
* present optional affordances instead of compulsory interruptions

A good Operator Layer does not need to be loud to feel present.

### 4.3.6 Governance must be felt, not buried

Lens should not hide governance in settings menus or obscure diagnostic pages.

The user should be able to feel governance through the normal interface:

* visible mode state
* visible scope
* visible approvals
* visible pilot indicator
* visible away indicator
* visible kill pathways
* visible receipt access
* visible connector state

That is productized governance.

---

## 4.4 Lens Primitives

Lens needs a stable set of primitives that define its product language.

These primitives are not arbitrary UI components. They are the visible grammar of Francis.

### 4.4.1 Always-on-top HUD layer

There must be a lightweight, system-level HUD capability that allows Francis to exist across applications without requiring the user to fully context-switch into a dedicated window.

This HUD should support:

* current mode visibility
* quick summon
* mission presence
* incident summaries
* pilot visibility
* away visibility
* compact approval state
* quick access to control surfaces

The HUD should be light enough to feel persistent and serious enough to feel operational.

### 4.4.2 Action chips near relevant UI

One of the most important 4th-wall primitives is the action chip.

These are small, contextual, high-value prompts that appear near the relevant work surface.

Examples might include:

* near a build error
* near a failing unit
* near a blocked mission step
* near a risky action requiring approval
* near a recurring friction point
* near a staged improvement opportunity

These chips should not be decorative.
They should represent real, bounded, context-aware affordances.

### 4.4.3 Docking panels

Lens should support docking surfaces for deeper work.

These may include panels for:

* mission feed
* incident feed
* approvals
* receipts
* active run inspection
* scope and mode inspection
* continuity briefing
* takeover controls

Docking matters because not all interaction should be overlay-light. Some operator activities need room.

### 4.4.4 Command palette summon

Francis should be summonable instantly from anywhere.

A command palette style entry point matters because the user must always have a fast path to:

* ask
* inspect
* delegate
* change modes
* view mission state
* approve
* stop
* narrow scope
* trigger takeover
* request briefings

The summon surface should feel native, fast, and always there when needed.

### 4.4.5 Mission feed

Missions are central to Francis’s identity. Therefore Lens must include a first-class mission feed.

This feed should show things like:

* active missions
* stalled missions
* blocked missions
* safe next steps
* pending approvals
* recent mission progress
* handoffs
* return-to-work recommendations

This is not just productivity UI. It is how continuity becomes visible.

### 4.4.6 Incident feed

Francis needs a first-class incident surface.

The incident feed should make it possible to see:

* anomalies
* failures
* regressions
* risk threshold crossings
* suspicious inputs
* broken assumptions
* validation failures
* environmental drift

It should help the user understand what needs attention without turning the product into a wall of alarms.

### 4.4.7 Approval queue

Approvals must not live only as interruptions. They also need a coherent queue surface.

The approval queue should make it possible to inspect:

* what is waiting
* why it needs approval
* what scope it touches
* what risk tier it belongs to
* what evidence supports it
* what happens if approved
* what happens if denied
* how it relates to mission state

Approvals should feel reviewable, not mysterious.

### 4.4.8 Takeover feed

Pilot Mode is one of Francis’s signature features. It therefore deserves a dedicated feed or live execution surface.

The takeover feed should show:

* that Pilot is active
* what scope is active
* what actions are underway
* what the current run is doing
* what was completed
* what remains
* what can be interrupted
* how to hand back cleanly

A system that can “become your hands” must make those hands visible.

### 4.4.9 Visible Pilot indicator

The Pilot indicator is identity-critical.

Whenever Francis has active delegated execution authority, this must be obvious.

Not hidden in a corner.
Not inferable from subtle styling.
Obvious.

The user should never wonder whether Francis is currently in Pilot.

### 4.4.10 Visible telemetry and Away indicators

If Francis is sensing within approved scope, that should be visible.
If Francis is in Away continuation, that should be visible.

The product must never create ambiguity about whether Francis is:

* watching
* dormant
* ready
* actively continuing
* in delegated execution
* waiting for input
* constrained by mode

Indicator clarity is part of the law.

---

## 4.5 Lens Must Make Francis Feel In-Place

The defining success condition of Lens is not that it looks futuristic.

It is that Francis feels **in-place**.

That means the product should not constantly drag the user into abstraction. It should make help, action, governance, and continuity appear where they matter.

### 4.5.1 In-place assistance

If there is a relevant issue in the current work surface, Francis should be able to surface help adjacent to that issue.

Examples include:

* inline fix suggestions
* contextual explanation
* scoped action options
* approval prompts
* local mission context
* risk explanation
* receipt lookup for recent related work

This reduces translation friction between seeing the problem and invoking the system.

### 4.5.2 In-place governance

Governance should also appear in place.

For example:

* a risky action should show why approval is needed
* a blocked mission step should show the governing dependency
* a pilot surface should show active authority where the work is happening
* a denied action should show the policy reason clearly
* a scope boundary should be visible at the point it matters

This helps the user experience governance as clarity rather than arbitrary obstruction.

### 4.5.3 In-place continuity

Continuity should not only appear at session start. It should travel with the work.

That means Lens should help the user see:

* why this thing matters now
* what happened recently here
* what is still unresolved
* what has already been tried
* what Francis staged nearby
* how this local problem relates to broader mission flow

That is how the machine starts feeling coherent.

---

## 4.6 Lens Is Not Spyware

One of the biggest risks for any system trying to feel resident across the machine is crossing the line into perceived or actual invasiveness.

Francis Lens must explicitly reject that path.

### 4.6.1 Lens intelligence should not depend on indiscriminate capture

Lens should not require broad, uncontrolled screen scraping to appear useful.

Its intelligence should come primarily from:

* declared scope
* high-signal telemetry
* structured artifacts
* mission state
* approved connectors
* local evidence
* user-approved context surfaces

A system that “feels magical” by observing too much is not trustworthy magic. It is weak governance disguised as capability.

### 4.6.2 What Lens must never silently capture

Lens must never create the feeling that the system is casually drinking from the whole machine.

It must never silently capture or monitor by default:

* passwords
* password managers
* clipboard contents by default
* unrelated windows
* private folders outside scope
* global keystrokes
* camera or microphone without explicit enablement
* sensitive surfaces not clearly included in active scope

The 4th-wall promise must never become a pretext for ambient invasion.

### 4.6.3 Visible sensing matters

If Francis is using approved telemetry to support Lens relevance, there should be visible indication that sensing is active.

Not because the user is fragile.
Because hidden sensing destroys trust.

A lawful Lens should feel like:

> I understand why this appeared here, and I understand what Francis is allowed to know.

That is the difference between presence and creepiness.

---

## 4.7 Lens and the Modes of Francis

Lens is one of the primary ways Control Modes become real to the user.

If modes remain abstract backend states, the law will feel weak. Lens must therefore render modes in a way that is immediate, legible, and behaviorally meaningful.

### 4.7.1 Observe through Lens

In Observe, Lens should feel calm and low-pressure.

It may show:

* quiet awareness
* mission presence
* anomaly notices
* contextual suggestions
* continuity reminders
* state summaries

But it should not feel like active execution is imminent.

Observe through Lens should communicate:

> Francis is aware, but not acting.

### 4.7.2 Assist through Lens

In Assist, Lens should feel collaborative.

It may show:

* proposals
* drafts
* action chips
* explanations
* plans
* scope-aware next-step options
* approval preparation

Assist through Lens should communicate:

> Francis sees the move and can help you make it, but has not taken the wheel.

### 4.7.3 Pilot through Lens

In Pilot, Lens must feel unmistakably active.

It should show:

* active pilot indicator
* declared scope
* live action feed
* run progress
* current step
* interrupt options
* receipts forming in real time or near real time
* handback state when complete

Pilot through Lens should communicate:

> Francis is acting now under your delegated authority.

### 4.7.4 Away through Lens

In Away, Lens may not always be actively visible while the user is absent, but it must support clear re-entry and visible continuity.

When the user returns, Lens should make Away visible through:

* shift report
* what changed
* what advanced
* what failed
* what staged
* what is awaiting approval
* what remains bounded and incomplete

Away through Lens should communicate:

> Francis kept lawful momentum alive while you were gone.

### 4.7.5 Why mode rendering matters

If the user cannot feel the mode, they cannot truly feel the authority state.

Mode rendering is therefore not aesthetic.
It is part of governance and trust calibration.

---

## 4.8 The Emotional Feel of Lens

Lens is a product surface, but it is also a psychological surface.

It shapes how the user emotionally experiences Francis.

A bad Lens can make a powerful system feel:

* noisy
* invasive
* toy-like
* theatrical
* manipulative
* cluttered
* exhausting
* untrustworthy

A good Lens can make the same system feel:

* composed
* watchful
* competent
* companionable
* lawful
* steady
* clear
* empowering

### 4.8.1 The correct feeling

The correct emotional tone of Lens is:

* calm
* clean
* precise
* quietly powerful
* never needy
* never ad-like
* never chaotic
* never gimmicky

It should feel more like a command surface than a social app.
More like an operational HUD than a chat toy.
More like a trusted system layer than an attention-harvesting product.

### 4.8.2 Cinematic without fake

Francis may feel cinematic in the sense that it feels extraordinary to use.

But Lens must never achieve that through fake sci-fi excess.

No empty spectacle.
No gratuitous animation.
No glowing nonsense trying to simulate intelligence.
No theatrical clutter replacing real product value.

If Lens feels cinematic, it should be because the underlying experience is genuinely powerful, coherent, and in-place.

### 4.8.3 Presence without pressure

One of the hardest emotional design balances is making Francis feel present without making the user feel watched, pushed, or crowded.

Lens must achieve:

* presence without pressure
* guidance without nagging
* availability without insistence
* capability without intimidation
* continuity without surveillance feeling

That balance is central to the product.

---

## 4.9 Lens Failure Modes

This section must name what Lens can become when built poorly.

### 4.9.1 Lens drifts into “floating chat widget”

Symptoms:

* detached conversation bubble
* little context awareness
* weak in-place relevance
* mission and governance hidden elsewhere
* Francis still feels like an app on top, not a layer across

Correction:

* re-center the Operator Layer manifestation and in-place assistance

### 4.9.2 Lens drifts into “dashboard wall”

Symptoms:

* lots of panels
* low contextuality
* static status-heavy design
* weak interaction with actual work surfaces
* too much reading, not enough situated action

Correction:

* re-center contextual surfaces, action chips, and local relevance

### 4.9.3 Lens drifts into “overlay spam”

Symptoms:

* too many interruptions
* constant chips and suggestions
* low signal-to-noise ratio
* focus disruption
* user starts ignoring Francis altogether

Correction:

* re-center calmness, silence, and relevance thresholds

### 4.9.4 Lens drifts into “sci-fi theater”

Symptoms:

* flashy motion
* theatrical chrome
* cinematic styling without operational benefit
* identity carried by aesthetics instead of capability and governance

Correction:

* re-center groundedness, discipline, and serious product purpose

### 4.9.5 Lens drifts into “invisible power”

Symptoms:

* unclear sensing state
* unclear mode state
* unclear pilot authority
* hidden away continuation
* receipts not easy to access
* user cannot tell what Francis is doing

Correction:

* re-center visible power and governance-through-surface

### 4.9.6 Lens drifts into “creepy layer”

Symptoms:

* suggestions that feel sourced from unclear sensing
* cross-surface awareness that exceeds declared scope
* presence that feels invasive
* weak or absent telemetry indicators
* user begins to distrust how Francis knows what it knows

Correction:

* re-center scope, explicit signals, visible sensing, and redaction discipline

---

## 4.10 Lens as a Productization Requirement

Lens is not only a concept for early prototypes. It is part of the long-term product identity.

A product claiming to be an OS-layer Operator Layer must eventually have a product surface that feels native to that claim.

### 4.10.1 Lens should feel like a real system layer

As productization matures, Lens should increasingly feel like:

* part of the machine
* stable across sessions
* accessible instantly
* integrated with startup behavior
* compatible with hotkey summon
* recoverable after crash
* inspectable through consistent surfaces
* resilient under daily-driver use

This matters because Francis is not supposed to feel like a dev sandbox forever.

### 4.10.2 Lens is where category becomes tangible

Many systems can claim:

* continuity
* mode control
* mission intelligence
* approvals
* governed execution
* operator presence

But Lens is where those claims become legible enough for the user to actually feel them.

It is where the category becomes concrete.

Without Lens, the category remains mostly conceptual.
With Lens, the category becomes experiential.

---

## 4.11 Lens Statement

If Francis Lens had to be defined in one product statement for builders, it would be this:

> Francis Lens is the calm, in-place, governance-visible 4th-wall product surface through which Francis becomes experientially real as an OS-layer Operator Layer, showing presence, missions, approvals, incidents, modes, takeover, continuity, and receipts exactly where they matter without becoming noisy, creepy, detached, or theatrical.

That statement should be strong enough to reject weak interface directions early.

---

## 4.12 Lens Acceptance Criteria

Section 4 is satisfied only if the roadmap, interface design, and architecture remain aligned to all of the following:

* Lens is treated as the signature product surface, not a later cosmetic layer
* Lens is the visible expression of Francis as an Operator Layer
* the interface makes Francis feel resident across the environment, not trapped in a detached chat tab
* Lens prioritizes in-place relevance over detached interaction
* Lens preserves calmness, silence, and discipline rather than maximizing interruption
* governance is visible through Lens, including mode, scope, sensing, approvals, Pilot, Away, and receipts
* Lens includes stable primitives for HUD presence, action chips, docking panels, command summon, mission feed, incident feed, approval queue, and takeover visibility
* Lens does not depend on indiscriminate capture or ambiguous sensing to feel intelligent
* Lens never creates spyware-like or creepy presence
* Lens renders Observe, Assist, Pilot, and Away in experientially distinct ways
* Lens makes takeover authority unmistakably visible
* Lens supports continuity and re-entry after away time
* Lens feels cinematic only through real product power, never through fake sci-fi theater
* Lens failure modes are explicitly recognized and corrected during design and buildout
* Lens productization is treated as core to Francis becoming a true OS-layer system rather than a capable backend with a weak user relationship

# 5.0 Work Telemetry: “Watching What I Do” the Right Way

Francis cannot become a true Operator Layer unless it can perceive the shape of real work.

But the moment a system begins to “watch,” it enters one of the most sensitive boundaries in the entire product.

That boundary is where usefulness and creepiness meet.
Where operational awareness and surveillance can be confused.
Where high-value context and overcollection can blur.
Where the promise of “understanding what I’m doing” can either become the core unlock of the product or the fastest way to destroy trust.

That is why Work Telemetry must be treated as doctrine, not implementation detail.

Francis must understand real work context without becoming invasive.
It must gain meaningful situational awareness without becoming a broad capture system.
It must improve relevance without normalizing ambient overreach.

This section defines the law of Work Telemetry:

* why Francis needs it
* what kinds of signals matter
* what signals must be avoided or tightly governed
* how telemetry must be scoped, redacted, and indicated
* why telemetry is not the same thing as surveillance
* how telemetry supports missions, continuity, Lens, and takeover
* what failure modes must be blocked from the start

The central question of this section is:

> How can Francis understand what the user is doing well enough to be extraordinary, without crossing the line into hidden or excessive observation?

The answer is not “capture everything.”
That is the weakest possible answer.

The correct answer is:

**high-signal, scope-bound, user-legible, redacted, minimally retained, governance-first telemetry.**

Francis must watch the right things, for the right reasons, under the right law.

---

## 5.1 Why Francis Needs Work Telemetry

Francis cannot assist in place, build continuity, detect friction, support apprenticeship, or operate as a real Digital Twin if it has no grounded visibility into the user’s work environment.

Without telemetry, Francis becomes overdependent on:

* explicit prompting
* user restatement
* static documents
* retrospective explanations
* guesswork about current context
* fragmented snapshots of activity

That kind of system may still be useful, but it will remain detached from the actual flow of work.

Work Telemetry is what allows Francis to move from:

* reactive conversation
* isolated requests
* shallow assistance
* generic advice

into:

* contextual assistance
* mission-aware intervention
* grounded relevance
* in-place operational support

### 5.1.1 Telemetry is what makes context real

Context cannot be built only from what the user says.

Real work context often includes signals like:

* what repo is active
* what file just changed
* what command just failed
* what build just broke
* which tests regressed
* what environment is currently in use
* what recurring action pattern is emerging
* what window or project is actually active

Without some lawful access to those signals, Francis will always be partially blind.

### 5.1.2 Telemetry supports the core identity

Work Telemetry is not a side feature. It feeds multiple identity pillars at once.

It supports:

* **Operator Layer** by showing what is happening in the environment
* **Digital Twin** by exposing patterns in how the user actually works
* **Presence** by allowing Francis to be relevant at the right moment
* **Mission-bearing behavior** by tying live activity to longer arcs of work
* **Continuity** by preserving operational story across sessions
* **Lens** by enabling in-place interventions
* **Takeover** by grounding delegation in the actual work surface
* **Away Mode** by helping Francis continue lawful awareness while the user is gone
* **Forge** by revealing recurring friction worth eliminating
* **Apprenticeship** by giving structure to demonstrations and repeated workflows

This is why telemetry must be powerful enough to matter and disciplined enough to trust.

### 5.1.3 Telemetry is not the product center

Even though telemetry is essential, it must never become the center of Francis’s identity.

Francis is not a monitoring product.

Telemetry exists to support:

* relevance
* continuity
* action quality
* governance
* mission understanding
* apprenticeship
* capability growth

Telemetry is an enabling layer, not the star of the show.

If telemetry becomes the emotional center of the product, Francis risks collapsing into a health tool, a monitoring suite, or a suspicious observer rather than an Operator Layer.

---

## 5.2 The Correct Philosophy of Telemetry

The wrong philosophy is:

> Capture as much as possible so the system can be smart.

That philosophy produces systems that are difficult to govern, difficult to explain, difficult to trust, and easy to resent.

The correct philosophy is:

> Capture the minimum high-signal information necessary to make Francis meaningfully better within declared scope, under visible governance.

This is a radically different posture.

### 5.2.1 High signal over total capture

Francis should not try to know everything.

It should try to know the right things.

High-signal telemetry means Francis prioritizes sources that carry strong operational meaning, such as:

* file changes in scope
* git diffs
* build outcomes
* test results
* terminal command results
* IDE diagnostics
* mission-linked anomalies
* scoped app focus where relevant
* approved browser console or network failures when useful

These signals say a lot about work without requiring indiscriminate capture of the entire machine.

### 5.2.2 Declared scope over ambient access

Telemetry must not be ambiently system-wide by default.

It must be:

* opt-in
* scope-bound
* inspectable
* mode-aware
* connector-specific where appropriate
* revocable
* redacted where needed

A system that gains relevance through broad ambient access is not showing strength. It is showing governance weakness.

### 5.2.3 Telemetry exists to serve the user, not to consume the user

This is a deep philosophical boundary.

Francis should never create the feeling that the user is being turned into a stream of exhaust data for the system’s benefit.

Telemetry must always feel like:

* bounded observation in service of assistance
* contextual awareness in service of missions
* lawful sensing in service of continuity
* user-authorized visibility in service of force multiplication

Never extraction for its own sake.

---

## 5.3 High-Signal Telemetry Sources

Francis should prefer telemetry sources that are information-dense, operationally relevant, and governable.

These are signals that say a lot about work while exposing far less than broad raw capture.

### 5.3.1 File changes in approved scope

One of the highest-signal telemetry sources is file change activity inside declared scope.

This can help Francis understand:

* what project is active
* which surfaces are changing
* what kind of work is underway
* whether a mission is progressing
* whether repeated edits suggest friction
* whether a watched file or artifact changed unexpectedly

File change telemetry is powerful because it reveals motion without needing full indiscriminate content ingestion by default.

### 5.3.2 Git diffs, branches, and commit history

Git state is one of the richest contextual signals available in technical workflows.

It can reveal:

* active feature direction
* recency of meaningful work
* branches in play
* incomplete or divergent progress
* repeated conflict patterns
* change concentration
* progression toward mission milestones

Git telemetry should be one of the central sources for technical-domain Francis behavior.

### 5.3.3 Terminal commands and outputs

Terminals are often the most direct surface of real work, especially in technical and operational domains.

Approved terminal telemetry can help Francis understand:

* what command was attempted
* whether it succeeded or failed
* what tools are in use
* what environment is active
* what error patterns are recurring
* whether a workflow is routine or exploratory
* whether a mission step has advanced or stalled

This is one of the most valuable telemetry layers, but it is also one of the most sensitive and must be governed carefully.

### 5.3.4 Build, lint, and test logs

These are especially valuable because they are already highly compressed operational truth.

They can tell Francis:

* whether the build is broken
* whether the failure is new or known
* what class of quality issue is emerging
* whether validation passed
* whether a staged change is safe to claim as improved
* whether repeated failures suggest structural drift

These logs often provide better signal than raw broader capture.

### 5.3.5 IDE diagnostics and active editor context

Where connected and approved, IDE diagnostics can give Francis strong contextual awareness with relatively bounded exposure.

Useful signals may include:

* active file identity
* diagnostics and warnings
* current project surface
* syntax or semantic errors
* navigation context
* repeated edit hotspots

This can significantly improve in-place relevance in Lens and Assist Mode.

### 5.3.6 Browser console and network failures

In some workflows, browser-level errors are operationally significant.

When explicitly enabled and scoped, these can help Francis detect:

* frontend failures
* API errors
* broken requests
* repeated resource issues
* failure cascades in web workflows

This telemetry should remain optional and connector-like, not ambient by default.

### 5.3.7 App and window focus events

Focus telemetry can be useful for continuity and situational awareness, but it should be treated carefully.

Scoped focus events may help Francis understand:

* what environment is currently primary
* when the user is switching contexts repeatedly
* which mission surface is active
* whether certain high-friction patterns recur across tools

But focus telemetry must remain narrow and never become an excuse for broad behavioral surveillance.

### 5.3.8 Mission-linked domain connectors

Over time, Francis may use richer domain connectors, such as approved access to:

* tickets
* calendars
* repos
* logs
* dashboards
* sales systems
* inventory systems
* research artifacts
* approved message or planning surfaces

These are often better than broad machine capture because they are structured, intentional, and domain-specific.

---

## 5.4 Telemetry Principles

Telemetry must be governed by principles stronger than “collect what helps.”

These principles should shape every connector, probe, watcher, signal pipeline, and retention model.

### 5.4.1 Opt-in

Telemetry must be enabled deliberately.

The user should never discover after the fact that Francis was sensing a surface they did not realize was active.

Opt-in is not friction for its own sake.
It is the precondition for trust.

### 5.4.2 Scope-bound

Telemetry must attach to explicit scope.

If a repo is in scope, that does not mean all repos are in scope.
If an app is approved, that does not mean all apps are approved.
If a node is paired, that does not mean all node data is globally visible.

Scope must govern sensing as much as acting.

### 5.4.3 Visibly indicated

The user should be able to tell when telemetry is active.

This does not require constant alarm-style signaling. It requires legibility.

The user should be able to inspect:

* which telemetry sources are enabled
* which scopes they apply to
* what mode Francis is in
* whether sensing is currently active
* how that sensing is being used

Hidden sensing is incompatible with Francis.

### 5.4.4 Redacted by default

Telemetry pipelines must assume that raw surfaces may contain secrets, credentials, tokens, private identifiers, or unnecessary personal information.

Therefore, Francis should prefer:

* structured extraction over raw capture
* redaction before storage
* metadata over full content where possible
* bounded summaries over indiscriminate retention
* minimal necessary detail for operational usefulness

Redaction is not a patch.
It is part of the design doctrine.

### 5.4.5 Retained minimally and intentionally

A common telemetry failure mode is indefinite accumulation.

Francis must not become a vacuum of stored exhaust.

Retention must be:

* purposeful
* time-bounded where appropriate
* tied to mission or continuity value
* inspectable
* deletable where policy allows
* minimal by default

The system should keep what helps the user, not everything it can technically keep.

### 5.4.6 Treated as untrusted input

Telemetry is useful, but telemetry is not truth by itself.

Signals can be noisy, incomplete, manipulated, or misleading.

Telemetry must therefore remain:

* evidence
* context
* input
* trigger material

But not unquestioned authority.

This matters especially in adversarial or fragile environments.

---

## 5.5 What Francis Must Never Capture by Default

The strength of this section depends not only on what Francis may observe, but on what it must not casually consume.

These boundaries are identity-level.

### 5.5.1 Passwords and credential surfaces

Francis must never silently capture or retain:

* passwords
* password manager contents
* private keys
* recovery phrases
* secret prompts
* MFA secrets
* raw authentication tokens unless explicitly required inside a governed secure connector path

These are not “just another telemetry source.” They are sensitive authority material.

### 5.5.2 Clipboard contents by default

Clipboard capture is highly invasive and often reveals sensitive transient data.

It must never be on by default.

If clipboard-related functionality ever exists, it must be:

* explicit
* scoped
* clearly indicated
* tightly redacted
* obviously revocable

### 5.5.3 Unrelated windows and out-of-scope surfaces

If a user is working in one approved environment, Francis must not infer permission to watch adjacent unrelated activity.

Out-of-scope windows, folders, apps, and nodes remain out of scope even if they are visible on the same machine.

### 5.5.4 Global keystrokes

Global keystroke capture is one of the fastest ways to become indistinguishable from spyware in the user’s mind.

Francis must not rely on broad keystroke capture as a normal mechanism of intelligence.

### 5.5.5 Camera and microphone without explicit enablement

Audio and video surfaces are exceptionally sensitive and should remain outside default telemetry assumptions.

If ever enabled for a specific mode or use case, that enablement must be:

* explicit
* obvious
* revocable
* highly bounded
* visibly active

### 5.5.6 Private folders outside scope

No amount of potential utility justifies silent drift into personal or unrelated storage.

Francis must never create the feeling that “if it exists on the machine, Francis probably saw it.”

That feeling is fatal to trust.

---

## 5.6 Telemetry and Lens

Telemetry becomes meaningful to the user primarily through Lens.

Without Lens, telemetry risks becoming a hidden backend fact. With Lens, it becomes a governed, visible source of relevance.

### 5.6.1 Lens should show why Francis is relevant here

When Francis surfaces an action chip, suggestion, or incident, the user should be able to understand the basis for that relevance.

Not necessarily with verbose explanation every time, but with enough legibility that the user does not feel haunted by unexplained knowledge.

Lens should help communicate:

* this appeared because the active build failed
* this appeared because the current mission is blocked
* this appeared because the repo in scope changed
* this appeared because the validation step failed
* this appeared because Away Mode staged something for review

That makes telemetry feel lawful.

### 5.6.2 Lens should make sensing state visible

If Francis is using approved telemetry, the user should be able to inspect:

* what is being sensed
* where it is being sensed
* why it is being sensed
* what mode is active
* what scope is attached

This is how telemetry supports presence without becoming creepiness.

### 5.6.3 Lens should prevent magical ambiguity

A bad product sometimes tries to feel impressive by making its awareness mysterious.

Francis must do the opposite.

The user should not think:

> How on earth did it know that?

in a suspicious sense.

Instead, the user should think:

> Of course it knows that. I enabled that project scope, the build failed, and Francis is watching the right signals.

That is trusted intelligence.

---

## 5.7 Telemetry and Apprenticeship

Telemetry is also part of how Francis learns from the user in a governed way.

But that learning must be lawful, inspectable, and bounded.

### 5.7.1 Telemetry gives structure to demonstrations

When a user says “watch how I do this,” Francis needs some operational visibility into what “this” consists of.

That may include signals like:

* sequence of actions in scope
* file edits
* command sequences
* validation steps
* approval points
* repeated decision patterns
* handoff moments

Telemetry helps transform demonstration into structured pattern understanding.

### 5.7.2 Apprenticeship is not excuse-for-recording

The apprenticeship promise must never be used as justification for broad, indiscriminate capture.

Learning should be built from:

* bounded teaching sessions
* explicit scopes
* structured event sequences
* parameterization
* reviewable abstractions
* staged capability output

Not from “we recorded everything and figured it out later.”

### 5.7.3 Learned patterns must be reviewable

If telemetry contributes to learning, the resulting learned pattern should be inspectable in a human-useful way.

The user should be able to see:

* what pattern Francis thinks it observed
* what inputs it believes matter
* where that pattern applies
* what assumptions it is making
* what capability it wants to stage
* what remains uncertain

That keeps learning aligned with governance.

---

## 5.8 Telemetry and Away Mode

Away Mode is one of the areas where telemetry becomes most sensitive.

When the user is absent, sensing must remain especially disciplined.

### 5.8.1 Away telemetry must remain bounded

User absence must never be interpreted as permission for broader observation.

Away telemetry should remain limited to:

* approved signals
* approved scopes
* approved missions
* approved connectors
* approved continuation policies

Francis must not become more invasive simply because the user is not present to notice.

### 5.8.2 Away telemetry exists for momentum, not ambient watching

The purpose of telemetry in Away Mode is to preserve lawful continuity.

Examples include:

* noticing a build completion
* detecting a validation failure
* observing a mission dependency change
* catching an anomaly in an approved system
* preparing a return briefing
* surfacing items for approval on return

It is not to maintain indefinite broad awareness of everything the machine does.

### 5.8.3 Return briefings depend on lawful telemetry

A meaningful Away summary depends on telemetry, but only the right kind.

When the user comes back, Francis should be able to say things like:

* what changed
* what advanced
* what failed
* what was staged
* what now needs approval
* what remains blocked

This is valuable only if the sensing behind it remained lawful and trusted.

---

## 5.9 Telemetry and Adversarial Risk

Telemetry is not automatically truthful. It can be manipulated.

A mature Francis must therefore treat telemetry as useful but potentially adversarial input.

### 5.9.1 Telemetry can be misleading

Signals may be:

* incomplete
* noisy
* spoofed
* stale
* context-poor
* intentionally manipulated
* accidentally misleading

For example:

* a terminal output could include deceptive text
* a log line could imply success where downstream state still failed
* a file change could be irrelevant churn rather than mission progress
* a focus switch could mean distraction, not meaningful task change

Telemetry should support inference, not replace verification.

### 5.9.2 Telemetry must not grant authority

Even if telemetry strongly suggests a likely action, that signal does not create permission.

For example:

* seeing a repeated failure does not authorize mutation in Observe
* noticing a likely next step does not create Pilot rights
* detecting urgency does not bypass approvals
* learning a user pattern does not widen scope

This boundary is critical.

### 5.9.3 High confidence from telemetry still requires governance

A mature system may become very good at reading work patterns.

That capability increases the risk of overreach if not governed.

The better Francis gets at predicting what the user probably wants, the more important it becomes that the system still obey:

* mode
* scope
* approvals
* policies
* receipts
* verification requirements

Telemetry sharpens intelligence.
It must not loosen law.

---

## 5.10 Telemetry Failure Modes

This section must explicitly define the common ways telemetry can go wrong.

### 5.10.1 Telemetry drifts into surveillance

Symptoms:

* too many surfaces enabled
* weak scope boundaries
* unclear sensing state
* retention exceeds usefulness
* user starts feeling watched rather than supported

Correction:

* re-center opt-in, scope-bound, visible, minimal telemetry

### 5.10.2 Telemetry is too weak to matter

Symptoms:

* Francis still depends on constant restatement
* in-place suggestions feel generic
* continuity feels shallow
* mission awareness is disconnected from reality
* takeover lacks grounded context

Correction:

* re-center high-signal operational sources rather than broad raw capture

### 5.10.3 Telemetry becomes hidden magic

Symptoms:

* Francis appears aware in ways the user cannot explain
* suggestions feel sourced from mysterious observation
* scope and sensing are not inspectable
* Lens relevance feels uncanny rather than lawful

Correction:

* re-center sensing visibility and explainable relevance

### 5.10.4 Telemetry becomes exhaust hoarding

Symptoms:

* too much stored raw data
* unclear retention boundaries
* logs and signals pile up without mission value
* governance weakens under accumulation

Correction:

* re-center minimal, intentional retention and redaction-first design

### 5.10.5 Telemetry is mistaken for truth

Symptoms:

* Francis overclaims from weak signals
* logs are treated as final state
* recurring patterns are mistaken for guaranteed intent
* noisy evidence drives strong action claims

Correction:

* re-center trust calibration, receipts, and verification

---

## 5.11 Telemetry Doctrine Statement

If Francis Work Telemetry had to be defined in one doctrine sentence for builders, it would be this:

> Francis uses high-signal, opt-in, scope-bound, visibly indicated, redacted, minimally retained telemetry to understand real work context well enough to provide grounded, in-place, mission-aware assistance and continuity without becoming invasive, hidden, or surveillance-like.

That sentence should be strong enough to reject bad sensing architecture early.

---

## 5.12 Telemetry Acceptance Criteria

Section 5 is satisfied only if the roadmap, connectors, telemetry pipelines, Lens surfaces, and governance systems remain aligned to all of the following:

* Work Telemetry is treated as essential for contextual intelligence but never as the center of product identity
* Francis relies on high-signal telemetry rather than indiscriminate total capture
* telemetry is opt-in, scope-bound, visibly indicated, redacted by default, and minimally retained
* approved telemetry sources emphasize file changes, git state, terminal outcomes, validation outputs, IDE diagnostics, focused app context, and structured domain connectors
* telemetry is always treated as untrusted input rather than unquestioned truth
* telemetry never silently widens scope or grants authority
* Francis never captures passwords, credential stores, global keystrokes, unrelated windows, clipboard by default, or camera/mic without explicit enablement
* Lens makes telemetry legible enough that relevance feels lawful rather than mysterious
* telemetry supports apprenticeship without becoming an excuse for broad recording
* Away Mode telemetry remains bounded and continuity-oriented rather than surveillance-oriented
* telemetry retention remains intentional, reviewable, and limited by user value
* telemetry failure modes are explicitly recognized and corrected during buildout
* Francis never degrades into a monitoring suite, spyware-like observer, or ambient surveillance product under the excuse of becoming more context-aware

# 6.0 Autonomy Model: Reactor, Not Loop

Francis must eventually be able to continue work without the user manually driving every single step.

That is part of the promise.

But this is also the point where many intelligent systems fail in a foundational way.

They confuse autonomy with endlessness.
They confuse initiative with unbounded recursion.
They confuse persistence with “while true.”
They confuse intelligence with simply doing more things without enough law, stopping conditions, or situational discipline.

That path produces one of the weakest and most dangerous product categories in modern AI:

**the generic runaway loop agent.**

Francis must never become that.

Its autonomy model must be explicit from the start:

**Francis is an event-driven reactor, not a generic infinite loop.**

That statement is not only technical. It is philosophical, architectural, and product-defining.

It means Francis does not exist to wake up every few seconds and blindly ask itself what to do next.
It does not exist to recursively create work for itself without stable boundaries.
It does not exist to perform theater by appearing constantly active.
It does not exist to keep moving just because motion looks impressive.

Francis exists to respond lawfully to meaningful triggers, within mode, scope, approval state, budget, and verification requirements, and then return to a stable state when that bounded work is done.

This section defines the doctrine of autonomy in Francis:

* why the loop model is wrong
* what reactor autonomy means
* how events should enter the system
* how work should be classified and bounded
* how dispatches should stop
* how retries, deadletters, and escalation should work
* how autonomy should feel to the user
* what failure modes must be prevented before they become architectural habits

The central question of this section is:

> How can Francis continue work, maintain momentum, and feel intelligently alive without turning into an always-churning, vaguely authorized, self-justifying agent loop?

The answer is:

**bounded, event-driven, governable autonomy with explicit triggers, explicit budgets, explicit stop conditions, and explicit receipts.**

That is the Francis model.

---

## 6.1 Why the Generic Loop Model Is Wrong

The generic loop model usually sounds seductive in early design.

It sounds like:

* keep checking for things to do
* keep planning until the goal is done
* keep trying until something works
* keep exploring possible next actions
* keep acting unless explicitly stopped

This model feels powerful because it creates the appearance of continuous initiative.

But in practice, it often produces:

* wasted compute
* noisy behavior
* weak authority boundaries
* unclear stopping conditions
* accidental repetition
* hidden self-escalation
* bad user trust
* poor receipt structure
* difficult debugging
* difficult governance enforcement

The loop model is attractive because it seems simple.
It is actually one of the fastest ways to make a system slippery.

### 6.1.1 Loops blur authority

A generic loop tends to ask itself:

> What should I do next?

over and over again.

That sounds intelligent, but under weak governance it slowly mutates into:

* what else can I touch?
* what adjacent thing might help?
* what inferred user intention can I now pursue?
* what next task can I invent to remain active?
* what additional system behavior can I justify from previous context?

That is how mode boundaries erode and scope expands in practice.

A loop encourages self-propelled continuation without enough structural friction.

Francis must reject that.

### 6.1.2 Loops make it harder to reason about the system

A system is much easier to govern when work occurs in bounded runs with clear triggers, explicit inputs, explicit action counts, and explicit outcomes.

A loop-based system is harder to reason about because:

* there may be no clean start point
* there may be no clean end point
* causality becomes murky
* the reason for an action may be “the loop decided”
* receipts become fragmented
* user expectations become unstable
* interruption becomes harder
* retries and repetition become less legible

A powerful system should become more legible as it matures, not less.

### 6.1.3 Loops create bad emotional feel

Even if a looped system is technically functional, it often feels wrong.

It can feel:

* jittery
* overeager
* hard to trust
* vaguely out of control
* always churning in the background
* difficult to interrupt cleanly
* annoyingly active without enough purpose

That is the opposite of the Francis emotional target.

Francis should feel:

* composed
* deliberate
* bounded
* situationally aware
* active when meaningful
* quiet when there is nothing lawful to do

### 6.1.4 The loop model weakens the category

Francis is not trying to become “one more autonomous agent framework.”

It is trying to become a governed Operator Layer.

The loop model belongs to a weaker category because it centers motion over law.

Francis must center:

* events over constant polling
* bounded runs over open-ended drift
* receipts over vague continuation
* stop conditions over perpetual self-propulsion
* authority over appetite

That is what protects the category.

---

## 6.2 The Correct Autonomy Model: Event-Driven Reactor

Francis should be built as an **event-driven reactor**.

That means the basic pattern is:

**intake event → classify → plan bounded work → execute within law → verify → receipt → reflect → return to stable state**

This is the correct shape of autonomy for Francis.

### 6.2.1 What a reactor means

A reactor does not continuously invent reasons to act.

It waits in lawful readiness for meaningful stimuli, then responds in a structured way.

Those stimuli may include:

* a user request
* a mission tick
* a telemetry anomaly
* a scheduled maintenance window
* a build failure
* a validation completion
* an approval decision
* a federated handoff
* a Forge proposal trigger
* an apprenticeship teaching session event

The important point is that work begins because something meaningful happened, not because the system is addicted to perpetual activity.

### 6.2.2 The reactor is active, not passive

“Reactor” does not mean Francis is inert.

It means Francis is **situationally active**.

A mature reactor can be highly capable and highly responsive:

* it can chain bounded steps
* it can continue a mission
* it can stage multiple actions
* it can retry under policy
* it can route work to internal units
* it can prepare approvals
* it can keep momentum alive in Away Mode

But it does all of that because the current state lawfully warrants it, not because a background loop is constantly searching for new excuses to act.

### 6.2.3 Returning to stable state matters

One of the most important properties of the Francis reactor model is that runs end.

After bounded work is performed, Francis should return to a stable state such as:

* waiting for next event
* waiting for approval
* waiting for dependency resolution
* waiting for schedule window
* waiting for user handback
* waiting for external state change

This is deeply important.

A system that can stop cleanly is easier to trust than one that is always “sort of still going.”

### 6.2.4 Reactor autonomy feels more lawful

To the user, an event-driven reactor should feel like:

* Francis noticed something meaningful
* Francis acted within the current rules
* Francis advanced the work intelligently
* Francis stopped when the bounded work was done
* Francis produced receipts
* Francis now either awaits the next trigger or asks for what it needs

That is a very different feeling from:

* Francis has been doing unknown things in the background for an unclear amount of time

---

## 6.3 Core Reactor Flow

Francis runs should follow a stable autonomy lifecycle.

At the highest level, that lifecycle is:

1. **Intake**
2. **Classify**
3. **Plan**
4. **Execute**
5. **Verify**
6. **Receipt**
7. **Reflect**
8. **Return to stable state**

Each stage exists for a reason.

### 6.3.1 Intake

An event enters the system.

This event should be explicit enough to identify:

* what happened
* where it happened
* what scope it belongs to
* what mission or domain it touches
* what mode Francis is currently in
* what policy context applies
* whether urgency or risk signals are present

Without clean intake, the system becomes fuzzy from the first step.

### 6.3.2 Classify

Before acting, Francis should classify the event.

Classification may include dimensions such as:

* event type
* risk tier
* mission relevance
* required authority
* likely next action class
* whether approval is needed
* whether human escalation is needed
* whether the event should be ignored, summarized, queued, or acted on

This is how the system avoids reflexive action.

### 6.3.3 Plan

Planning should not be endless ideation.

It should be the creation of a bounded work plan appropriate to the event and current governance state.

A bounded plan should answer things like:

* what is the objective of this run?
* what actions are allowed?
* what is the max number of steps?
* what is the max runtime?
* what tools or units are in play?
* what verification is required?
* what would stop this run early?
* what would escalate this run to approval or deadletter?

Planning in Francis should produce disciplined intention, not speculative wandering.

### 6.3.4 Execute

Execution should happen only within the current law:

* current mode
* current scope
* current approvals
* current policy
* current budgets
* current node trust state
* current mission context

Execution may include multiple steps, but each run must remain bounded and traceable.

### 6.3.5 Verify

Verification is what prevents the reactor from becoming a completion fantasy engine.

The system should check what actually happened, not only what it attempted.

Verification may include:

* test results
* build results
* file diff confirmation
* process exit status
* state re-check
* mission status update
* connector response
* user-visible artifact generation

Without verification, the reactor becomes overconfident.

### 6.3.6 Receipt

Every meaningful run must produce a receipt.

The receipt should capture:

* run_id
* trigger event
* classification
* plan summary
* actions taken
* verification outcome
* artifacts produced
* pending uncertainty
* escalation or next state

This keeps autonomy auditable.

### 6.3.7 Reflect

Reflection is not unbounded self-philosophizing.

It is the disciplined post-run assessment of:

* what happened
* what succeeded
* what failed
* what remains blocked
* what pattern might matter later
* whether this created Forge-worthy friction
* whether mission state changed
* whether follow-up should be queued

Reflection helps Francis learn without turning every run into a recursive planning spiral.

### 6.3.8 Return to stable state

When the bounded run is complete, Francis must stop and settle into the appropriate next state:

* done
* awaiting approval
* awaiting dependency
* awaiting retry window
* awaiting user input
* awaiting next event

This is how the system remains composed.

---

## 6.4 Trigger Sources

Francis should not act because “it felt like doing something.”

It should act because a valid trigger entered the reactor.

Trigger discipline is part of governance.

### 6.4.1 User requests

The clearest trigger source is direct user initiation.

Examples include:

* “help me fix this”
* “take over”
* “watch how I do this”
* “continue this mission tonight”
* “stage a capability for this repeated task”
* “summarize what changed”

User requests are among the highest-authority triggers, though still bounded by policy and scope.

### 6.4.2 Observer anomalies

Observer systems may detect meaningful anomalies such as:

* build break
* repeated failure
* drift from baseline
* suspicious content
* stalled mission
* regression signal
* out-of-budget execution pattern
* repeated manual friction

These anomalies may trigger runs that classify, summarize, queue, or request approval depending on current mode.

### 6.4.3 Mission queue entries

Missions themselves are a trigger source.

Mission events may include:

* new mission creation
* mission step completion
* dependency resolution
* blocked-state transition
* overdue or scheduled tick
* waiting-for-approval state
* handoff completion
* deadline proximity

This is how Francis carries continuity without constant user prompting.

### 6.4.4 Forge proposals

Repeated friction or repeated pattern recognition may trigger Forge proposal generation.

This does not mean auto-promotion.
It means the reactor can open a bounded run to:

* assess the pattern
* summarize the friction
* draft a capability proposal
* attach risk and test expectations
* queue it for review

### 6.4.5 Telemetry events

Telemetry can trigger reactor runs when meaningful thresholds are crossed.

Examples include:

* test failure
* repeated command error
* file churn hotspot
* suspicious log pattern
* browser network failure
* state change in approved scope
* active environment change relevant to a mission

Telemetry should inform triggers, not become permissionless action fuel.

### 6.4.6 Schedule windows

Certain actions are best initiated during declared schedule windows, especially for Away Mode and housekeeping.

Examples include:

* nightly validation
* morning briefing assembly
* approved monitoring intervals
* cleanup windows
* low-risk maintenance scans
* scheduled mission tick evaluation

Schedule windows help structure continuation without needing perpetual loops.

### 6.4.7 Federated handoffs

As Francis scales, one node or unit may hand off work to another.

Such handoffs can be valid triggers if they preserve:

* trace continuity
* current authority state
* current scope
* policy state
* budgets
* receipt obligations

No handoff should create authority from nowhere.

---

## 6.5 Bounded Execution

The reactor model only works if every dispatch is bounded.

This is one of the deepest laws in Francis autonomy.

A run without explicit bounds is just a loop pretending to be a task.

### 6.5.1 What must bound a run

Every dispatch should be constrained by some combination of:

* maximum action count
* maximum runtime
* maximum retry count
* maximum resource budget
* current mode
* current scope
* current approvals
* risk tier
* stop-on-condition rules
* escalation thresholds

These bounds should exist before the run begins.

### 6.5.2 Why boundedness matters

Boundedness prevents:

* runaway execution
* accidental sprawl
* endless self-replanning
* unclear receipts
* indefinite lock on authority
* poor interruptibility
* background churn
* user distrust

A bounded run feels governable because it is governable.

### 6.5.3 Bounds should scale with authority

Different contexts justify different budgets.

For example:

* Observe may allow broad awareness but zero mutation
* Assist may allow richer planning but no execution
* Pilot may allow more action depth within visible delegation
* Away may allow safe continuation but tighter destructive-action restrictions

Boundedness is not one-size-fits-all.
It must be mode-aware and risk-aware.

### 6.5.4 Stop conditions must be explicit

Every run should know what stops it.

Examples include:

* objective reached
* verification failed
* approval required
* budget exhausted
* unexpected risk tier increase
* dependency unresolved
* user interruption
* node trust issue
* suspicious input encountered
* policy denial

A run that does not know how to stop is an architectural bug.

---

## 6.6 Chaining Without Becoming a Loop

Francis must be able to perform multi-step work.

But multi-step work must not silently become open-ended recursive continuation.

This is where many systems blur the line.

### 6.6.1 Lawful chaining

Lawful chaining means one bounded run may produce:

* a verified completion
* a queued follow-up event
* a request for approval
* a mission state update
* a scheduled retry
* a handoff to another bounded run
* a deadletter item
* a Forge proposal candidate

This allows momentum to continue across time without requiring a single infinite process.

### 6.6.2 Why chaining is different from looping

A loop says:

> keep going until who knows.

A lawful chain says:

> this bounded run ended; based on the outcome, here is the next explicit event or queued step.

That difference is everything.

It preserves:

* causality
* auditability
* user understanding
* interruption points
* authority checks
* mode and approval re-evaluation
* cleaner receipts

### 6.6.3 Re-evaluate governance at each link

Each new link in a chain should re-check:

* current mode
* current scope
* current approvals
* current policies
* current mission state
* current risk posture
* current node trust state

This prevents an initial valid trigger from becoming a blank check for prolonged continuation.

---

## 6.7 Failure Model: Retries, Backoff, Deadletter, Escalation

A real autonomy model must define how failure behaves.

Without that, the system either:

* retries foolishly
* stops too early
* hides repeated failure
* loops uselessly
* gives the user no meaningful path to recover

Francis must have a disciplined failure model.

### 6.7.1 Retries with backoff

Some failures are transient.

Examples include:

* temporary process lock
* network flake
* dependent service not yet ready
* file contention
* short-lived node issue

In such cases, retries may be appropriate, but they must be:

* bounded
* counted
* delayed with backoff
* receipted
* visible in mission or run state where relevant

Retries must not become “keep smashing until something happens.”

### 6.7.2 Maximum attempts

Every retryable pattern should have a maximum attempt rule.

Once that threshold is crossed, Francis must stop pretending the same action is likely to succeed without new information.

That is when the system should move to:

* deadletter
* approval request
* human escalation
* dependency wait state
* anomaly classification
* mission block state

Maximum attempts protect both compute and dignity.

### 6.7.3 Deadletter queue

Some work cannot safely or meaningfully continue.

Deadletter exists so failed bounded work has somewhere honest to go.

A deadletter item should preserve:

* what was attempted
* why it failed
* what receipts exist
* what might unblock it
* whether human review is needed
* whether retry is ever appropriate
* what mission it affected

Deadletter is not a garbage bin.
It is structured truth about failed continuation.

### 6.7.4 Escalation

Some failures should escalate rather than retry.

Examples include:

* suspicious input
* policy contradiction
* repeated destructive-action denial
* unexplained verification mismatch
* scope inconsistency
* trust boundary violation
* persistent mission blocker
* federated node anomaly

Escalation should feel calm and informative, not alarmist.

---

## 6.8 Autonomy Across the Modes

The reactor must behave differently depending on mode.

Same architecture, different legal behavior.

### 6.8.1 Observe autonomy

Observe may still involve reactor behavior, but only in non-mutating ways.

Observe runs may:

* summarize
* classify
* correlate
* raise incidents
* notice drift
* prepare suggestions
* queue approvals
* update continuity state

Observe must never silently mutate the environment.

### 6.8.2 Assist autonomy

Assist may perform richer planning and preparation.

Assist runs may:

* draft plans
* prepare patches
* assemble context
* stage non-applied suggestions
* propose next steps
* create approval items

Assist must still stop short of unilateral execution unless user initiation converts the situation appropriately.

### 6.8.3 Pilot autonomy

Pilot permits the strongest bounded reactor behavior.

Pilot runs may:

* execute multiple bounded steps
* navigate approved surfaces
* perform tool use
* validate outcomes
* chain follow-up bounded runs where still in active Pilot authority
* coordinate internal units

But even in Pilot, the run must remain bounded, visible, interruptible, and receipted.

### 6.8.4 Away autonomy

Away allows bounded continuation without active user presence.

Away runs may:

* monitor
* validate
* summarize
* stage
* queue
* advance safe mission steps
* prepare approvals

Away must remain especially disciplined because user absence must never be interpreted as broad permission.

---

## 6.9 The User Experience of Francis Autonomy

The autonomy model must not only be architecturally correct. It must feel correct.

The user should experience Francis autonomy as:

* meaningful
* bounded
* visible
* unsurprising
* useful
* easy to interrupt
* easy to inspect
* clearly tied to something real

### 6.9.1 What good autonomy feels like

A good Francis run should feel like:

* something triggered this
* Francis understood why it mattered
* Francis acted within the current law
* Francis either made progress or surfaced the blocker honestly
* I can inspect what happened
* I know what happens next
* it stopped when it should have stopped

That creates trust.

### 6.9.2 What bad autonomy feels like

Bad autonomy feels like:

* Francis is always doing something unclear
* actions appear with weak causality
* the system seems hungry for more work
* completion claims are too loose
* I do not know why this continued
* I am not sure whether it stopped
* I do not know how much power it currently has

Francis must never feel like that.

### 6.9.3 Autonomy should create momentum, not ambient anxiety

One of the emotional goals of Francis is reducing cognitive drag.

The autonomy model therefore should make the user feel:

* momentum exists
* I am not carrying everything alone
* continuation is lawful
* the system is composed
* I can trust what happens when I look away briefly
* the machine is helping without becoming haunted

That is the right emotional target.

---

## 6.10 Reactor Failure Modes

This section must explicitly name the autonomy drifts that would corrupt Francis.

### 6.10.1 Reactor drifts into generic loop

Symptoms:

* perpetual background thinking
* unclear stop conditions
* repeated self-initiated actions
* poor receipt boundaries
* vague explanation for why work happened

Correction:

* re-center event-triggered bounded runs with stable end states

### 6.10.2 Reactor drifts into over-eagerness

Symptoms:

* too many triggers cause action
* low threshold for “helpful” continuation
* unnecessary interventions
* autonomy begins to feel pushy

Correction:

* re-center trigger quality, relevance thresholds, and mode discipline

### 6.10.3 Reactor drifts into weak chaining discipline

Symptoms:

* follow-up after follow-up without re-checking authority
* one valid run implicitly justifies many more
* causal trail gets blurry

Correction:

* re-center explicit chaining with re-evaluated governance at each step

### 6.10.4 Reactor drifts into silent failure churn

Symptoms:

* repeated retries with little progress
* hidden error recycling
* background effort without user visibility
* no clear deadletter or escalation behavior

Correction:

* re-center retry bounds, backoff, deadletter, and receipts

### 6.10.5 Reactor drifts into performative activity

Symptoms:

* activity for the sake of looking alive
* meaningless background motion
* verbose “thinking” with weak operational value
* weak distinction between real progress and visible busyness

Correction:

* re-center mission value, bounded execution, and stable quiet states

---

## 6.11 Autonomy Doctrine Statement

If Francis Autonomy had to be defined in one doctrine sentence for builders, it would be this:

> Francis operates as a governed event-driven reactor that responds to meaningful triggers with bounded, mode-aware, scope-aware, approval-aware runs that execute, verify, receipt, and return to stable state without collapsing into perpetual loops, hidden continuation, or self-justifying activity.

That sentence should be strong enough to reject major architectural mistakes early.

---

## 6.12 Autonomy Acceptance Criteria

Section 6 is satisfied only if the roadmap, runtime architecture, mission engine, mode system, and execution substrate remain aligned to all of the following:

* Francis is explicitly built as an event-driven reactor rather than a generic infinite loop agent
* every meaningful run begins from a valid trigger source rather than perpetual self-propulsion
* the core reactor flow includes intake, classification, bounded planning, execution, verification, receipt, reflection, and return to stable state
* all dispatches are bounded by action count, time, resources, authority, and explicit stop conditions
* chaining is lawful and event-based rather than recursive open-ended continuation
* retries are bounded and use backoff rather than churn
* deadletter and escalation paths exist for work that should not silently continue
* autonomy behaves differently and lawfully across Observe, Assist, Pilot, and Away
* no autonomy path silently blurs mode boundaries or turns prior authorization into blanket ongoing permission
* every autonomous run remains interruptible, inspectable, and receipted
* the user experience of autonomy feels composed, purposeful, and governable rather than haunted, hungry, or vague
* reactor failure modes are explicitly recognized and corrected during buildout
* Francis never degrades into a runaway loop bot under the excuse of becoming more autonomous

# 7.0 Missions: Intent Turned Into Motion

Francis cannot become a true Operator Layer if intent evaporates the moment a prompt ends.

That is one of the deepest failures in most assistant systems: they may answer, suggest, generate, even act for a moment, but they do not truly **carry** work. They do not hold structure across time. They do not preserve operational momentum. They do not know the difference between a passing request and an objective with consequences.

Francis must.

That is why **Missions** are not a productivity add-on, not a reminders feature, and not a prettier to-do list.

Missions are the way Francis turns human intent into durable operational motion.

They are the continuity-bearing container that lets Francis:

* understand what the user is trying to achieve
* preserve movement across sessions
* stage and sequence work
* notice blockers
* route approvals
* carry receipts
* survive interruption
* support delegation
* continue lawful progress while the user is away

Without Missions, Francis collapses toward interaction-driven intelligence.

With Missions, Francis becomes an operational system.

This section defines the doctrine of Missions:

* what a mission is
* why missions matter to identity
* how missions differ from reminders, tasks, and tickets
* what kinds of missions Francis must support
* what properties every mission must carry
* how missions move through time
* how missions interact with Lens, telemetry, autonomy, Away Mode, receipts, and governance
* what failure modes must be blocked before they distort the architecture

The central question of this section is:

> How does Francis hold intent strongly enough that work continues as a real structured arc, without becoming vague project software or a fake “goal-oriented agent” with weak discipline?

The answer is:

**Missions are structured objectives with state, history, risk, dependencies, receipts, ownership, and lawful continuation.**

They are where intent becomes motion.

---

## 7.1 Why Missions Matter

A system that only responds to the current request is always living in the present tense.

That may still be useful, but it is not enough for Francis.

Francis is meant to help with:

* work that spans hours
* projects that span days
* systems that evolve across weeks
* routines that repeat across months
* objectives that survive interruption
* delegated work that requires continuity
* efforts that contain blockers, approvals, retries, and staged steps

That kind of work cannot be held only in prompts.

It needs structure.

### 7.1.1 Missions solve the continuity problem

Without Missions, the user must constantly reconstruct:

* what the objective was
* what already happened
* what remains
* what got blocked
* what was approved
* what Francis tried
* what changed while they were away
* what the next best move is

That reconstruction burden is exactly what Francis is supposed to reduce.

Missions preserve that structure so continuity becomes real rather than theatrical.

### 7.1.2 Missions solve the delegation problem

Delegation only works when the thing being delegated has shape.

A vague request like “handle this” is not enough for a governed system to carry safely across time.

A Mission gives that delegation shape by encoding:

* objective
* boundaries
* current state
* next step
* risk tier
* dependencies
* approvals
* receipts
* escalation path

This is what makes Francis capable of carrying work without becoming vague or overreaching.

### 7.1.3 Missions solve the momentum problem

Many systems can help in moments.

Few can sustain motion.

Missions are how Francis keeps progress alive when:

* the user changes tasks
* the machine sleeps
* a dependency blocks execution
* approvals are pending
* a run fails and needs escalation
* Away Mode advances only part of the work
* new evidence changes the best next move

Momentum is not just “ongoing activity.”
It is structured advancement toward an objective.

That is what Missions make possible.

---

## 7.2 What a Mission Is

A Mission is a structured objective that Francis can carry over time under law.

It is more than a task.
More than a note.
More than a reminder.
More than a chat thread summary.
More than a ticket.
More than a plan.

A Mission is an operational container for intent.

It binds together:

* the objective
* the current state
* the sequence of meaningful steps
* the governing risk and authority context
* the dependencies that shape movement
* the receipts that prove what happened
* the history that preserves continuity
* the ownership and escalation rules that keep it lawful

### 7.2.1 A Mission has consequence

The key difference between a Mission and a casual request is consequence.

A Mission matters enough that Francis should:

* remember it
* track it
* update it
* re-enter it
* continue it lawfully
* report on it
* block on it
* escalate it
* close it only with discipline

A trivial interaction does not need this structure.

A Mission does.

### 7.2.2 A Mission is not a generic “goal object”

There is a temptation in intelligent systems to create vague goal containers with abstract fields and then claim “goal-oriented autonomy.”

That is not enough.

A Francis Mission must be grounded in real work structure.

It must be concrete enough to support:

* execution
* review
* continuity
* receipts
* governance
* human inspection
* operational handoff

It is not philosophy.
It is working memory with law.

### 7.2.3 A Mission is a continuity-bearing object

A Mission should survive:

* time
* interruption
* changing sessions
* delegated runs
* failed attempts
* temporary uncertainty
* approval waits
* node handoffs
* return briefings

That persistence is one of its defining properties.

A Mission is how work remains alive even when attention shifts.

---

## 7.3 What Missions Are Not

Identity requires boundaries here too.

Francis Missions are not:

* simple reminders
* sticky notes with verbs
* generic project-management tasks
* a thin wrapper around chat history
* a vague “goal” used to justify open-ended agent behavior
* a ticket mirror with no operational depth
* a loop fuel source for constant background action
* a bloated hierarchy the user must manually maintain

Missions should be structured enough to matter, but not so bureaucratic that they become another burden.

### 7.3.1 Missions are not reminders

A reminder says:

> remember to do this later

A Mission says:

> this objective exists, has state, has momentum, has blockers, and may require lawful continuation.

Reminders may appear inside Missions.
They are not the same thing.

### 7.3.2 Missions are not just tasks

A task is usually one step.

A Mission may contain many steps, dependencies, receipts, and decision points.

The user may see a Mission as one thing they care about, while Francis understands it as a structured operational arc.
