### 7.3.3 Missions are not excuses for autonomy drift

Francis must never use “active mission” as a blank check to keep acting.

Mission existence does not override:

* mode
* scope
* approvals
* policy
* budgets
* stop conditions

Missions justify continuity.
They do not create sovereignty.

### 7.3.4 Missions are not project-management theater

Francis should not become generic PM software with AI sprinkled on top.

The purpose of Missions is not to produce more lists and status tags.
The purpose is to carry intent in a way that supports actual work.

If the system has many mission objects but little meaningful motion, then the design has failed.

---

## 7.4 Mission Types

Francis should support multiple classes of Missions because not all objectives have the same structure, urgency, or continuation pattern.

Mission type matters because it influences:

* how progress is measured
* how autonomy behaves
* what counts as blocked
* what kinds of approvals are likely
* what receipts matter most
* what sort of handoff or briefing is appropriate

### 7.4.1 Project Missions

Project Missions represent larger arcs of work with multiple steps and milestones.

Examples:

* ship a feature
* stabilize a repo
* launch a reporting workflow
* prepare a client deliverable
* stand up an internal automation
* complete a business ops implementation

These often involve dependencies, staged progress, validation, and sustained continuity.

### 7.4.2 Maintenance Missions

Maintenance Missions preserve or improve ongoing system health and continuity.

Examples:

* monitor a production service
* keep an environment clean and validated
* maintain a nightly reporting pipeline
* check inventory drift
* keep a home node stable
* manage recurring system upkeep

These are often cyclical, lower-drama, and continuity-heavy.

### 7.4.3 Learning Missions

Learning Missions represent skill acquisition, pattern extraction, or understanding-building.

Examples:

* learn how the user handles a recurring task
* observe a workflow for apprenticeship
* investigate repeated friction to prepare a Forge proposal
* study a repo structure before assisting further
* understand a new domain or process under scope

These are critical for Francis becoming more aligned over time.

### 7.4.4 Investigation Missions

Investigation Missions exist when something is unclear, broken, suspicious, or risky.

Examples:

* find root cause of a failure
* assess whether a regression is real
* determine why a deployment stalled
* inspect suspicious content or policy conflict
* compare possible solution paths before action

These often produce findings, evidence, and next-step recommendations rather than immediate resolution.

### 7.4.5 Execution Missions

Execution Missions are direct action-bearing objectives.

Examples:

* apply a safe fix
* stage a branch-based patch
* process a batch
* prepare a release artifact
* run a migration inside declared boundaries
* carry a defined workflow from start to verification

These often interact heavily with Pilot or Away depending on policy and risk.

### 7.4.6 Approval-Waiting Missions

Some Missions are structurally paused on human decision.

Examples:

* patch ready, waiting for promote decision
* diff prepared, awaiting merge approval
* capability staged, awaiting promotion
* risky action requires explicit signoff
* mission branch validated, awaiting handback choice

This is important because “waiting” is not the same as “dead.”

A mature system must preserve these states honestly.

### 7.4.7 Composite Missions

Some real objectives combine multiple types over time.

A project Mission may contain:

* investigation at the start
* execution in the middle
* approval waiting at the end
* maintenance after release

Francis must be able to preserve one coherent mission arc even when the internal work pattern evolves.

---

## 7.5 Mission Properties

Every Mission must carry a stable set of properties that make it governable, inspectable, and continuity-bearing.

These fields are not arbitrary metadata. They are the structure that lets Francis operate lawfully over time.

### 7.5.1 Objective

Every Mission needs a clear objective.

This is the answer to:

> What is this Mission trying to achieve?

The objective must be specific enough that it can guide planning and closure, not so vague that any activity can be justified under it.

### 7.5.2 Status

Every Mission needs explicit state.

Status might conceptually include states such as:

* proposed
* active
* blocked
* waiting approval
* in progress
* staged
* verifying
* completed
* deadlettered
* canceled
* archived

State is one of the main ways the system remains honest.

### 7.5.3 Step list

A Mission should maintain a structured understanding of meaningful steps, not necessarily every micro-action.

This helps Francis preserve:

* sequence
* partial completion
* branching options
* completed work
* pending work
* blocked work
* next candidate moves

The step list should support action, not bureaucracy.

### 7.5.4 Next step

The next step should be explicit enough that Francis or the user can re-enter motion quickly.

This is one of the highest-value fields for continuity because it answers:

> What is the best lawful move from here?

### 7.5.5 Risk tier

Every Mission should carry risk awareness.

Risk may influence:

* approval requirements
* autonomy limits
* execution budgets
* verification strictness
* handoff formality
* escalation behavior

A Mission handling destructive infra work is not the same as a Mission drafting a low-risk content artifact.

### 7.5.6 Dependencies

A Mission must know what it depends on.

Dependencies may include:

* human approvals
* external state changes
* another mission step
* build success
* connector availability
* a specific node
* a trusted environment
* a policy precondition

Without dependency awareness, Missions either thrash or misrepresent progress.

### 7.5.7 Receipts

A Mission must be able to point to evidence of motion.

This includes:

* runs
* artifacts
* validation outputs
* diffs
* summaries
* incident links
* approval records
* handoff records

Receipts are part of what makes a Mission real.

### 7.5.8 History

Mission history preserves the operational story.

This includes:

* what was attempted
* what changed
* what failed
* what succeeded
* what got approved
* what got denied
* what got escalated
* what got deferred

History is crucial for both trust and continuity.

### 7.5.9 Ownership

Every Mission needs clear ownership structure.

At minimum this includes:

* the user as principal
* Francis as carrier/operator
* other relevant internal units if delegated
* whether a specific node is currently active on it
* who or what must approve next if applicable

Ownership prevents ambiguity.

### 7.5.10 Escalation path

Every Mission should know what happens if normal progress fails.

This may include:

* approval request
* deadletter
* incident creation
* handoff to another internal unit
* user attention on return
* scheduled re-evaluation
* cancellation or archival path

A Mission without escalation becomes brittle.

---

## 7.6 Mission Lifecycle

Missions must move through a real lifecycle, not exist as static objects that accumulate dust.

A Mission should feel alive while it matters and settled when it is done.

### 7.6.1 Mission creation

A Mission may begin from:

* direct user request
* explicit conversion of a task into a mission
* structured continuation of an active effort
* repeated friction that deserves durable tracking
* a triggered investigation
* a delegated Pilot or Away objective
* a system-detected need for continuity
* a Forge or apprenticeship arc

Mission creation should feel intentional, not automatic in a noisy way.

### 7.6.2 Mission activation

Not every Mission is immediately actionable.

Activation is the point where the Mission enters live operational flow.

This may include:

* scope confirmation
* mode alignment
* dependency check
* step initialization
* risk assignment
* first next-step selection

### 7.6.3 Mission progression

A progressing Mission should show meaningful movement.

That movement may be:

* completed step
* verified action
* new dependency resolution
* new evidence
* approval outcome
* successful handoff
* staged artifact
* updated next step

Progress is not mere busyness.
It is change in meaningful mission state.

### 7.6.4 Mission blocking

A Mission must be able to become honestly blocked.

Blocked is not failure.
Blocked is truth.

Examples include:

* missing approval
* failing dependency
* connector unavailable
* policy restriction
* unresolved anomaly
* missing user input
* scope conflict
* verification mismatch

A mature system does not hide blocked state. It uses it.

### 7.6.5 Mission completion

A Mission is complete only when its objective is satisfied to the required standard.

Completion should require:

* objective met
* required verification performed
* receipts linked
* unresolved blockers closed or acknowledged
* remaining uncertainty made explicit if any
* handback or closure summary produced

Completion claims must remain disciplined.

### 7.6.6 Mission archival

Not every completed or canceled Mission should remain active forever.

Archival preserves history while removing live operational pressure.

Archived Missions are still valuable because they may support:

* continuity memory
* apprenticeship patterns
* Forge opportunities
* future reference
* user-specific strategy understanding

---

## 7.7 Missions and the Reactor Model

Missions and the reactor model are deeply linked.

The reactor gives Francis bounded runs.
Missions give those runs continuity-bearing purpose.

### 7.7.1 Missions are not loops

A Mission should never be implemented as “keep doing things toward this goal until stopped.”

That is just a goal-shaped loop.

Instead, a Mission should generate or receive bounded events such as:

* next step ready
* dependency resolved
* approval received
* verification failed
* run completed
* blocker encountered
* review needed
* schedule window opened

This preserves lawful continuation without runaway behavior.

### 7.7.2 Missions coordinate bounded runs

A Mission acts as the continuity structure that multiple bounded runs can attach to.

This allows Francis to:

* work in bursts
* stop cleanly
* re-enter later
* change direction with new evidence
* survive approval waits
* carry progress through interruptions

That is the correct shape of motion.

### 7.7.3 Missions preserve causality

Because runs link back to Missions, the user can understand:

* why this run happened
* what objective it belonged to
* what changed in the Mission afterward
* whether the run advanced the work
* whether the Mission is now blocked, completed, or waiting

This makes the system easier to trust.

---

## 7.8 Missions and Lens

Missions are one of the most important things Lens must make visible.

If Francis carries Missions but the user cannot feel them, then continuity remains abstract.

### 7.8.1 Mission feed as continuity surface

Lens should provide a clear Mission surface showing:

* what is active
* what is blocked
* what is awaiting approval
* what progressed recently
* what needs attention next
* what Francis recommends now

This is how the user stops feeling like they must hold every objective in their head.

### 7.8.2 Missions should appear in place

Mission relevance should not be trapped only in a feed.

If the user is inside a work surface related to a Mission, Lens should help make that local relevance visible.

For example:

* this file belongs to Mission X
* this error blocks Mission Y
* this approval would advance Mission Z
* this staged patch is part of Mission A

This is where the machine starts feeling coherent.

### 7.8.3 Return briefings should be mission-shaped

When the user returns after time away, Lens should not merely show raw events.

It should show mission-level meaning:

* which Missions advanced
* which stalled
* which are ready for review
* which now need approval
* which changed priority
* which need human judgment

That is more useful than a pile of logs.

---

## 7.9 Missions and Away Mode

Away Mode without Missions becomes vague background activity.

Missions are what make Away work lawful and intelligible.

### 7.9.1 Away should advance missions, not just generate activity

A mature Away Mode should be able to say:

* this Mission advanced
* this Mission is now blocked
* this Mission has staged work for your review
* this Mission failed verification and needs attention
* this Mission is waiting on approval
* this Mission is unchanged because its dependency did not resolve

That is meaningful continuation.

### 7.9.2 Missions limit Away drift

Missions help prevent Francis from becoming generically busy while the user is gone.

Away work should generally attach to:

* an active Mission
* a lawful maintenance pattern
* an approved monitoring objective
* a continuity-preserving check

This prevents background activity from becoming vague system appetite.

### 7.9.3 Away handback should be mission-centered

When the user returns, Francis should present the story in Mission terms, not only runtime terms.

The user should quickly understand:

* what moved
* what didn’t
* why
* what now needs them
* what can continue later
* what deserves priority next

That is the real value of Away continuity.

---

## 7.10 Missions and Apprenticeship / Forge

Missions are one of the best places for Francis to recognize patterns worth learning or capability worth building.

### 7.10.1 Missions reveal repeated friction

When similar blockers or repeated manual steps show up across Missions, Francis should notice.

This may indicate:

* a candidate apprenticeship pattern
* a Forge proposal
* a missing internal tool
* a workflow worth parameterizing
* a briefing pattern worth improving

Missions provide the structured context for seeing these repetitions clearly.

### 7.10.2 Missions preserve examples of how the user works

Because Missions carry history, steps, receipts, and outcomes, they become strong evidence for:

* preferred working style
* approval thresholds
* solution taste
* quality expectations
* recurring problem-solving sequences
* real-world completion standards

That makes them valuable to the Digital Twin arc.

### 7.10.3 Forge outputs may attach back to missions

A staged capability should often be understandable in terms of the Mission friction that produced it.

This is powerful because it lets Francis explain:

* what pain point this removes
* where it came from
* how often it recurred
* what it will save going forward
* why promotion may be worthwhile

That is compounding leverage made visible.

---

## 7.11 Mission Governance

Missions are not outside the law.

They must remain governed just like runs, scopes, and control modes.

### 7.11.1 A Mission does not create authority

The existence of a Mission never overrides:

* current mode
* current scope
* current approvals
* current policy
* current budgets
* current risk constraints

This must remain explicit.

A Mission may justify continued attention.
It does not justify unlawful action.

### 7.11.2 Mission continuation must stay scoped

A Mission may concern a large objective, but that does not mean Francis gains broad access across everything adjacent to it.

Scope must still be explicit and inspectable.

For example:

* a repo Mission does not grant access to every repo
* a sales workflow Mission does not grant access to every customer system
* a home ops Mission does not grant ambient visibility into unrelated private surfaces

Mission relevance is not the same thing as scope.

### 7.11.3 Mission closure claims require discipline

Francis must not say a Mission is done just because:

* it attempted the main step
* a likely fix was applied
* one output was produced
* a summary was written
* the user went away
* no new event arrived

Mission completion requires evidence that the objective reached its actual standard of done.

### 7.11.4 Mission ownership must stay human-centered

The Mission belongs to the user.

Francis carries it.
Francis helps structure it.
Francis may progress it.
Francis may stage and summarize it.

But the Mission remains part of the user’s world, not Francis’s independent agenda.

---

## 7.12 The User Experience of Missions

The Mission system must feel like relief, not bureaucracy.

That emotional requirement is crucial.

### 7.12.1 What good Missions feel like

A strong Mission experience should make the user feel:

* I do not have to remember everything myself
* my work has shape
* I can leave and come back without losing the thread
* Francis understands what is in motion
* progress is visible
* blockers are honest
* handoffs are clear
* I can trust what “next” means

That is a major unlock.

### 7.12.2 What bad Missions feel like

A bad Mission system feels like:

* another task manager I have to maintain
* status fields with weak operational value
* lots of objects, little movement
* continuity theater
* noisy organizational overhead
* AI inventing missions I did not really mean
* unclear relationship between Missions and actual work

Francis must avoid that completely.

### 7.12.3 Mission UX should reduce reconstruction cost

One of the biggest practical benefits of Missions is lower reconstruction cost.

A user should not have to spend ten minutes figuring out:

* where they left off
* why a task stalled
* what Francis already did
* what approval is waiting
* whether a fix was verified
* what now matters most

Mission UX should collapse that burden into something calm and legible.

---

## 7.13 Mission Failure Modes

This section must explicitly name the ways the Mission system can go wrong.

### 7.13.1 Missions drift into to-do list software

Symptoms:

* lots of task-like entries
* weak continuity
* little receipt linkage
* little operational meaning
* no real relationship to execution or governance

Correction:

* re-center Missions as structured operational objectives, not checklist items

### 7.13.2 Missions drift into vague goal theater

Symptoms:

* abstract goals with weak next steps
* AI language around “objectives” but little real motion
* unclear completion standards
* open-ended continuation without discipline

Correction:

* re-center objective clarity, status truth, next-step specificity, and bounded runs

### 7.13.3 Missions drift into autonomy justification

Symptoms:

* system uses an active Mission as excuse to keep acting
* approvals weaken because “the mission is still active”
* scope subtly widens in the name of progress
* user feels missions are giving Francis too much momentum

Correction:

* re-center governance, scope, and the rule that Missions do not create authority

### 7.13.4 Missions drift into bureaucracy

Symptoms:

* too many fields
* too much manual maintenance
* heavy status rituals
* user spends more time managing Missions than benefiting from them

Correction:

* re-center operational usefulness, calm defaults, and automatic structure where lawful

### 7.13.5 Missions drift into false progress

Symptoms:

* lots of status movement
* weak verification
* completion claims without receipts
* summaries that sound productive but do not map to real change

Correction:

* re-center receipts, verification, and meaningful state transitions

---

## 7.14 Mission Doctrine Statement

If Francis Missions had to be defined in one doctrine sentence for builders, it would be this:

> Francis Missions are structured, continuity-bearing objectives that turn human intent into lawful operational motion by preserving state, steps, risk, dependencies, receipts, history, ownership, and escalation across bounded runs, approvals, interruptions, and away time without collapsing into generic tasks, vague goals, or autonomy excuses.

That sentence should be strong enough to reject weak mission architecture early.

---

## 7.15 Mission Acceptance Criteria

Section 7 is satisfied only if the roadmap, mission model, Lens surfaces, reactor architecture, and continuity systems remain aligned to all of the following:

* Missions are treated as structured objectives, not reminders, generic tasks, or vague goal wrappers
* Missions preserve continuity across sessions, interruptions, approvals, and Away Mode
* Missions include clear objective, status, step structure, next step, risk tier, dependencies, receipts, history, ownership, and escalation path
* Mission state changes reflect real operational truth rather than performative status movement
* Missions coordinate bounded runs rather than justifying open-ended loops
* Mission existence never creates authority beyond current mode, scope, approvals, or policy
* Lens makes Missions visible enough that continuity and momentum feel real to the user
* Away Mode advances or reports on Missions in intelligible, governed ways
* Missions support apprenticeship and Forge by revealing repeated patterns and friction
* Mission UX reduces reconstruction burden rather than adding project-management overhead
* Mission completion requires appropriate verification and receipt linkage
* Mission failure modes are explicitly recognized and corrected during buildout
* Francis never degrades into task-manager software, vague goal theater, or mission-shaped autonomy drift

# 8.0 Forge: Francis Builds New Capabilities

Francis does not reach its real potential merely by becoming better at using the capabilities it already has.

That would still leave it trapped in a fixed ceiling.

A true Operator Layer must not only act.
It must also **improve its own operational surface in a governed way**.

That is what **Forge** is for.

Forge is how Francis turns repeated friction into compounding leverage.
It is how recurring pain stops being merely endured and starts becoming removable.
It is how the system evolves from “something that helps me do tasks” into “something that helps eliminate classes of tasks from my life.”

Without Forge, Francis can still become useful.
But it will remain bounded by the imagination and implementation of its original builders.

With Forge, Francis becomes a system that can grow new lawful capability in response to the actual recurring shape of the user’s work.

This is not self-modification in the reckless sense.
It is not uncontrolled code generation.
It is not a magical auto-upgrade engine.
It is not a loophole around governance.

Forge is governed capability growth.

It is the pipeline by which Francis notices repeated friction, proposes an improvement, stages an implementation, validates it, and presents it for promotion under explicit approval.

This section defines the doctrine of Forge:

* why Forge matters
* what friction is
* how Forge proposals begin
* how staged capabilities must be built
* what validation and documentation are required
* why promotion must always remain governed
* how Forge interacts with Missions, Apprenticeship, Lens, and the Capability Economy
* what failure modes must be blocked before Forge becomes dangerous or incoherent

The central question of this section is:

> How can Francis grow new capability from real-world recurring friction without becoming an uncontrolled self-authoring system?

The answer is:

**friction → proposal → staged implementation → tests, docs, and risk tier → validation → explicit user promotion → active capability**

That is the Forge path.

---

## 8.1 Why Forge Matters

If Francis only becomes better at operating existing tools, it still leaves one major class of value untouched:

**removing recurring friction altogether.**

There is a huge difference between:

* helping the user do something faster
* and making that something no longer need to be done repeatedly at all

Forge exists for the second category.

### 8.1.1 The highest leverage is often not execution but elimination

Many recurring burdens in computing are not hard because each individual step is difficult.

They are hard because they recur:

* the same cleanup sequence
* the same formatting pass
* the same report assembly
* the same validation path
* the same system check
* the same multi-step handoff
* the same brittle workaround
* the same repetitive research flow
* the same translation from intent into tooling

An assistant can help perform these again and again.
Forge exists to ask a higher-value question:

> Should this still be a repeated burden at all?

That is compounding leverage.

### 8.1.2 Forge is how Francis becomes more than static intelligence

A system without Forge is fundamentally static.

It may:

* answer better
* act better
* understand context better
* learn preferences better

But it still depends on a fixed capability set plus ad hoc one-off behavior.

Forge changes that by letting Francis turn observed repetition into reusable governed structure.

This is one of the deepest reasons Francis can become “mine” over time instead of merely “useful.”

### 8.1.3 Forge is central to the compounding promise

Francis promises not only help, but leverage that compounds.

That compounding comes from several layers:

* continuity
* apprenticeship
* knowledge fabric
* mission history
* better trust calibration
* domain adaptation

But Forge is one of the clearest mechanisms of compounding because it produces durable new capability.

A capability that gets promoted into the system can save effort:

* tomorrow
* next week
* across future projects
* across similar missions
* across similar deployments
* across managed copies where lawful and appropriate

Forge is therefore one of the main engines of long-term value creation.

---

## 8.2 What Forge Is

Forge is the governed capability-construction pipeline inside Francis.

It is the part of the system that notices recurring friction and tries to convert it into reusable power.

That power may take many forms, including:

* automation packs
* operator routines
* validation helpers
* workflow templates
* action bundles
* domain-specific micro-tools
* approval-preparation helpers
* reporting builders
* remediation handlers
* parameterized skills from apprenticeship

### 8.2.1 Forge is not random tool generation

Forge is not “generate something clever because maybe it will help.”

It must begin from real observed friction, repeated need, or recognized pattern value.

The key inputs to Forge include:

* recurring user pain
* repeated mission overhead
* repeated manual steps
* recurring system fragility
* observed apprenticeship patterns
* repeated bottlenecks
* recurring approval-preparation work
* domain-specific missing functionality

This keeps Forge grounded.

### 8.2.2 Forge is not promotion by enthusiasm

A proposed capability is not the same thing as a valid capability.

A staged capability is not the same thing as a promoted capability.

A capability that “seems useful” is not the same thing as one that has earned trust.

Forge must preserve these distinctions carefully.

### 8.2.3 Forge is governed growth

Forge is one of the most powerful systems in Francis, which means it is also one of the most dangerous if poorly bounded.

If Forge is allowed to become:

* auto-promoting
* under-tested
* undocumented
* weakly scoped
* weakly receipted
* loosely risked
* hard to inspect

then Francis stops being governed capability growth and starts becoming uncontrolled mutation.

That must never happen.

---

## 8.3 What Friction Means

Forge begins with friction.

But “friction” must be understood precisely.

Friction is not merely “something the system found interesting.”
It is not novelty.
It is not random inconvenience.
It is not every repeated event.

Friction is repeated cost in the user’s operational world.

### 8.3.1 Forms of friction

Friction may appear as:

* repeated manual sequences
* repeated workaround logic
* repetitive validation steps
* repeated formatting or transformation work
* repeated environment setup
* repeated comparison or triage effort
* repeated approval prep
* repeated mission stall patterns
* repeated bug-fix rituals
* repeated evidence-gathering flows
* repeated reporting or summarization structure

In other words, friction is usually one of:

* repeated labor
* repeated cognitive burden
* repeated coordination cost
* repeated context reconstruction
* repeated low-value ceremony
* repeated preventable failure pattern

### 8.3.2 Friction must be meaningful

Not every repetition deserves a new capability.

Forge should be biased toward friction that is:

* recurring
* costly enough to matter
* stable enough to parameterize
* useful enough to justify ongoing maintenance
* safe enough to stage
* clearly valuable if eliminated

This helps prevent capability sprawl.

### 8.3.3 Friction may come from Missions, Telemetry, or Apprenticeship

Forge should be able to detect potential friction from several major sources:

* repeated Mission history
* repeated telemetry patterns
* repeated user corrections
* observed teaching sessions
* repeated approval queues of similar shape
* recurring incident types
* repeated report or workflow assembly
* repeated “do this for me again” structures

This is why Forge is deeply connected to the rest of the architecture.

---

## 8.4 The Forge Pipeline

Forge must follow a stable and law-bound pipeline.

The canonical path is:

**friction → proposal → staged implementation → tests/docs/risk tier → validation → user promotion → active capability**

Each stage matters.

### 8.4.1 Friction detection

The system notices repeated burden or recurring pattern value.

This stage may include evidence such as:

* repeated steps across Missions
* repeated failures handled similarly
* repeated user instruction patterns
* repeated manual cleanup
* repeated low-value transformation tasks
* repeated triage logic
* repeated branching or test discipline patterns

Friction detection should be evidence-led, not guess-led.

### 8.4.2 Proposal

Before building anything, Forge should create a proposal.

The proposal should answer:

* what friction was observed?
* how often did it recur?
* what capability is being proposed?
* what part of the burden would it remove?
* what inputs would it require?
* what scope would it operate in?
* what risk tier appears likely?
* what tests and docs would be required?
* what benefit is expected if promoted?

This is where Francis says, in effect:

> I believe I can eliminate this recurring burden. Here is what I observed, what I built, and how it would help.

### 8.4.3 Staged implementation

If the proposal is accepted for staging, Forge may construct a capability in an isolated staging context.

This is crucial.

The capability must not be treated as live simply because it now exists.

Staged implementation should remain:

* isolated
* inspectable
* non-authoritative
* non-promoted
* receipted
* tied to its originating proposal

### 8.4.4 Tests, docs, and risk tier

Every staged capability must carry minimum quality material.

This includes:

* tests
* documentation
* risk tier
* summary
* expected scope
* dependencies
* validation path
* known limits or uncertainty

Without these, the capability is not promotion-ready.

### 8.4.5 Validation

Validation checks whether the staged capability behaves as intended under appropriate conditions.

Validation may include:

* unit tests
* integration tests
* simulation or dry-run paths
* example cases from the originating friction pattern
* risk-specific checks
* scope-bound execution checks
* policy compliance checks
* receipt and rollback checks where relevant

A capability that has not been validated has not earned trust.

### 8.4.6 User promotion

Promotion is the line between possibility and active power.

Only explicit promotion makes the capability part of the user’s active arsenal.

Promotion must therefore be:

* explicit
* inspectable
* receipted
* tied to risk
* linked to tests/docs/summary
* reversible where appropriate
* visible in the capability catalog

Auto-promotion must never occur.

### 8.4.7 Active capability

Once promoted, the capability becomes part of Francis’s usable operational library.

But even then, it remains governed by:

* scope
* mode
* approvals
* policy
* risk tier
* receipts
* verification
* promotion status
* versioning

Promotion creates availability, not lawlessness.

---

## 8.5 Forge Rules

Forge must operate under strict rules or it will become one of the fastest paths to system corruption.

### 8.5.1 Never auto-promote

This rule must remain absolute.

No matter how often a pattern has appeared, no matter how “obvious” the utility seems, and no matter how confident Francis feels in the implementation, the system must never auto-promote a newly staged capability into active use.

Promotion is a governance event.

### 8.5.2 Every staged capability must have tests

A capability without tests is an unverified suggestion wearing the costume of a tool.

Tests do not need to be maximal in every case, but they must be meaningful enough to show that the capability has been exercised against the pattern it claims to address.

### 8.5.3 Every staged capability must have documentation

Documentation matters because promotion is a trust act.

The user must be able to know:

* what this capability does
* what problem it solves
* where it should be used
* what scope it touches
* what risk it carries
* how it was tested
* how to review or disable it
* what assumptions it makes

A promoted capability should never feel mysterious.

### 8.5.4 Every staged capability must have a risk tier

Forge output must never pretend that all capabilities are equally safe.

Some capabilities may be:

* read-only
* low-risk transformation helpers
* branch-first patch routines
* higher-risk mutation flows
* external-action workflows
* destructive if misused
* privacy-sensitive
* connector-dependent

Risk tier is one of the main ways Forge remains governable.

### 8.5.5 Every promotion must be receipted

The user should always be able to inspect:

* when the capability was proposed
* when it was staged
* what evidence triggered it
* what tests ran
* what docs were attached
* what risk tier was assigned
* when it was promoted
* under whose approval it became active

This is part of the trust model.

### 8.5.6 Staged output must remain isolated until approved

Isolation is one of the most important protective boundaries in Forge.

A staged capability must not quietly influence the active environment before promotion.
It must not begin participating in runs.
It must not inject itself into action selection.
It must not assume future approval.

Until promoted, it is staged and nothing more.

---

## 8.6 What Forge Should Feel Like to the User

Forge is a powerful backend idea, but it must also feel right in the user experience.

The user should not experience Forge as hidden mutation.
They should experience it as controlled compounding.

### 8.6.1 The ideal Forge moment

A mature Forge interaction should feel like this:

> I noticed you keep doing this same costly thing.
> I studied the pattern and staged a capability that could eliminate it.
> Here is what it does.
> Here is how it was tested.
> Here is the risk tier.
> Here is where it would apply.
> Promote?

That is one of the highest-value user moments in Francis.

### 8.6.2 Forge should feel like leverage, not surprise

The user should feel:

* this came from real repeated pain
* this could genuinely save me time
* I understand what it would do
* I am still in control
* Francis is helping me remove friction, not just perform it faster
* nothing became active behind my back

That emotional experience is crucial.

### 8.6.3 Forge should not feel like speculative invention spam

A weak Forge would constantly propose low-value automations, novelty ideas, or barely useful internal tools.

That would quickly make the user distrust the whole pipeline.

Forge must be selective enough that proposals feel:

* earned
* relevant
* grounded
* useful
* not overwhelming
* not performative

High proposal quality matters.

---

## 8.7 Forge and Missions

Missions are one of the main places Forge gets its evidence.

### 8.7.1 Missions reveal repeated burden

Across multiple Missions, Francis may notice patterns like:

* similar setup steps
* similar blockers
* similar validation sequences
* repeated user corrections
* repeated reporting shapes
* repeated cleanup routines
* repeated investigation flows

These repeated burdens are exactly the kind of evidence Forge needs.

### 8.7.2 Mission-linked proposals are easier to trust

A Forge proposal is stronger when Francis can say:

* this pattern recurred across three Missions
* this step consumed repeated effort
* this validation path was repeated five times
* this approval-preparation structure keeps reappearing
* this issue type keeps forcing the same manual workflow

Mission evidence makes proposals feel grounded rather than speculative.

### 8.7.3 Forge capabilities can strengthen future Missions

Once promoted, a capability may reduce future Mission overhead by:

* shortening setup
* improving validation
* reducing repeat steps
* reducing stalled states
* improving handoff quality
* improving briefing quality
* auto-preparing evidence
* standardizing a repeated safe workflow

This is one way Missions and Forge create a compounding loop of value.

---

## 8.8 Forge and Apprenticeship

Forge and Apprenticeship are tightly related, but they are not identical.

Apprenticeship is about learning from demonstration.
Forge is about building reusable governed capability.

### 8.8.1 Apprenticeship can feed Forge

A user may demonstrate a workflow multiple times and say:

* “watch how I do this”
* “that’s the pattern”
* “make this reusable”
* “turn this into something I can promote later”

That is one of the clearest pathways from Apprenticeship into Forge.

### 8.8.2 Forge is where learned patterns become assets

A learned pattern is not yet an active system power.

Forge is where that pattern becomes:

* parameterized
* tested
* documented
* risk-tiered
* staged for approval
* promotable into the capability catalog

This is how one-off teaching becomes durable leverage.

### 8.8.3 Apprenticeship without Forge stays too ephemeral

Without Forge, Apprenticeship risks becoming “Francis got a bit better at this” without producing an explicit reusable asset.

Forge is what lets the user keep the learned value in a visible, governable form.

That is much stronger.

---

## 8.9 Forge and the Capability Catalog

Forge is not complete if built capabilities vanish into invisibility.

Promoted capabilities must live inside a coherent library.

### 8.9.1 Capabilities are assets

Every promoted capability should be treated as a real system asset:

* versioned
* inspectable
* testable
* documented
* risk-tiered
* governable
* reversible where appropriate
* attributable to source pattern or proposal

This is part of the Capability Economy later in the roadmap, but it begins here.

### 8.9.2 The catalog should answer practical questions

The user should be able to inspect:

* what capabilities exist
* which are active
* which are staged
* what each one does
* what risk tier it carries
* what tests/docs support it
* where it came from
* which Missions or patterns led to it
* whether it can be disabled, upgraded, or deprecated

A serious system does not hide its arsenal.

### 8.9.3 Catalog coherence matters

A bad Forge can flood the system with half-overlapping micro-capabilities until the catalog becomes messy and hard to trust.

Forge must therefore value:

* capability clarity
* capability uniqueness
* sensible boundaries
* avoidable overlap reduction
* deprecation paths
* version discipline

A growing arsenal is good.
A junk drawer is not.

---

## 8.10 Forge and Governance

Forge is one of the most governance-sensitive systems in Francis because it changes what Francis can do in the future.

That means governance must apply at every layer.

### 8.10.1 Capability growth is power growth

Every promoted capability changes the future operational surface of Francis.

That means Forge is not merely content generation.
It is controlled power expansion.

This is why tests, docs, risk, receipts, and promotion law are all mandatory.

### 8.10.2 Forge must not bypass core law

A staged or promoted capability must still obey:

* mode
* scope
* approvals
* policies
* risk tier
* receipts
* verification requirements
* revocability

Forge does not create law exemptions.

### 8.10.3 Promotion is not permanent trust

Even promoted capabilities should remain subject to:

* inspection
* disablement
* deprecation
* replacement
* versioning
* incident review
* rogue or anomaly handling if necessary

A capability can be useful and still later need to be withdrawn or superseded.

### 8.10.4 Forge must remain subordinate to the user

The user is not merely a recipient of Forge.

The user is the authority over whether proposed power becomes real power.

This must remain emotionally and structurally obvious.

---

## 8.11 Forge Failure Modes

This section must explicitly name the ways Forge can go wrong.

### 8.11.1 Forge drifts into uncontrolled self-modification

Symptoms:

* capabilities appear active without explicit promotion
* generated tools alter system behavior before review
* the system starts changing its own operational surface opaquely

Correction:

* re-center staged isolation, explicit promotion, and capability governance

### 8.11.2 Forge drifts into proposal spam

Symptoms:

* too many low-value capability proposals
* repeated “I can automate this” interruptions
* weak evidence for actual friction
* user starts ignoring Forge entirely

Correction:

* re-center proposal quality thresholds and evidence-backed friction detection

### 8.11.3 Forge drifts into under-tested automation

Symptoms:

* staged capabilities with weak tests
* exciting summaries hiding fragile behavior
* promotion decisions based on confidence rather than evidence

Correction:

* re-center validation rigor, docs, and risk-tier discipline

### 8.11.4 Forge drifts into capability clutter

Symptoms:

* overlapping tools
* unclear differences between capabilities
* hard-to-manage catalog
* weak deprecation/versioning discipline
* user no longer trusts the arsenal

Correction:

* re-center catalog coherence, versioning, and capability design boundaries

### 8.11.5 Forge drifts into hidden power expansion

Symptoms:

* Francis grows more able, but the user cannot clearly see how
* promoted capabilities are hard to inspect
* action selection starts depending on capabilities the user barely remembers approving

Correction:

* re-center visibility, receipts, promotion review, and catalog legibility

### 8.11.6 Forge drifts into speculative invention

Symptoms:

* capability ideas with weak real-world grounding
* internally “clever” but low-value generated tools
* builders become enamored with creation volume rather than leverage

Correction:

* re-center recurring real friction as the source of Forge value

---

## 8.12 Forge Doctrine Statement

If Francis Forge had to be defined in one doctrine sentence for builders, it would be this:

> Forge is the governed capability-construction pipeline through which Francis turns repeated real-world friction into staged, tested, documented, risk-tiered, user-promotable capabilities that compound leverage without allowing uncontrolled self-modification, hidden power growth, or promotion without law.

That sentence should be strong enough to reject bad architecture early.

---

## 8.13 Forge Acceptance Criteria

Section 8 is satisfied only if the roadmap, Forge pipeline, capability catalog, governance systems, and user experience remain aligned to all of the following:

* Forge is treated as a core compounding-leverage system, not a novelty automation generator
* Forge begins from recurring real friction, repeated burden, or structured learned patterns rather than speculative invention
* the canonical Forge pipeline remains friction → proposal → staged implementation → tests/docs/risk tier → validation → explicit user promotion → active capability
* no staged capability auto-promotes under any circumstance
* every staged capability includes meaningful tests, documentation, summary, and risk tier
* staged capabilities remain isolated and non-operative until explicitly promoted
* every promotion event is receipted, inspectable, and linked back to proposal evidence
* promoted capabilities remain subject to all normal governance law including scope, mode, approvals, policy, receipts, and revocability
* Forge integrates cleanly with Missions, Apprenticeship, and the Capability Catalog
* the user experience of Forge feels like controlled leverage growth rather than hidden mutation or proposal spam
* the capability catalog remains coherent, inspectable, and versioned rather than cluttered or opaque
* Forge failure modes are explicitly recognized and corrected during buildout
* Francis never degrades into uncontrolled self-modification, speculative auto-tool generation, or invisible power expansion under the excuse of becoming more adaptive

# 9.0 Apprenticeship: Teach Once, Keep Forever

One of the biggest failures in modern AI systems is that they remain permanently generic unless the human continuously translates themselves into prompts, settings, templates, or code.

The burden of adaptation stays on the user.

That is backwards.

A true Operator Layer must not only respond to instructions.
It must be able to **learn from demonstration**.

That is what **Apprenticeship** is for.

Apprenticeship is how Francis becomes more than generally capable.
It is how Francis becomes **operationally shaped around the user**.
It is how repeated teaching stops being wasted effort and starts becoming durable leverage.

Without Apprenticeship, Francis may still be helpful.
It may still build continuity, operate Missions, stage Forge capabilities, and assist in place.

But it will always be limited by a structural weakness:

> the user must keep re-teaching the system in indirect ways.

With Apprenticeship, Francis can begin learning from how the user actually works:

* how they sequence tasks
* what they consider “done”
* where they are strict
* where they are flexible
* what patterns they repeat
* what risks they avoid
* what decision points matter
* what style of work feels natural to them

This is not imitation for its own sake.
It is not roleplay.
It is not broad recording.
It is not behavioral surveillance disguised as personalization.

Apprenticeship is governed pattern acquisition from explicit, bounded demonstration.

This section defines the doctrine of Apprenticeship:

* why it matters
* what it is
* how teaching sessions should work
* how demonstrations become structured patterns
* how those patterns become reviewable and reusable
* how Apprenticeship interacts with Forge, Missions, Lens, and the Digital Twin identity
* what boundaries must prevent it from becoming creepy, opaque, or overreaching

The central question of this section is:

> How can Francis learn from the user’s real workflows in a way that compounds value, strengthens delegation, and preserves trust without requiring indiscriminate recording or silent behavioral capture?

The answer is:

**demonstrate → label intent → replay → generalize → skillize → stage for governed promotion**

That is the Apprenticeship path.

---

## 9.1 Why Apprenticeship Matters

If Francis is meant to become a Digital Twin Operator, then it cannot remain fully generic forever.

It must be able to become shaped by the user’s actual operating style.

That shaping can come from several sources:

* Mission history
* approval patterns
* continuity over time
* preferences expressed directly
* Forge proposals
* evaluation feedback

But one of the strongest and most direct sources is demonstration.

That is why Apprenticeship matters so much.

### 9.1.1 Apprenticeship solves the translation burden

Today, users often have to translate their own workflows into:

* repeated prompts
* templates
* checklists
* macros
* scripts
* SOPs
* painstaking corrections
* manual automation logic

Apprenticeship reduces that burden by letting the user say, in effect:

> Watch how I do this. Learn the pattern. Make it reusable.

That is an enormous unlock.

### 9.1.2 Apprenticeship strengthens delegation

Delegation works better when Francis does not just know the abstract objective, but understands the user’s preferred way of carrying it.

Apprenticeship helps Francis learn things like:

* how much validation the user wants
* what order they prefer for certain steps
* what counts as sufficient evidence
* how they like outputs structured
* which shortcuts they accept
* which shortcuts they reject
* when they want escalation
* when they want Francis to stay quiet

That makes future handoffs cleaner and more trustworthy.

### 9.1.3 Apprenticeship turns effort into compounding value

A user teaching Francis once should not feel like a one-off conversation artifact.

It should feel like investment.

That is the emotional and strategic heart of Apprenticeship:

> if I take the time to show Francis how I work, that value should persist.

Without this, personalization remains shallow.
With it, Francis can become meaningfully more “mine.”

---

## 9.2 What Apprenticeship Is

Apprenticeship is the governed process by which Francis learns reusable workflow patterns from explicit user demonstration.

It is not a vague learning layer.

It is a concrete pipeline for converting observed bounded examples into parameterized, reviewable, reusable operational structure.

### 9.2.1 Apprenticeship is learning from demonstration

The simplest form of the concept is:

* the user performs a task
* Francis watches within declared scope
* the user labels the intent
* Francis identifies the recurring pattern
* Francis replays or simulates the pattern
* Francis generalizes it into a reusable form
* Francis stages it for review or promotion

This makes learning concrete rather than mystical.

### 9.2.2 Apprenticeship is not broad memory absorption

Francis should not “learn the user” by quietly absorbing every behavior from every surface.

That would be weakly governed and likely creepy.

Apprenticeship must come from:

* explicit teaching moments
* bounded scopes
* declared demonstrations
* inspectable pattern extraction
* reviewable outputs
* governed promotion into capability when appropriate

The key word is **explicit**.

### 9.2.3 Apprenticeship is about operational pattern, not personal essence

Francis is not trying to absorb the user’s soul, personality, or private interior life.

It is learning operationally relevant things like:

* workflow shape
* decision thresholds
* sequencing habits
* quality standards
* preferred review points
* preferred output forms
* recurring subroutines
* acceptable variance ranges

This is important for both safety and clarity.

---

## 9.3 The Apprenticeship Pipeline

The canonical pipeline for Apprenticeship is:

**demonstrate → label intent → replay → generalize → skillize**

This sequence must remain disciplined.

### 9.3.1 Demonstrate

The user performs the workflow or task inside explicit teaching context.

This may involve:

* executing steps in a tool
* editing files
* running commands
* preparing a report
* investigating a failure
* assembling a deliverable
* reviewing changes
* handling a recurring ops routine

The demonstration should happen inside:

* declared scope
* declared mode
* clear teaching context
* lawful telemetry boundaries

Demonstration is the evidence source.

### 9.3.2 Label intent

Raw action sequence alone is not enough.

The user or system must identify what the sequence was actually trying to accomplish.

This matters because the same surface actions can correspond to very different intentions.

Intent labeling may clarify:

* objective
* success condition
* acceptable shortcuts
* important branching points
* required validations
* known exceptions
* what “done” means here
* what kind of environments or inputs this pattern applies to

Intent is what separates “recorded movement” from “understood workflow.”

### 9.3.3 Replay

Before a pattern is generalized, Francis should be able to replay or simulate the interpreted sequence in a bounded way.

Replay helps answer:

* did Francis understand the steps correctly?
* did it miss key decision points?
* are the inputs properly identified?
* is the sequence overly brittle?
* what assumptions is the system making?

Replay is both a learning check and a trust check.

### 9.3.4 Generalize

Once the demonstrated sequence is understood, Francis should attempt to generalize it.

Generalization means extracting the reusable structure from the specific instance.

This may involve identifying:

* variable inputs
* stable steps
* optional branches
* decision criteria
* validation checkpoints
* scope conditions
* failure handling
* output expectations

Generalization is where the system turns “what happened once” into “what could work again.”

### 9.3.5 Skillize

Skillizing means converting the generalized pattern into a reusable governed asset.

This may produce:

* a staged capability
* a reusable workflow template
* a parameterized operator routine
* a reviewable internal skill definition
* a Forge-ready proposal artifact

Skillizing is the bridge from learning to leverage.

---

## 9.4 The User Experience of Apprenticeship

Apprenticeship must feel intuitive, empowering, and bounded.

The user should not need to become a systems engineer just to teach Francis.

### 9.4.1 The core user phrases

At maturity, the user should be able to say things like:

* “Watch how I do this.”
* “This is the pattern.”
* “That choice matters.”
* “Do it like this next time.”
* “Turn this into a reusable capability.”
* “That part is optional.”
* “Always verify before that step.”
* “When this happens, stop and ask me.”

These are natural teaching interactions, not programming syntax.

### 9.4.2 Teaching should feel like investment

A good Apprenticeship session should make the user feel:

* I am teaching something that will matter later
* Francis is actually learning the shape, not just memorizing one instance
* I can review what it thinks I meant
* nothing becomes active without my knowledge
* the learned value will stay with me

That emotional outcome is critical.

### 9.4.3 Francis should feel like an apprentice, not a recorder

The difference matters.

A recorder simply stores what happened.

An apprentice tries to understand:

* why that step existed
* what was variable
* what was essential
* where judgment occurred
* what should happen in a similar future case
* where uncertainty remains

The user should feel taught, not harvested.

---

## 9.5 Demonstration Sessions

Apprenticeship should happen inside bounded teaching sessions or clear equivalent moments, not through vague ambient learning.

### 9.5.1 What a teaching session is

A teaching session is a declared context in which the user is showing Francis how to perform or understand a workflow.

This declaration matters because it clarifies:

* this is instructional
* this scope matters
* these signals may be used for learning
* this pattern is a candidate for reuse
* this is not ambient passive surveillance

The teaching context can be lightweight, but it must be explicit.

### 9.5.2 Teaching session inputs

A teaching session may include:

* actions in tools or files
* command sequences
* review decisions
* spoken or typed explanation
* corrections by the user
* demonstrations of branching logic
* examples of acceptable and unacceptable outcomes
* notes about exceptions or edge cases

This helps Francis avoid overfitting to raw action alone.

### 9.5.3 Teaching sessions must remain bounded

Even in teaching mode, Apprenticeship must remain governed.

This means:

* explicit scope
* explicit start and stop
* visible learning state
* no silent expansion into unrelated surfaces
* no hidden retention beyond policy
* no automatic promotion into active capability

Boundedness is part of trust.

---

## 9.6 What Francis Should Learn

Apprenticeship is strongest when it learns the right level of pattern.

Too shallow, and the learning is not very useful.
Too deep or too broad, and it becomes opaque or invasive.

The target is operationally meaningful pattern.

### 9.6.1 Workflow shape

Francis should learn the broad shape of repeated routines.

Examples:

* setup → inspect → modify → validate → summarize
* gather data → compare → filter → escalate
* reproduce → isolate → patch → test → hand back
* inspect inventory → identify drift → prepare reorder suggestions

Workflow shape is often one of the most reusable parts.

### 9.6.2 Decision points

Many workflows are defined less by the raw actions than by where the user makes judgments.

Francis should learn:

* when the user stops to verify
* when they escalate
* when they accept approximation
* when they reject a shortcut
* when they choose branch A versus branch B
* when they require human review

These are high-value parts of the pattern.

### 9.6.3 Validation discipline

Francis should learn what kinds of checks the user considers essential.

Examples:

* always run tests before closure
* always compare before-after data
* always verify output shape
* always inspect diff before promotion
* always ask before destructive action
* always summarize remaining uncertainty

Validation style is one of the strongest parts of operational identity.

### 9.6.4 Output preferences

Apprenticeship can also learn what form the user prefers for outputs.

Examples include:

* concise summary first
* detailed evidence below
* tabular comparison
* diff-first review
* recommendation with options
* stage-and-ask instead of stage-and-apply

This helps Francis feel more aligned in day-to-day use.

### 9.6.5 Exception handling

Some of the most valuable learning comes from how the user handles exceptions.

Examples:

* stop and ask if this threshold is crossed
* skip this branch if environment is missing dependency X
* if verification fails twice, deadletter and notify
* if confidence is low, present options instead of acting

This is where Apprenticeship can become truly operational.

---

## 9.7 What Apprenticeship Must Not Become

The strength of this section depends heavily on naming anti-goals.

Apprenticeship is powerful, and if built sloppily it can quickly become unacceptable.

### 9.7.1 Not indiscriminate recording

Apprenticeship must not be implemented as “record everything and infer later.”

That approach is:

* privacy-weak
* hard to govern
* hard to explain
* hard to review
* easy to resent
* too close to surveillance

Francis must learn from explicit bounded demonstration, not from indiscriminate capture.

### 9.7.2 Not behavioral profiling theater

Francis is not trying to generate psychological narratives or human archetypes about the user.

It should not drift into:

* amateur psychoanalysis
* emotional inference theater
* personality extraction
* vague identity labeling
* creepy behavioral summaries

It is learning workflow, not soul.

### 9.7.3 Not silent permission expansion

A learned pattern must never create new authority.

Francis must not think:

* “I’ve seen them do this before, so I can now do it whenever”
* “This pattern implies broader scope”
* “Because I understand their style, approval is no longer necessary”
* “Their likely preference is enough authority to proceed”

Pattern knowledge informs behavior.
It does not replace law.

### 9.7.4 Not opaque internalization

The user must not lose sight of what Francis believes it has learned.

If Francis cannot explain the learned pattern in human terms, the Apprenticeship system is too opaque.

---

## 9.8 Replay and Review

Before a learned pattern becomes reusable, Francis should be able to show what it thinks it understood.

Replay and review are crucial here.

### 9.8.1 Replay is a trust mechanism

Replay allows the user to inspect whether Francis interpreted the demonstration correctly.

This may include:

* showing the inferred step sequence
* identifying variable inputs
* showing branching conditions
* highlighting required validation points
* showing where human approval would still be required
* identifying places where Francis is uncertain

This turns hidden learning into inspectable learning.

### 9.8.2 Review should allow correction

A mature Apprenticeship system should allow the user to say things like:

* “No, that step is optional.”
* “That branch only applies in this environment.”
* “You missed the validation step.”
* “Always ask me before this part.”
* “This is the actual goal, not what you inferred.”
* “That exception matters a lot.”

Correction is part of learning, not a sign of failure.

### 9.8.3 Replay should not equal automatic execution

Replay exists for understanding and validation, not for automatic live deployment.

A replayed pattern is still not an active capability until it moves through the proper governed path.

---

## 9.9 Generalization and Parameterization

The heart of Apprenticeship is not memorizing exact movement.

It is learning reusable structure.

### 9.9.1 Generalization extracts the reusable pattern

Francis should ask, implicitly or explicitly:

* what stayed constant here?
* what changed between examples?
* what was the real objective?
* what inputs are variable?
* what decision criteria are stable?
* what outputs define success?
* which constraints always matter?
* which parts are environment-specific?

This is where teaching becomes durable.

### 9.9.2 Parameterization makes reuse possible

A pattern should become parameterized enough that it can apply again without being tied to one specific past event.

Parameters may include things like:

* file path
* branch name
* report period
* system target
* comparison set
* validation threshold
* escalation condition
* output format

Parameterization is what makes the learned skill flexible rather than brittle.

### 9.9.3 Generalization must remain honest

Francis must not over-generalize from too little evidence.

A single demonstration may be enough for some simple patterns, but not for complex workflows.

The system must be able to say:

* this appears reusable
* this might still be too specific
* this part is uncertain
* I need another example to generalize safely

This is part of trust calibration.

---

## 9.10 From Apprenticeship to Forge

Apprenticeship and Forge meet at a powerful transition point.

A learned pattern becomes much more valuable when it can become a staged reusable capability.

### 9.10.1 Skillize means Forge-ready structure

When Francis skillizes a learned workflow, it should produce something that can be handed into Forge with:

* pattern summary
* parameterization
* usage scope
* decision logic
* validation expectations
* risk tier candidate
* documentation draft
* test candidate structure

This turns teaching into an asset pipeline.

### 9.10.2 Not every learned pattern needs promotion

Some learned patterns may remain lightweight and local.

Others may deserve full staged capability status.

Francis should distinguish between:

* preference adaptation
* workflow understanding
* candidate reusable skill
* Forge-worthy promoted capability

Not all learning must become system expansion.

### 9.10.3 Promotion still requires governance

Even if the user taught the pattern directly, that does not eliminate the need for:

* tests
* docs
* risk awareness
* review
* explicit promotion

Teaching is evidence, not automatic deployment authority.

---

## 9.11 Apprenticeship and the Digital Twin

Apprenticeship is one of the strongest mechanisms by which Francis becomes a true Digital Twin.

### 9.11.1 The twin becomes operationally aligned

Through Apprenticeship, Francis can become better aligned with:

* the user’s sequencing
* the user’s decision thresholds
* the user’s review habits
* the user’s validation discipline
* the user’s preferred output shape
* the user’s escalation boundaries
* the user’s tolerance for autonomy

This is much stronger than surface-level personalization.

### 9.11.2 Apprenticeship should make takeover feel more “like me”

As Francis learns repeated workflows, future Pilot Mode or Assist behavior should begin to feel more aligned.

The user should increasingly feel:

* it solves this the way I would want it solved
* it pauses where I would want caution
* it validates where I would validate
* it hands back in the format I prefer
* it is not doing strange AI improvisation

That is the Digital Twin promise made real.

### 9.11.3 Digital Twin alignment must remain subordinate

Even as Francis becomes more aligned, it must never interpret that alignment as autonomous sovereignty.

The better Francis knows the user’s patterns, the more important it becomes that it still obey:

* mode
* scope
* approvals
* policy
* explicit authority

Alignment is not permission.

---

## 9.12 Apprenticeship and Lens

Lens must make Apprenticeship feel visible, legible, and non-creepy.

### 9.12.1 Lens should show teaching state

If Francis is in a teaching or observation-for-learning context, that should be visible.

The user should know:

* that teaching is active
* what scope is included
* what kind of signals are being used
* when the session starts
* when it ends
* what was learned or staged afterward

This is part of trust.

### 9.12.2 Lens should help review learned patterns

A mature Lens should make it easy to inspect:

* what Francis thinks the pattern is
* where uncertainty exists
* what parameters it extracted
* what part could become reusable
* what would require approval to promote

That makes Apprenticeship feel like collaboration, not hidden ingestion.

### 9.12.3 Lens should prevent magical ambiguity

The user should not feel that Francis “somehow learned me” in unexplained ways.

A good Lens should make the lineage visible:

* you taught this here
* this example contributed to the pattern
* this is how it was generalized
* this is what it could become

That is much more trustworthy.

---

## 9.13 Governance of Apprenticeship

Because Apprenticeship deals with behavioral pattern learning, governance here must be especially strong.

### 9.13.1 Explicit teaching context

Where possible, learning should occur inside explicit teaching contexts or clearly signaled learning moments.

The user should not feel like every action is a training signal by default.

### 9.13.2 Scope still applies

Teaching a workflow in one repo, app, or environment does not create blanket learning rights across unrelated domains.

A learned pattern must remain connected to:

* its valid scope
* its intended domain
* its documented assumptions
* its promotion state

### 9.13.3 No hidden retention beyond policy

Learning sessions must obey retention law.

The system should keep what is useful for the learned pattern, not broad raw behavioral exhaust indefinitely.

### 9.13.4 Learned patterns must remain inspectable

If the user cannot review the learned skill in plain operational terms, the Apprenticeship system is too opaque to trust.

### 9.13.5 Learned pattern is not legal authority

This must remain absolute.

A learned pattern may improve future suggestions, planning, replay, or candidate capability staging.

It may not grant:

* new scope
* new mode
* new approval rights
* exemption from policy
* silent execution authority

That line must never blur.

---

## 9.14 Apprenticeship Failure Modes

This section must explicitly define the ways Apprenticeship can go wrong.

### 9.14.1 Apprenticeship drifts into surveillance

Symptoms:

* too much passive behavioral capture
* weak distinction between teaching and ordinary work
* unclear learning scope
* user starts feeling watched rather than taught

Correction:

* re-center explicit teaching context, bounded sessions, and visible learning state

### 9.14.2 Apprenticeship drifts into literal playback

Symptoms:

* Francis memorizes exact sequences without understanding intent
* small variations break the learned pattern
* brittle replay instead of reusable skill

Correction:

* re-center intent labeling, generalization, and parameterization

### 9.14.3 Apprenticeship drifts into opaque learning

Symptoms:

* user cannot inspect what was learned
* learned behavior changes future outputs mysteriously
* Francis feels more adapted, but the reasons are hidden

Correction:

* re-center replay, review, and plain-language pattern summaries

### 9.14.4 Apprenticeship drifts into silent authority growth

Symptoms:

* learned familiarity starts replacing approval
* Francis assumes repeated past behavior authorizes future action
* mode and scope boundaries weaken under “I know how you do this”

Correction:

* re-center the rule that learning informs behavior but never creates authority

### 9.14.5 Apprenticeship drifts into low-value overfitting

Symptoms:

* too many tiny patterns captured
* cluttered skill candidates
* brittle micro-behaviors mistaken for durable workflows
* little real leverage created

Correction:

* re-center meaningful recurring workflow structures and actual user value

---

## 9.15 Apprenticeship Doctrine Statement

If Francis Apprenticeship had to be defined in one doctrine sentence for builders, it would be this:

> Apprenticeship is the governed process by which Francis learns reusable operational patterns from explicit, bounded user demonstration through intent labeling, replay, generalization, parameterization, and skillization so that teaching compounds into durable leverage without becoming surveillance, opaque behavioral profiling, or silent authority expansion.

That sentence should be strong enough to reject weak learning architecture early.

---

## 9.16 Apprenticeship Acceptance Criteria

Section 9 is satisfied only if the roadmap, teaching flows, telemetry boundaries, replay systems, Forge integration, and user experience remain aligned to all of the following:

* Apprenticeship is treated as a core path to Digital Twin alignment rather than a vague personalization layer
* the canonical Apprenticeship path remains demonstrate → label intent → replay → generalize → skillize
* learning comes from explicit, bounded demonstration rather than indiscriminate passive recording
* teaching sessions are scope-bound, visible, and governable
* Francis learns operational pattern, decision points, validation discipline, exception handling, and output preferences rather than psychological or invasive personal profiling
* replay and review make learned patterns inspectable and correctable by the user
* generalization and parameterization are required so learned patterns become reusable rather than brittle playback
* skillized patterns can feed Forge, but promotion still requires full governance including tests, docs, risk, and explicit approval
* learned familiarity never creates new authority, scope, approval rights, or policy exemptions
* Lens makes learning state and learned-pattern review legible enough that Apprenticeship feels collaborative rather than mysterious
* Apprenticeship failure modes are explicitly recognized and corrected during buildout
* Francis never degrades into a surveillance learner, behavior-profiling system, literal macro recorder, or silently self-authorizing “I know how you work” agent under the excuse of becoming personalized

Session-by-session build — we do as much as we can each session, and once your full roadmap is assembled, you can paste the finished file back in and I’ll help refine, unify, tighten, or elevate it.
