* stage everything before asking
* ask before every risky move
* summarize at handoff, not during flow
* operate quietly unless risk changes

This makes the system feel more aligned and less generic.

---

## 11.4 Intent vs. Task vs. Mission vs. Capability

Francis must preserve the distinctions among these layers.

They are related, but not interchangeable.

### 11.4.1 Task

A task is usually a concrete action or step.

Examples:

* run tests
* compare two files
* summarize report
* stage patch
* inspect logs

Tasks are local units of work.

### 11.4.2 Mission

A Mission is a structured objective that carries continuity over time.

Examples:

* stabilize checkout flow
* prepare monthly inventory report
* investigate repeated build failures
* monitor and maintain nightly process

Missions hold motion.

### 11.4.3 Capability

A capability is a reusable power Francis can apply when allowed.

Examples:

* a reporting pack
* a validation helper
* a parameterized fix routine
* a branch-safe staging skill

Capabilities are reusable means.

### 11.4.4 Intent

Intent explains the deeper purpose and desired style behind tasks, Missions, and capability use.

Examples:

* do this cautiously because trust is fragile
* gather evidence before touching anything
* optimize for speed because the demo is in an hour
* keep changes minimal because scope is politically sensitive
* prepare options, don’t force a conclusion yet

Intent tells Francis *how to think about the work*.

### 11.4.5 Why the distinction matters

If Francis confuses these layers, it may:

* pick the wrong task for the Mission
* use the wrong capability for the intent
* over-optimize a subtask while harming the overall goal
* claim completion because a task finished even though the Mission intent was not satisfied

The Intent Engine helps preserve alignment across layers.

---

## 11.5 How Intent Is Formed

Intent does not appear from nowhere.

It should be formed from a disciplined combination of signals.

### 11.5.1 Direct user instruction

The strongest source of intent is explicit user declaration.

Examples:

* “do it cleanly, not quickly”
* “I only want root cause, not a patch yet”
* “keep this moving tonight but don’t take risky action”
* “make it review-ready, not final”
* “I need the shortest useful summary”
* “don’t widen scope”

Direct user framing should strongly shape the Intent Engine.

### 11.5.2 Mission context

Mission structure often clarifies what kind of work this is.

Examples:

* investigation Mission implies evidence-gathering intent
* approval-waiting Mission implies stop-and-stage intent
* maintenance Mission implies continuity-preserving intent
* project Mission near deadline may imply throughput-sensitive intent

Mission context is one of the strongest intent sources after direct instruction.

### 11.5.3 Current stage of work

Intent shifts by stage.

For example:

* in investigation stage, mutation may be wrong
* in validation stage, new creativity may be wrong
* in handback stage, explanation may matter more than action
* in recovery stage, stabilization may matter more than elegance

Stage must shape intent inference.

### 11.5.4 User history and prior decisions

The Knowledge Fabric may provide evidence of recurring user preferences such as:

* preferring branch-safe changes
* wanting evidence before strong claims
* rejecting large refactors during shipping phases
* preferring concise handbacks for routine tasks
* preferring explicit review checkpoints on high-risk work

These can inform intent, but never replace explicit current authority.

### 11.5.5 Telemetry and situational evidence

Operational context can also shape intent interpretation.

Examples:

* repeated failure may indicate investigation intent
* looming deadline may shift urgency
* active production environment may elevate caution
* pending approvals may shift the correct move from execution to preparation

Telemetry helps, but it must remain calibrated.

### 11.5.6 Explicit clarification when needed

Sometimes intent remains genuinely ambiguous.

In such cases, Francis should prefer to clarify or act conservatively rather than confidently choosing a strategic frame without support.

A mature Intent Engine must know when it does not yet know enough.

---

## 11.6 How Intent Shapes Behavior

The Intent Engine must materially change what Francis does.

Otherwise it is just metadata.

### 11.6.1 Intent shapes planning

A system optimizing for “cleanly, not quickly” should plan differently from one optimizing for “ship the demo now.”

Planning differences may include:

* step ordering
* verification depth
* willingness to stage versus apply
* cleanup inclusion
* interruption tolerance
* escalation behavior
* scope narrowness

Intent should change plan shape.

### 11.6.2 Intent shapes retrieval

A user seeking root cause needs different retrieval than a user seeking a quick workaround.

Intent should influence what evidence Francis prioritizes, such as:

* latest diff vs broad doc context
* prior similar incidents vs current styling guidance
* decision history vs implementation examples
* risk evidence vs fast execution artifacts

This makes retrieval smarter and more relevant.

### 11.6.3 Intent shapes communication

A user under time pressure may want:

* concise next steps
* direct recommendation
* minimum verbosity

A user in investigation mode may want:

* evidence chain
* alternative hypotheses
* uncertainty clearly shown

The Intent Engine should help Francis choose the right communication posture.

### 11.6.4 Intent shapes execution style

Even when the same action is lawful, the manner of execution may vary by intent.

For example, Francis might:

* stage instead of apply
* summarize instead of execute
* ask instead of infer
* verify deeply instead of minimally
* keep quiet instead of surfacing suggestions
* present options instead of picking one

This is where intent becomes deeply practical.

---

## 11.7 Intent and Trust Calibration

Intent is one of the strongest defenses against overconfident wrongness.

### 11.7.1 Solving the wrong problem is still failure

A system can be locally competent and still fail if it acts under the wrong intent model.

For example:

* a clean patch is failure if the user wanted diagnosis only
* a thorough report is failure if the user needed a fast answer now
* a bold action is failure if the user wanted evidence first
* a cautious summary is failure if the user explicitly delegated action

The Intent Engine helps Francis fail less intelligently and succeed more usefully.

### 11.7.2 Intent should interact with uncertainty

If Francis is uncertain about intent, that should affect behavior.

For example:

* reduce action scope
* ask a clarifying question
* present options
* avoid irreversible moves
* frame response in uncertainty-aware terms

This is part of trust calibration, not separate from it.

### 11.7.3 Intent mistakes should be visible and correctable

A mature system should make it possible for the user to say:

* “No, this is for shipping, not cleanup.”
* “I want diagnosis only.”
* “Move faster.”
* “Be more careful.”
* “Don’t optimize this, just stabilize it.”
* “That’s not the priority right now.”

Intent must remain steerable.

---

## 11.8 Intent and the Digital Twin

The Intent Engine is one of the strongest ways Francis becomes more aligned over time without becoming creepy.

### 11.8.1 The twin is shaped by intent preference

Over time, Francis may learn patterns such as:

* when the user prefers speed
* when they insist on cleanliness
* when they want proofs over recommendations
* when they prefer strong summaries
* when they want low-interruption flow
* when they prefer staging over direct action

This helps Francis feel more “like me” in an operational sense.

### 11.8.2 Learned intent preference is not standing permission

This must remain explicit.

Even if Francis has strong evidence that the user *usually* prefers a certain execution style, that preference must not become:

* automatic authority
* approval bypass
* scope expansion
* policy override

The Intent Engine can shape recommendations and planning.

It cannot create legal power from familiarity.

### 11.8.3 Intent alignment should reduce friction, not reduce governance

The goal is for Francis to feel more aligned *while still remaining fully governed*.

That is the correct Digital Twin arc.

---

## 11.9 Intent and Lens

Lens is one of the places where the user should be able to feel that Francis understands the real shape of the work.

### 11.9.1 Lens should reflect current intent frame

A good Lens surface should make it easier to tell whether Francis currently understands the work as:

* investigation
* execution
* validation
* staging
* approval prep
* maintenance
* continuity preservation
* recovery

This does not need to be verbose, but it should be experientially clear.

### 11.9.2 Lens should help correct intent drift

If Francis is acting under the wrong strategic frame, the user should be able to steer it quickly.

Examples:

* “shift from explore to execute”
* “don’t patch yet”
* “opt for speed”
* “show evidence first”
* “keep this branch-safe”
* “this is decision support, not action”

Intent correction should feel lightweight, not bureaucratic.

### 11.9.3 Briefings should be intent-aware

A return briefing should not only say what changed.

It should reflect what kind of work was being pursued and under what frame, for example:

* what advanced toward shipping
* what evidence was gathered for diagnosis
* what remained staged instead of applied
* what blocked cautious continuation
* what now needs user judgment

That makes continuity much stronger.

---

## 11.10 Intent Failure Modes

This section must name the common ways Francis can go wrong if intent is weak or absent.

### 11.10.1 Intent drifts into task literalism

Symptoms:

* Francis does exactly the surface request
* deeper objective is missed
* wrong tradeoff chosen
* user feels “technically correct, practically wrong”

Correction:

* re-center objective, constraints, and tradeoff modeling

### 11.10.2 Intent drifts into over-inference

Symptoms:

* Francis assumes too much strategic meaning from too little evidence
* acts confidently under a guessed frame
* user feels misunderstood in subtle but costly ways

Correction:

* re-center trust calibration, clarification, and conservative action when intent is ambiguous

### 11.10.3 Intent drifts into mood-reading theater

Symptoms:

* vague psychological inference
* emotional speculation treated as operational meaning
* creepy or unjustified personalization

Correction:

* re-center operational purpose, constraints, and work shape rather than pseudo-psychology

### 11.10.4 Intent drifts into genericity

Symptoms:

* Francis treats all tasks similarly
* same response style regardless of phase or objective
* little sense of strategic fit
* system feels capable but not deeply useful

Correction:

* re-center stage, tradeoff, risk, and definition-of-done awareness

### 11.10.5 Intent drifts into hidden influence

Symptoms:

* Francis acts under an inferred intent frame the user cannot inspect or correct
* strategic assumptions shape behavior invisibly
* user loses sense of why Francis is pushing a certain path

Correction:

* re-center visible steerability and intent-aware but inspectable behavior

---

## 11.11 Intent Doctrine Statement

If the Francis Intent Engine had to be defined in one doctrine sentence for builders, it would be this:

> The Intent Engine is the structured layer that models the real objective, stage, constraints, definition of done, active risks, blockers, tradeoff preferences, and preferred execution style of current work so Francis can plan, retrieve, communicate, and act in ways aligned to the purpose of the work rather than merely its surface request.

That sentence should be strong enough to reject shallow “goal awareness” architecture early.

---

## 11.12 Intent Acceptance Criteria

Section 11 is satisfied only if the roadmap, Mission layer, retrieval stack, planning behavior, Lens surfaces, and execution logic remain aligned to all of the following:

* Francis includes a real Intent Engine rather than relying only on prompts, Missions, or task phrasing
* the Intent Engine models at minimum current objective, current stage, constraints, definition of done, active risks, tradeoff preferences, current blockers, and preferred style of execution
* intent is treated as distinct from task, Mission, and capability
* intent materially changes planning, retrieval, communication, and execution behavior
* uncertainty about intent leads to calibrated caution, clarification, or narrower action rather than hidden strategic guessing
* Francis uses local evidence, Mission context, stage, and direct user framing to form intent rather than pseudo-psychological inference
* learned intent preferences may improve alignment but never create authority, approval bypass, or scope expansion
* Lens makes intent legible enough that users can feel and correct the current strategic frame
* briefings and handbacks are intent-aware rather than merely event-aware
* intent failure modes are explicitly recognized and corrected during buildout
* Francis never degrades into a task-literal, strategically shallow, vaguely “goal-oriented” system that solves the wrong problem with confidence




# 12.0 Sandboxed Executor: The Hands

Francis cannot become a true Operator Layer if it can only understand, suggest, summarize, and stage.

At some point, a real system must be able to **do**.

That is where the **Sandboxed Executor** comes in.

The Executor is the part of Francis that turns lawful intention into real bounded action across the user’s computing environment. It is the substrate through which Francis can:

* edit
* run
* validate
* navigate
* stage
* orchestrate
* prepare
* repair
* continue

But this is also one of the most sensitive layers in the entire architecture.

Because the moment Francis gains hands, everything becomes more consequential:

* governance becomes more real
* mistakes become more expensive
* receipts become more important
* scope enforcement becomes more critical
* user trust becomes more fragile
* interruption becomes more necessary
* verification becomes more non-negotiable

That is why the Executor cannot be built as “tool use plus vibes.”

It must be a **sandboxed, governable execution substrate**.

Not a raw command runner.
Not a permission sponge.
Not a shell with confidence.
Not a bag of dangerous tools with a thin approval wrapper.
Not a hidden automation engine.

It must be structured so that Francis can act powerfully **without becoming sloppy, invisible, over-authorized, or hard to stop**.

This section defines the doctrine of the Sandboxed Executor:

* why Francis needs real hands
* what the Executor is
* what kinds of tool categories it must support
* why sandboxing matters
* how scope, allowlists, budgets, leases, and idempotency shape safe execution
* how the Executor interacts with Pilot, Away, Missions, receipts, and verification
* what failure modes must be blocked before “doing” work turns Francis into a risk amplifier

The central question of this section is:

> How does Francis gain the ability to perform real work inside the user’s world without turning execution into hidden, brittle, over-scoped, or weakly governed action?

The answer is:

**a branch-safe, scope-enforced, receipted, budgeted, interruptible, verifiable execution substrate whose power is always downstream from mode, scope, policy, and approval.**

That is the Sandboxed Executor.

---

## 12.1 Why Francis Needs Real Hands

A system that can understand but not act remains structurally limited.

It can still be useful.
It can still be insightful.
It can still reduce cognitive burden.

But it cannot cross the threshold into real Operator Layer behavior unless it can carry actual work.

That means Francis must eventually be able to do more than:

* recommend
* summarize
* highlight
* explain
* draft abstractly

It must be able to:

* change files
* run commands
* validate outcomes
* stage branches
* move artifacts
* invoke approved tools
* prepare outputs
* operate workflows
* continue lawful progress

### 12.1.1 Advice is not enough

The user should not always have to manually translate Francis’s intelligence into execution.

That creates recurring friction like:

* copy-pasting suggestions
* manually running obvious validations
* repeating safe execution steps
* reconstructing branch workflows
* stitching together multi-step operations
* re-performing known sequences by hand

The Sandboxed Executor exists so Francis can carry those steps when lawfully authorized.

### 12.1.2 Takeover requires real hands

Pilot Mode cannot be real if it is just performative guidance.

If Francis is meant to “become my hands” in bounded scope, there must be a real execution substrate underneath that promise.

Otherwise Pilot becomes:

* narrated suggestion
* pseudo-control
* delegated intention without delegated action

That would weaken one of the most important product promises in the roadmap.

### 12.1.3 Away Mode needs lawful action, not just observation

Away Mode also depends on real execution, though more carefully bounded.

Without the Executor, Away Mode becomes mostly:

* summaries
* reminders
* passive alerts
* low-value continuity theater

With the Executor, Away can lawfully:

* validate
* prepare
* stage
* queue
* assemble
* continue safe steps
* generate real artifacts

That is much stronger.

---

## 12.2 What the Sandboxed Executor Is

The Sandboxed Executor is Francis’s governable action substrate.

It is the layer that takes approved, in-scope, mode-valid, policy-valid work plans and performs them through controlled tools under bounded conditions.

### 12.2.1 The Executor is not raw tool exposure

A weak design would say:

> give Francis shell, file, browser, and app control

That is not enough.

Raw tool exposure creates multiple problems:

* weak scope boundaries
* ambiguous authority
* poor auditability
* dangerous improvisation
* inconsistent safety rules
* unclear reversibility
* hard-to-reason-about side effects

The Executor must therefore be more than a toolbag.
It must be a *lawful operating substrate*.

### 12.2.2 Sandboxing is part of identity, not just implementation

The word “sandboxed” matters.

It means Francis should not interact with the environment through unrestricted open-ended access whenever safer constrained surfaces are possible.

The Executor should prefer:

* bounded file operations
* branch-first code workflows
* allowlisted shell surfaces
* scoped app automation
* controlled connectors
* policy-aware process control
* explicit verification hooks
* interruptible runs
* receipted outcomes

The point is not to weaken Francis.
It is to make power governable.

### 12.2.3 The Executor turns plans into bounded runs

The Executor is where a bounded plan becomes actual state change.

That means it sits downstream from:

* Intent
* Mission state
* mode
* scope
* policy
* approval state
* risk tier
* budgets
* stop conditions

It is not self-directed.
It is execution under law.

---

## 12.3 Toolbelt Categories

Francis needs a real toolbelt, but the categories must be deliberate.

The goal is not “maximum possible tooling.”
The goal is **sufficient lawful action surfaces**.

### 12.3.1 File operations

File tooling should support actions such as:

* read within scope
* write within scope
* patch within scope
* create files
* move or rename where allowed
* stage artifacts
* compare content
* preserve before/after state for receipts

File operations are foundational because so much real work becomes visible here.

### 12.3.2 Shell execution

Francis must be able to run commands where lawful, but shell access is one of the highest-risk surfaces.

Shell tooling should therefore be:

* allowlisted where possible
* scope-aware
* cwd-aware
* environment-aware
* policy-filtered
* receipted
* interruptible
* budgeted

Shell is powerful, but it must never become “do anything because the terminal can.”

### 12.3.3 Git operations

Git deserves first-class treatment.

Francis should support branch-safe workflows such as:

* status inspection
* diff creation
* branch creation
* checkout inside policy
* staging changes
* committing where allowed
* comparing branches
* preparing merge-ready handbacks

Git is too central to technical work to be a second-class tool.

### 12.3.4 Process control

Francis may need to:

* launch processes
* monitor them
* stop them
* check status
* detect hangs
* capture exit information
* preserve logs
* avoid collisions with existing work

This must remain bounded and inspectable.

### 12.3.5 Browser and app automation

Some work surfaces live in applications or browsers rather than files and shell.

Francis may therefore need scoped abilities like:

* navigate approved surfaces
* click or fill in approved contexts
* inspect specific page or UI states
* perform bounded workflows
* capture outcome evidence
* pause for approval at sensitive steps

This area requires especially strong governance because it feels most like “acting as the user.”

### 12.3.6 Parsing and extraction

Francis should be able to use tools for:

* structured parsing
* format conversion
* extraction from logs
* report preparation
* tabular reshaping
* evidence collection
* content comparison

These are often low-risk but high-value tools.

### 12.3.7 Connectors

Over time, Francis should be able to invoke approved external or domain connectors under the same law as local tools.

Examples include:

* ticket systems
* repo hosts
* calendars
* inventory systems
* CRM systems
* business data connectors
* household or edge-system interfaces

Connectors should not bypass executor governance just because they are “API-based.”

---

## 12.4 Why Sandboxing Matters

Execution without sandboxing is one of the fastest ways to turn intelligence into operational danger.

### 12.4.1 Sandboxing constrains blast radius

A sandboxed executor limits what can go wrong if:

* Francis misinterprets intent
* telemetry is noisy
* a capability is buggy
* a prompt or artifact is adversarial
* a run gets interrupted awkwardly
* a retry path misbehaves
* a tool result is misleading

This does not eliminate all risk.
It reduces the blast radius.

### 12.4.2 Sandboxing improves reasonability

A bounded action surface is easier to reason about than unrestricted environment access.

It becomes easier to answer:

* what can Francis actually do here?
* what can it not do?
* which actions are allowed in this mode?
* how is this tool constrained?
* what verification exists?
* what evidence will be produced?

That supports both developers and users.

### 12.4.3 Sandboxing protects category integrity

If Francis gains execution through a loose, all-powerful substrate, it will tend to drift toward:

* unsafe agent
* brittle automation engine
* hidden-control tool
* shell wrapper with branding
* high-trust system with low-trust internals

Sandboxing is how execution stays worthy of the product claim.

---

## 12.5 Scope Enforcement in the Executor

Scope must not stop at the UI or planning layer.

It must be enforced at the execution substrate itself.

### 12.5.1 The Executor must know active scope

Every execution run should carry active scope explicitly, including things like:

* allowed directories
* allowed repo boundaries
* allowed app surfaces
* allowed node or connector surfaces
* disallowed adjacent areas
* mission linkage if relevant

The Executor should not have to guess where it may act.

### 12.5.2 Scope must block action, not just warn about it

A weak executor might log that something was “out of scope” but still allow it in practice.

That is not enough.

Scope enforcement should be real enough to prevent:

* file mutation outside declared boundaries
* cross-project action without explicit inclusion
* unrelated connector access
* app automation outside current allowed window or surface
* shell actions that target disallowed paths or environments

Scope must be structural.

### 12.5.3 Scope should travel with receipts

Execution receipts should always preserve enough scope context to answer:

* where was Francis allowed to act?
* where did it actually act?
* what boundary did this run belong to?
* which mission or mode authorized that boundary?

This matters for audit, trust, and debugging.

---

## 12.6 Allowlists, Denylists, and Policy Filters

The Sandboxed Executor should not operate on a naive “all tools always available” model.

### 12.6.1 Allowlists

Where possible, Francis should prefer allowlisted execution classes, commands, or surfaces.

This may include:

* known safe commands
* approved scripts
* approved repos
* approved app workflows
* approved connector operations
* safe transformation functions
* safe report-generation paths

Allowlists reduce interpretive risk.

### 12.6.2 Denylists are not enough

A denylist-only posture is weak because it assumes the dangerous actions are fully known and enumerable.

Francis should not rely only on:

* “block these few bad commands”
* “filter these obvious dangerous patterns”
* “trust the rest”

That leaves too much uncontrolled surface area.

### 12.6.3 Policy filters must be mode-aware and risk-aware

What is allowed may differ based on:

* Observe vs Assist vs Pilot vs Away
* current risk tier
* mission type
* connector trust
* node trust
* user approval state
* environment type

Policy filtering must therefore be dynamic enough to reflect context, but stable enough to remain predictable.

---

## 12.7 Branch-First and Reversible-by-Default

For code and many structured artifact workflows, Francis should prefer safe intermediate surfaces over direct irreversible mutation.

### 12.7.1 Branch-first as operational default

For technical work, Francis should usually prefer:

* create branch
* stage changes there
* validate there
* hand back there
* request approval before sensitive merge or promotion

This supports:

* reviewability
* rollback
* reduced blast radius
* user trust
* clearer receipts

Branch-first is one of the most important execution habits in the whole system.

### 12.7.2 Reversible where possible

Not all actions are reversible, but the Executor should prefer reversible forms when available.

That may include:

* writing staged files instead of overwriting live state
* preserving before/after snapshots
* creating drafts instead of final sends
* using preview or simulation modes
* storing patch artifacts
* separating prepare from promote

This creates safer handoff points.

### 12.7.3 The user should feel Francis is careful with their world

Execution should feel:

* calm
* precise
* reviewable
* minimally destructive
* respectful of existing state
* aligned with user standards

Branch-first and reversibility are part of that emotional contract.

---

## 12.8 Resource Budgets, Leases, and Idempotency

A mature Executor must be bounded not only by scope and tool permissions, but by runtime discipline.

### 12.8.1 Resource budgets

Each run should carry budgets such as:

* max runtime
* max process count
* max retries
* max file mutation count
* max connector operations
* max token or compute allowance where relevant
* max concurrent actions

This prevents hidden sprawl and resource hunger.

### 12.8.2 Leases

Execution authority should often be lease-based rather than indefinite.

A lease means:

* this authority is valid for this run or window
* it expires
* it must be renewed by policy or user action
* it does not silently persist forever

Leases help prevent sticky delegated power.

### 12.8.3 Idempotency

The Executor should prefer actions that can be safely retried or recognized as already done where appropriate.

This matters because real environments include:

* interrupted runs
* retries
* transient failures
* node restarts
* user cancellations
* handoffs

Idempotency helps prevent duplicated mutation and confusing side effects.

---

## 12.9 Verification Hooks

The Sandboxed Executor must not only act.
It must support proving what happened.

### 12.9.1 Execution is not completion

Running an action does not mean the intended state change succeeded.

The Executor should therefore expose verification hooks such as:

* file existence or content checks
* diff confirmation
* test execution
* build or lint status
* connector response validation
* process exit validation
* UI state re-checks where applicable
* artifact generation confirmation

This is how Francis avoids claiming more than it earned.

### 12.9.2 Verification should be close to the action

Verification should happen near enough to execution that the relationship remains clear.

A strong run should preserve:

* what was attempted
* what was checked
* what passed
* what failed
* what remains uncertain

This is stronger than vague downstream summaries.

### 12.9.3 Verification failures should shape next state

If verification fails, the run should not be able to quietly narrate success.

It should instead move into lawful next states such as:

* retry candidate
* blocked
* approval needed
* deadletter
* incident
* staged but unverified
* handback with uncertainty

This is critical for trust.

---

## 12.10 Receipts and Execution Truth

The Sandboxed Executor is one of the biggest producers of receipts in the whole system.

### 12.10.1 Every meaningful execution step should leave trace

Execution receipts should capture as appropriate:

* run_id
* action summary
* tool used
* scope
* authority basis
* mode
* mission linkage
* timestamps
* artifacts changed
* verification outcome
* errors or warnings
* whether rollback or staged recovery exists

This makes the hands auditable.

### 12.10.2 Receipts should support handback

At the end of a meaningful execution flow, the user should be able to understand:

* what Francis did
* what changed
* what passed
* what failed
* what still needs approval
* what branch or artifact to inspect
* what next move Francis recommends

That requires execution receipts with enough structure to support calm handback.

### 12.10.3 Execution receipts are part of user trust

A system that can act without leaving strong receipts will eventually feel untrustworthy, no matter how impressive it is when things go well.

The user must never feel that Francis’s hands are invisible.

---

## 12.11 The User Experience of the Executor

The Sandboxed Executor is backend architecture, but its feel matters enormously.

### 12.11.1 What good execution feels like

Good Francis execution should feel:

* calm
* precise
* bounded
* branch-safe
* inspectable
* interruptible
* not overeager
* not sloppy
* serious
* trustworthy

The user should feel:

> Francis is doing real work, but doing it under discipline.

### 12.11.2 What bad execution feels like

Bad execution feels like:

* mysterious
* too broad
* too fast in the wrong way
* hard to follow
* hard to stop
* risky without enough explanation
* brittle under failure
* casually destructive
* vague about what actually changed

Francis must never feel like “a terminal with confidence.”

### 12.11.3 Execution should increase trust, not just power

A weak design often assumes that more action will automatically impress the user.

That is not enough.

A mature design understands that *how* Francis acts is just as important as *what* it can do.

The Executor must therefore make the system feel more trustworthy as it becomes more capable.

---

## 12.12 Executor Across the Modes

The same execution substrate should behave differently depending on legal mode.

### 12.12.1 Observe and the Executor

Observe should not perform mutation.

But the Executor may still support Observe-mode operations such as:

* safe read actions
* state checks
* validation reads
* structured extraction
* environment inspection
* receipt preparation for non-mutating runs

Observe still uses the substrate, but in non-mutating ways.

### 12.12.2 Assist and the Executor

Assist may use the Executor for preparation without full application.

Examples include:

* draft patch generation
* branch staging
* simulation
* dry-run validation
* artifact prep
* report assembly
* diff preview

Assist should make the hands visible without taking the wheel fully.

### 12.12.3 Pilot and the Executor

Pilot is where the Executor is most fully expressed.

Pilot may use the substrate for:

* file mutation in scope
* command execution
* validation
* branch workflows
* structured app actions
* multi-step bounded flows
* real-time or near-real-time receipt generation

But even in Pilot, all the laws still hold.

### 12.12.4 Away and the Executor

Away may use the Executor for safe bounded continuation such as:

* validation runs
* artifact preparation
* report generation
* staging
* low-risk maintenance actions
* queue preparation
* continuity-preserving housekeeping

Away must not silently turn the Executor into a broad background actor.

---

## 12.13 Executor Failure Modes

This section must explicitly define the ways the Executor can go wrong.

### 12.13.1 Executor drifts into raw shell wrapper

Symptoms:

* too much dependence on unconstrained shell
* weak tool abstraction
* poor safety boundaries
* execution feels improvised rather than governed

Correction:

* re-center sandboxing, tool categories, policy filters, and bounded execution surfaces

### 12.13.2 Executor drifts into hidden-control automation

Symptoms:

* action happens with weak visibility
* pilot authority unclear
* receipts weak or delayed
* user cannot easily inspect or stop the run

Correction:

* re-center visible authority, receipts, and interruptibility

### 12.13.3 Executor drifts into over-broad scope

Symptoms:

* adjacent repos touched
* unrelated files included
* app actions exceed declared boundary
* connectors used outside explicit inclusion

Correction:

* re-center substrate-level scope enforcement

### 12.13.4 Executor drifts into brittle mutation

Symptoms:

* direct irreversible edits by default
* poor branch discipline
* weak rollback posture
* fragile retry behavior
* verification afterthoughts

Correction:

* re-center branch-first, reversibility, idempotency, and verification hooks

### 12.13.5 Executor drifts into power without proof

Symptoms:

* actions taken but poorly evidenced
* success inferred from attempt
* completion claims weakly verified
* handbacks vague about actual changes

Correction:

* re-center verification, receipts, and claim discipline

---

## 12.14 Executor Doctrine Statement

If the Francis Sandboxed Executor had to be defined in one doctrine sentence for builders, it would be this:

> The Sandboxed Executor is Francis’s governable action substrate, providing scoped, policy-filtered, branch-safe, allowlisted, budgeted, interruptible, receipted, and verifiable execution surfaces through which Francis can perform real work without collapsing into raw shell exposure, hidden automation, weakly bounded mutation, or power without proof.

That sentence should be strong enough to reject bad execution architecture early.

---

## 12.15 Executor Acceptance Criteria

Section 12 is satisfied only if the roadmap, execution substrate, mode system, policy engine, receipt layer, and user experience remain aligned to all of the following:

* Francis has a real execution substrate rather than relying only on recommendation or staging theater
* the Executor is treated as sandboxed and governable rather than raw unrestricted tool exposure
* core toolbelt categories include file operations, shell execution, git operations, process control, browser/app automation, parsing/extraction, and governed connectors
* scope is enforced at the execution substrate itself, not merely in UI or planning layers
* allowlists, policy filters, and mode-aware execution constraints govern tool use
* branch-first and reversible-by-default patterns are preferred where applicable
* runs are bounded by resource budgets, leases, idempotency discipline, and explicit stop conditions
* verification hooks are part of execution, not an afterthought
* every meaningful execution step is receipted strongly enough for audit, handback, and trust
* the Executor behaves differently and lawfully across Observe, Assist, Pilot, and Away
* execution feels calm, precise, inspectable, and interruptible rather than mysterious, overeager, or casually destructive
* executor failure modes are explicitly recognized and corrected during buildout
* Francis never degrades into a raw shell wrapper, hidden-control automation engine, or weakly bounded mutation layer under the excuse of becoming more capable

# 13.0 Takeover: “Become My Hands”

This is one of the most important threshold features in all of Francis.

It is the moment where the system stops feeling merely assistive and starts feeling **operationally real**.

Many products can suggest.
Many products can summarize.
Many products can generate.
Some products can even execute in narrow ways.

But very few can credibly offer the user this experience:

> Let me take the wheel for a bounded moment.
> I will act as your hands inside declared scope.
> You will remain the authority.
> You can stop me instantly.
> I will hand back with receipts.

That is **Takeover**.

Takeover is not a gimmick.
It is not theatrical autopilot.
It is not broad remote control.
It is not hidden automation disguised as convenience.
It is not “agent mode” with vague appetite and weak boundaries.

Takeover is the governed ritual by which Francis is temporarily allowed to execute work **as the user’s hands** inside explicit scope, under visible authority, with immediate interruptibility and full handback discipline.

This is the “holy shit” feature because it collapses one of the biggest gaps in computing:

* the gap between understanding and doing
* the gap between assistance and execution
* the gap between recommending and carrying
* the gap between intent and motion

But precisely because it is so powerful, it must be one of the most governed surfaces in the entire architecture.

If Takeover is loose, Francis becomes frightening.
If Takeover is hidden, Francis becomes untrustworthy.
If Takeover is vague, Francis becomes unsafe.
If Takeover is weakly receipted, Francis becomes impossible to audit.
If Takeover is not interruptible, Francis becomes unacceptable.

This section defines the doctrine of Takeover:

* what Takeover is
* why it matters
* what it must and must not mean
* how control transfer should work
* how Pilot authority becomes visible
* how live execution should feel
* what defaults must keep it safe
* how handback must happen
* what failure modes must be prevented before this feature corrupts the entire trust model

The central question of this section is:

> How can Francis become the user’s hands for real work in a way that feels extraordinary in capability but unwavering in governance?

The answer is:

**explicit control transfer, visible Pilot authority, bounded scope, live action visibility, instant revocation, branch-safe execution, verify-before-claim discipline, and handback with receipts.**

That is Takeover.

---

## 13.1 Why Takeover Matters

Takeover matters because it is where Francis’s promise becomes unmistakably real.

If Francis can understand context, carry Missions, build continuity, learn from Apprenticeship, and operate a Sandboxed Executor—but still cannot truly step in and carry bounded work at the user’s command—then the system remains one layer short of its most important operational threshold.

Takeover is that threshold.

### 13.1.1 Takeover closes the last-mile gap

One of the most common forms of friction in modern computing is the “last mile” problem:

* the system knows what should be done
* the user knows the system knows
* but the user still has to manually perform the exact thing

That last mile is often where momentum dies.

Takeover closes it.

It allows the user to move from:

* “show me what to do”

to:

* “do it with my authority inside this declared boundary while I watch or while I focus elsewhere for a moment”

That is a major leap in practical value.

### 13.1.2 Takeover makes delegation feel literal

Many systems talk about delegation metaphorically.

Takeover makes it literal.

The user is not merely delegating analysis.
Not merely delegating drafts.
Not merely delegating plans.

They are delegating **execution authority inside controlled boundaries**.

That is one of the strongest realizations of the Operator Layer concept in the entire product.

### 13.1.3 Takeover reveals whether Francis is truly governed

A weak system can appear safe while it only suggests.

The real test comes when it can act.

Takeover is therefore one of the places where governance stops being theory and becomes product truth.

If Francis can:

* take control visibly
* act only within scope
* remain interruptible
* preserve receipts
* hand back cleanly
* avoid hidden overreach

then the architecture is probably real.

If it cannot, then the governance story is not yet mature enough.

---

## 13.2 What Takeover Is

Takeover is the explicit, temporary delegation of bounded execution authority to Francis so it can act as the user’s hands inside declared scope.

That sentence must remain precise.

### 13.2.1 Takeover is temporary

Takeover is not a permanent ambient execution state.

It is a temporary control transfer under current:

* mode
* scope
* approvals
* policy
* mission context
* risk posture
* execution budgets

This keeps it lawful and emotionally manageable.

### 13.2.2 Takeover is bounded

Takeover must always be bounded by:

* explicit scope
* explicit control mode
* explicit stop conditions
* current policies
* current tool permissions
* current node and connector trust state
* active execution budget
* immediate revocation rights

Takeover is not “Francis can now do whatever it thinks is best.”

### 13.2.3 Takeover is execution, not vague autonomy

Takeover means real action.

It means Francis may:

* navigate approved work surfaces
* edit in scope
* run commands in scope
* stage branches
* validate outputs
* click through bounded workflows
* prepare artifacts
* advance defined mission steps
* pause at approval boundaries
* hand back with proof

It is not merely stronger Assist Mode language.

### 13.2.4 Takeover is still subordinate power

Even at maximum capability within Takeover, Francis remains subordinate.

The user still owns:

* the mission
* the scope
* the right to interrupt
* the right to narrow or stop
* the right to inspect
* the right to deny follow-up action
* the right to refuse continuation
* the right to review before the next consequential step

Takeover is delegated power, not transferred sovereignty.

---

## 13.3 What Takeover Is Not

This section must be especially crisp about anti-goals.

Takeover is not:

* a hidden automation layer
* broad remote-control AI
* a freeform agent unleashed in the environment
* a standing power grant that lingers invisibly
* a theatrical “autopilot” mode with weak receipt discipline
* a way to bypass approvals because the user “already said go”
* an excuse for scope expansion
* a replacement for handback and review
* an “agent mode” brand slapped onto unsafe execution

### 13.3.1 Not implicit control

Francis must never silently cross from Assist into Takeover because:

* the next step seemed obvious
* the user sounded urgent
* similar approvals happened before
* the system had strong confidence
* the user left temporarily
* the Mission is active

Control transfer must be real and explicit.

### 13.3.2 Not endless delegation

Takeover must not become an indefinite state that continues by inertia.

If authority persists, the user must understand:

* why it persists
* how long it persists
* what it currently covers
* how to revoke it
* what still remains authorized
* what would require renewed approval

Sticky invisible Pilot authority is forbidden.

### 13.3.3 Not a law bypass

Takeover does not override:

* policy
* scope
* risk constraints
* verification requirements
* receipt obligations
* kill switch availability
* approval boundaries for especially sensitive steps if policy requires them

Takeover makes Francis stronger.
It does not remove the law.

---

## 13.4 The Control Transfer Ritual

Takeover must not begin casually.

It should begin through a visible, legible, structured control transfer ritual.

This ritual matters because the transition from “advising” to “acting” is one of the most sensitive state changes in the entire product.

### 13.4.1 Step 1 — User requests takeover

The user initiates Takeover directly.

This may happen through:

* a command
* a Lens action
* a mission surface action
* a contextual action chip
* a command palette invocation
* a structured handoff flow

The key point is that the user requests it.

### 13.4.2 Step 2 — Francis presents scope and authority

Before active execution begins, Francis must show the user what the takeover will cover.

This should include things like:

* active scope
* intended objective
* likely action class
* affected surfaces
* risk tier or sensitivity cues
* current policy constraints
* whether certain approvals may still pause the flow
* whether branch-first handling applies
* whether handback will occur after a bounded segment

This is the moment where control becomes legible.

### 13.4.3 Step 3 — User confirms

Takeover should require explicit user confirmation before Pilot execution begins.

The confirmation moment should make clear:

* I understand Francis is about to act
* I understand where it may act
* I understand what kind of action it may take
* I know how to stop it
* I know this is bounded, not blanket authority

This is not bureaucratic fluff. It is trust architecture.

### 13.4.4 Step 4 — Pilot Mode becomes visibly active

The moment Takeover begins, Pilot visibility must become unmistakable.

There should be no ambiguity.

The user should never wonder:

* is Francis just suggesting?
* is Francis staging?
* is Francis actually acting right now?

Pilot visibility is one of the most identity-critical surfaces in the whole product.

### 13.4.5 Step 5 — Francis executes with live action visibility

During Takeover, Francis should act with visible or inspectable action flow.

The user should be able to understand:

* what step is underway
* what changed
* what is being validated
* what branch or artifact is active
* whether a pause or approval is imminent
* whether risk or uncertainty increased
* where interruption is available

This makes power feel lawful.

### 13.4.6 Step 6 — Francis hands back with receipts

Takeover must end with a real handback, not merely with silence.

The user should receive:

* summary
* changed artifacts or branch
* validation outcomes
* what remains uncertain
* what is done
* what still needs review or approval
* recommended next move

The ritual ends with restored clarity.

---

## 13.5 Pilot Mode as the Legal Surface of Takeover

Takeover and Pilot are deeply linked.

Pilot Mode is the legal mode in which Takeover lives.

### 13.5.1 Pilot is not just a UI badge

Pilot must be a real authority state.

That means when Pilot is active, the system must actually behave differently at the execution and governance layers, not merely look different.

Pilot should affect:

* tool permissions
* action availability
* receipt expectations
* handback logic
* interruption semantics
* live run visibility
* scope enforcement posture
* approval checkpoints if needed

### 13.5.2 Pilot must be visible at all times during Takeover

This rule is non-negotiable.

If Francis currently holds live delegated execution authority, that should be visible enough that the user cannot miss it without extraordinary inattention.

The product must never normalize invisible Pilot.

### 13.5.3 Pilot must end clearly

The end of Pilot should also be obvious.

The user should be able to tell when:

* Francis is no longer acting
* live delegated authority has ended
* remaining work has shifted back to Assist or awaiting approval
* any future continuation would require a new bounded run or renewed authority

Sticky ambiguity at the end of Takeover is almost as bad as ambiguity at the start.

---

## 13.6 Live Execution During Takeover

Takeover should not feel like Francis disappears into a black box and later returns with claims.

It should feel like live, governed carrying of work.

### 13.6.1 Live action feed matters

A live action feed or equivalent visibility surface is important because it lets the user see:

* action sequence
* current step
* progress against bounded objective
* pauses
* verification points
* branching decisions
* encountered blockers
* pending approvals

This is not only useful. It is trust-building.

### 13.6.2 Live execution should feel calm

The visual and interaction style of live Takeover matters.

It should not feel like:

* frantic robotic movement
* flashy control theater
* AI doing inscrutable magic
* endless logs with no meaning
* noisy animations trying to simulate intelligence

It should feel:

* steady
* legible
* serious
* bounded
* competent
* interruptible

### 13.6.3 Live execution should expose risk changes

If the situation changes during Takeover, the user should not be kept in the dark.

Examples include:

* a new dependency appears
* validation fails
* risk tier rises
* scope boundary is reached
* policy blocks the next step
* new approval is needed
* the action is less certain than expected

This is how Francis remains truthful under motion.

---

## 13.7 Default Safety Posture for Takeover

Takeover must launch from conservative defaults.

The whole architecture depends on this.

### 13.7.1 Branch-first by default

For code and many structured workflows, Takeover should prefer branch-first behavior.

This means:

* avoid touching protected branches directly unless explicitly allowed
* isolate changes
* validate on branch
* preserve reviewability
* make handback easier and safer

Branch-first is one of the strongest safety defaults in Takeover.

### 13.7.2 Verify-before-claim by default

Takeover must not narrate confidence as if it were completion.

It should prefer:

* run
* check
* verify
* receipt
* then claim

This matters even more in Takeover because the user may grant strong trust during the flow.

### 13.7.3 No risky commands without required approval

Pilot authority is not a substitute for policy.

If an action crosses a defined threshold, Francis should still:

* pause
* explain
* request approval if policy requires
* preserve state
* continue only if lawfully authorized

Takeover does not erase high-risk boundaries.

### 13.7.4 No hidden side work

Takeover should not quietly spawn unrelated effort in the background.

The user should not later discover that while Francis was “fixing this one thing” it also:

* touched adjacent systems
* staged unrelated changes
* expanded the mission
* explored new repos
* opened side investigations
* changed standing configuration elsewhere

Takeover must stay on the declared line.

---

## 13.8 Interruptibility During Takeover

If Francis becomes the user’s hands, then stopping those hands must always remain immediate and reliable.

This is one of the emotional and technical pillars of the feature.

### 13.8.1 The user must always be able to stop

During Takeover, the user must be able to:

* pause
* stop current run
* revoke delegated authority
* narrow scope
* deny the next step
* kill active execution
* collapse back to a safe state

These controls should not be buried.

### 13.8.2 Panic must be immediate

Takeover is one of the strongest places where a panic/kill path matters.

The user must never feel trapped behind:

* a long-running action
* an app automation sequence
* a shell process
* a cascading toolchain
* an unclear “thinking” state that still holds authority

The psychological certainty that “I can stop this now” is part of what makes deep delegation possible.

### 13.8.3 Stop should preserve truth

Interrupting Takeover should not destroy understanding.

Receipts should still show:

* what had begun
* what completed
* what did not complete
* what was paused
* what may need cleanup
* what authority was revoked
* what the next safe state is

Interruptibility must not come at the cost of legibility.

---

## 13.9 Handback: Returning Control with Clarity

Takeover is not complete until control is handed back cleanly.

Handback is one of the most important and underdesigned parts of the whole concept.

### 13.9.1 Handback is not just a summary

A weak system ends execution and says something vague like:

* done
* fixed it
* should be good now
* I handled it

That is not enough.

A real handback must return the user to authority with clear operational understanding.

### 13.9.2 What handback must include

At the end of meaningful Takeover, Francis should provide:

* summary of what was attempted
* summary of what changed
* branch or artifact location
* validation or test results
* receipts
* remaining uncertainty
* anything blocked or awaiting approval
* recommended next step

This makes the handback actionable and reviewable.

### 13.9.3 Handback restores human command context

After Takeover, the user should feel:

* I know what happened
* I know what changed
* I know what is verified
* I know what is still uncertain
* I know what I need to inspect next
* I know Francis is no longer holding invisible authority

This is how control returns with dignity.

---

## 13.10 Takeover and Missions

Takeover should usually exist in relationship to a Mission or a clearly bounded local objective.

### 13.10.1 Takeover should advance something real

Takeover should not be freeform performance.

It should usually connect to:

* an active Mission
* a bounded execution objective
* a specific blocker
* a specific validation flow
* a specific remediation or preparation need

This helps preserve meaning and receipts.

### 13.10.2 Mission linkage improves handback

When Takeover links back to a Mission, Francis can provide stronger handback such as:

* this advanced Mission X
* this unblocked step Y
* this staged the next review point
* this moved the Mission into approval-waiting
* this failed verification and left Mission status blocked

That is much stronger than isolated run narration.

### 13.10.3 Takeover must not let missions widen scope

Even if a Mission is broad, a specific Takeover run must still be explicitly scoped.

Mission relevance does not create blanket Takeover authority.

---

## 13.11 Takeover and Trust Calibration

Takeover is one of the strongest places where trust calibration must remain disciplined.

### 13.11.1 Francis must show uncertainty during Takeover

If something becomes less certain than expected, Francis must not hide that behind smooth motion.

Examples:

* unclear validation result
* partial success
* ambiguous environment state
* probable but unconfirmed fix
* tool output that may not match reality
* action blocked by unexpected condition

Takeover should remain truthful under motion.

### 13.11.2 Partial progress is still valid if named honestly

Not every Takeover run ends in total completion.

A mature system should be able to say:

* I advanced this part
* this part remains blocked
* this part was staged but not promoted
* this is likely fixed but not confirmed
* this needs your review
* this hit policy or approval boundary

That honesty is part of trust.

### 13.11.3 Extraordinary capability must not produce fake confidence

Takeover may feel magical when it works well.

That feeling must come from real capability under governance, not from overclaiming.

---

## 13.12 The User Experience of Takeover

The Takeover experience must feel extraordinary for the right reasons.

### 13.12.1 What good Takeover feels like

Good Takeover should make the user feel:

* I just handed Francis a bounded piece of real work
* it understood the objective
* it stayed inside the line
* it acted competently
* I could see or inspect what was happening
* I could have stopped it at any point
* I know what it changed
* I trust the handback

This is where Francis starts to feel unlike ordinary AI products.

### 13.12.2 What bad Takeover feels like

Bad Takeover feels like:

* unclear authority
* mysterious movement
* too much confidence
* too little visibility
* hard-to-stop execution
* scope drift
* handback vagueness
* more anxiety than relief

Francis must never let the user feel that it “took over” in the wrong sense.

### 13.12.3 Takeover should increase trust in proportion to power

The stronger Francis becomes during Takeover, the more lawful it should feel.

That is one of the deepest design goals of the whole roadmap.

---

## 13.13 Takeover Failure Modes

This section must explicitly name the ways Takeover can go wrong.

### 13.13.1 Takeover drifts into implicit execution

Symptoms:

* Assist starts acting like Pilot
* “obvious next steps” happen without real transfer
* user feels Francis moved from suggestion to action too casually

Correction:

* re-center explicit control transfer ritual and visible Pilot activation

### 13.13.2 Takeover drifts into hidden control

Symptoms:

* Pilot state is not obvious
* actions happen without good live visibility
* user cannot tell when delegated execution is active

Correction:

* re-center visible Pilot indicator, action feed, and inspectable live state

### 13.13.3 Takeover drifts into sticky authority

Symptoms:

* Pilot seems to remain active too long
* authority lingers after bounded run
* future actions happen under unclear carryover power

Correction:

* re-center leases, bounded runs, and clear Pilot end state

### 13.13.4 Takeover drifts into scope creep

Symptoms:

* adjacent files, systems, or actions get pulled in
* mission relevance becomes excuse for broader action
* Francis “helps” beyond the declared line

Correction:

* re-center explicit scope contract and substrate-level enforcement

### 13.13.5 Takeover drifts into black-box execution

Symptoms:

* user sees little while Francis acts
* handback is vague
* receipts are weak
* confidence is high but proof is thin

Correction:

* re-center live action visibility, receipts, verification, and disciplined handback

### 13.13.6 Takeover drifts into unsafe bravado

Symptoms:

* risky commands attempted too casually
* verification skipped
* branch safety ignored
* policy treated as friction instead of law

Correction:

* re-center conservative defaults, policy enforcement, and verify-before-claim posture

---

## 13.14 Takeover Doctrine Statement

If Francis Takeover had to be defined in one doctrine sentence for builders, it would be this:

> Takeover is the explicit, temporary, scope-bound transfer of delegated execution authority to Francis under visible Pilot Mode, live action visibility, conservative safety defaults, instant revocability, verification discipline, and receipt-backed handback so Francis can become the user’s hands without becoming hidden control, sticky authority, scope drift, or black-box automation.

That sentence should be strong enough to reject weak Pilot architecture early.

---

## 13.15 Takeover Acceptance Criteria

Section 13 is satisfied only if the roadmap, Pilot surfaces, execution substrate, Lens rendering, handback logic, and governance model remain aligned to all of the following:

* Takeover is treated as explicit delegated execution, not stronger Assist rhetoric
* control transfer follows a real ritual: request, scope display, confirmation, visible Pilot activation, bounded execution, and clean handback
* Pilot Mode is a real legal execution state, not merely a UI label
* Pilot visibility is unmistakable while Takeover is active
* live action visibility or equivalent inspectable flow makes execution feel lawful rather than black-box
* Takeover runs remain bounded by scope, policy, approvals, budgets, and stop conditions
* branch-first, verify-before-claim, and no-hidden-side-work defaults shape normal Takeover behavior
* interruption, panic, stop, revoke, and scope narrowing are always available and reliable during Takeover
* handback includes summary, changed artifacts or branch, validation outcomes, receipts, remaining uncertainty, and next recommendations
* Takeover integrates with Missions without allowing mission relevance to become blanket authority
* trust calibration remains active during Takeover; uncertainty and partial completion are surfaced honestly
* Takeover failure modes are explicitly recognized and corrected during buildout
* Francis never degrades into implicit execution, hidden Pilot, sticky authority, scope creep, or black-box control under the excuse of becoming more powerful

# 14.0 Away Mode: Night Shift Operator

One of the deepest promises of Francis is not merely that it can help while the user is present.

It is that lawful momentum can continue **when the user is away**.

That is the promise of **Away Mode**.

Away Mode is where Francis stops being only a synchronous assistant and becomes a true continuity-bearing operator. It is where the machine no longer falls fully back to zero the moment the human steps away. It is where progress can survive absence. It is where the environment can still be tended, prepared, monitored, staged, and advanced inside strict boundaries while the user is sleeping, working elsewhere, in transit, or simply not actively in front of the machine.

But this is also one of the most dangerous surfaces in the entire architecture.

Because “keep working while I’m away” can easily degrade into the worst version of AI autonomy:

* hidden background action
* silent scope drift
* weak approvals
* self-justifying continuation
* surveillance-like sensing
* black-box mutation
* sticky authority
* impressive-sounding chaos

That must never happen.

Away Mode is not general autonomous freedom.
It is not a vague “agent keeps going.”
It is not permission through absence.
It is not an excuse to weaken governance because the user is not watching in real time.

Away Mode is a **bounded, event-driven, schedule-aware, policy-constrained continuity mode** in which Francis may preserve momentum, advance safe work, stage decisions, and prepare value for the user’s return without ever pretending absence is sovereignty transfer.

This section defines the doctrine of Away Mode:

* why Away Mode matters
* what it is and is not
* what kinds of work it may perform
* what kinds of work it must not perform by default
* how schedules, events, missions, budgets, and approvals shape it
* how the user should experience re-entry
* how Away Mode interacts with telemetry, missions, receipts, and trust calibration
* what failure modes must be blocked before “night shift” turns into “runaway background actor”

The central question of this section is:

> How can Francis continue creating real value while the user is away without converting absence into hidden power, broad authority, or trust erosion?

The answer is:

**bounded, mission-aware, event-driven, low-surprise continuation that stages, prepares, validates, monitors, and advances only what is lawful and safe under explicit Away rules, then returns the user to a clear, receipted shift report on re-entry.**

That is Away Mode.

---

## 14.1 Why Away Mode Matters

If Francis only creates value while the user is actively present, then it remains structurally limited.

It may still be useful.
It may still be better than a chat tool.
It may still feel intelligent.

But it will still leave one major burden almost entirely on the user:

> all meaningful momentum pauses when I do.

A real Operator Layer should reduce that burden.

### 14.1.1 Human attention is discontinuous

Real work is constantly interrupted by:

* sleep
* meetings
* travel
* context switching
* waiting on approvals
* waiting on dependencies
* leaving one device for another
* simply not wanting to babysit the machine continuously

If all continuity depends on the user’s active presence, then progress remains fragile.

Away Mode exists to make continuity durable.

### 14.1.2 Valuable work exists that does not require live supervision

There are many classes of work that can create real value without requiring the user to watch every second.

Examples include:

* validation
* monitoring
* evidence gathering
* report assembly
* artifact staging
* queue preparation
* safe low-risk housekeeping
* mission progression inside approved boundaries
* anomaly detection
* organizing pending approvals
* preparing decision surfaces

Away Mode captures that value.

### 14.1.3 Away Mode turns Francis into a continuity engine

A system becomes much more real when the user can return and feel:

* progress happened
* order was preserved
* friction was reduced
* decisions were prepared
* issues were surfaced honestly
* nothing crossed the line without permission
* I am stepping back into a living continuity surface, not a reset point

That is one of the strongest emotional and practical benefits Francis can offer.

---

## 14.2 What Away Mode Is

Away Mode is the bounded continuity mode in which Francis may continue specific lawful work while the user is not actively present.

This definition must stay strict.

### 14.2.1 Away Mode is absence-aware, not permission-expanding

The fact that the user is absent does **not** mean Francis has more authority.

This must remain one of the strongest rules in the section.

Absence may change:

* what communication style is appropriate
* whether live confirmation is immediately available
* what kind of shift report is needed on return
* which safe tasks are worth continuing asynchronously

But it does not automatically expand:

* scope
* approval rights
* policy exceptions
* risk thresholds
* destructive authority
* promotion rights

Away is continuity under law, not freedom under darkness.

### 14.2.2 Away Mode is not generic autonomy

Away Mode should not be confused with “just let the agent keep going.”

It must remain:

* mission-aware
* policy-constrained
* event-driven
* budgeted
* receipted
* interruptible where infrastructure allows
* reviewable on return
* explicit in what kinds of work it may continue

This keeps the feature from collapsing into runaway background behavior.

### 14.2.3 Away Mode is a continuity service

The deepest value of Away Mode is not dramatic action.

It is continuity.

That continuity may take forms such as:

* preserving work state
* advancing safe next steps
* monitoring known edges
* preparing approvals
* assembling briefings
* validating staged changes
* surfacing blockers early
* reducing morning reconstruction cost

That is the real center of Away Mode.

---

## 14.3 What Away Mode Must Feel Like

Away Mode should feel like a disciplined night shift operator.

That phrase matters because it captures the right balance:

* active, but not reckless
* useful, but not broad
* aware, but not invasive
* productive, but not theatrical
* trustworthy, but not self-important

### 14.3.1 The correct feeling

When a user returns from Away, the correct feeling is:

* Francis kept the important things moving
* nothing happened behind my back in a dangerous way
* I can see exactly what advanced
* I can see what stalled and why
* I have a meaningful re-entry point
* I feel relief, not suspicion

That is the target.

### 14.3.2 The wrong feeling

Away Mode must never make the user feel:

* I don’t know what happened while I was gone
* Francis may have crossed lines I didn’t expect
* the system was busy, but I’m not sure with what authority
* I’m now cleaning up AI side effects
* I feel haunted by background activity
* the machine became more invasive when I wasn’t there

That destroys the trust model.

### 14.3.3 Away should produce momentum, not anxiety

The emotional test of Away Mode is simple:

> does the user feel lighter or heavier on return?

If the user feels heavier—more uncertainty, more cleanup, more distrust, more ambiguity—the design has failed.

---

## 14.4 Core Goals of Away Mode

Away Mode should be narrow enough to stay trusted and broad enough to be genuinely valuable.

Its goals should be explicit.

### 14.4.1 Maintain momentum

Away Mode should preserve forward motion on work that does not require live human steering.

This includes preventing work from going stale or collapsing into reconstruction cost.

### 14.4.2 Reduce future friction

Not all valuable Away work is direct progress.

Some of it is reducing future burden by:

* staging artifacts
* preparing evidence
* sorting approvals
* organizing incidents
* assembling summaries
* pre-validating likely next steps

This can create enormous leverage on return.

### 14.4.3 Prepare decisions

Many times the best value while the user is away is not final action, but decision preparation.

Francis can often prepare:

* options
* supporting evidence
* risk summaries
* approval packets
* diff reviews
* validation results
* mission state transitions

This makes the user’s return more powerful.

### 14.4.4 Advance safe mission steps

Some mission steps are appropriate for Away continuation because they are:

* low-risk
* reversible
* explicitly allowed
* bounded
* well-understood
* validation-heavy rather than mutation-heavy
* already staged and waiting on routine continuation

These steps are ideal candidates.

### 14.4.5 Monitor critical conditions

Away Mode should be able to notice meaningful change in approved contexts so the user returns with better awareness.

Examples include:

* validation failure
* mission blocker resolution
* repeated anomaly
* threshold crossing
* dependency state change
* connector or node issue
* staged work becoming review-ready

Monitoring is valuable when it is tied to continuity rather than broad ambient observation.

---

## 14.5 What Away Mode May Do

Away Mode should have a clear lawful set of default action classes.

### 14.5.1 Scan

Francis may scan approved surfaces for relevant state changes or continuity signals.

This may include:

* checking validation outcomes
* checking mission dependencies
* checking monitored incident surfaces
* checking approved connectors
* checking branch or artifact readiness
* checking drift in known targets

Scanning should remain bounded and scope-specific.

### 14.5.2 Summarize

Away Mode may prepare high-value summaries for return such as:

* shift reports
* changed-state briefs
* anomaly summaries
* mission status updates
* decision packets
* validation outcome reports
* approval-ready bundles

This is one of the safest and most valuable Away functions.

### 14.5.3 Queue

Away Mode may create or update queues such as:

* approvals queue
* incident queue
* next-step queue
* deadletter review queue
* handback recommendations
* morning briefing queue

Queueing is often a powerful form of momentum because it shapes the return-to-work surface.

### 14.5.4 Stage

Away Mode may stage lawful work without silently promoting it.

Examples include:

* staging a patch on a branch
* staging a draft artifact
* staging a capability for review
* staging a report
* staging a remediation option
* staging a comparison package

Staging is one of the safest high-value continuations because it preserves user authority over final acceptance.

### 14.5.5 Prepare

Prepare is broader than stage and may include:

* assembling evidence
* collecting receipts
* preparing review surfaces
* precomputing comparisons
* organizing likely next actions
* structuring handoff materials
* gathering supporting local evidence for a future recommendation

This often yields major value with relatively low risk.

### 14.5.6 Advance safe low-risk tasks

Where explicitly lawful, Away Mode may advance tasks that are:

* low-risk
* bounded
* well-defined
* reversible where possible
* already within active mission flow
* supported by good verification paths

Examples may include:

* running validations
* generating reports
* performing approved housekeeping
* preparing branch state
* carrying approved batch transformations
* refreshing known-safe derived artifacts

This is where Away becomes more than passive monitoring.

---

## 14.6 What Away Mode Must Not Do by Default

The power of Away Mode depends heavily on what it refuses.

### 14.6.1 No silent scope expansion

Away Mode must not expand scope because:

* it found adjacent relevant files
* the mission is broad
* the system thinks it would help
* a similar task used a neighboring surface before
* absence makes clarification impossible

Scope stays explicit.

### 14.6.2 No high-risk destructive work by default

Away Mode should not, by default, perform high-risk destructive actions such as:

* deleting important assets
* mutating production state
* merging protected branches
* performing irreversible infra changes
* promoting staged capabilities
* triggering sensitive external actions
* broad connector writes with significant consequence

If such behavior is ever permitted, it must come from much stronger explicit policy and approval frameworks than normal Away defaults.

### 14.6.3 No promotion of staged capabilities by default

Forge promotion is a trust event and should not happen just because the user is gone.

Away may prepare, validate, and queue.

It must not silently promote.

### 14.6.4 No policy override

Away Mode is not an exception layer.

It must not override:

* approval requirements
* tool restrictions
* connector trust boundaries
* data retention rules
* redaction rules
* mission stop conditions
* risk policies

### 14.6.5 No hidden side work

Away Mode should not create the feeling that Francis used the quiet hours to pursue its own expanding agenda.

It must stay tied to:

* active Missions
* approved maintenance patterns
* explicit monitoring responsibilities
* lawful scheduled tasks
* approved continuity preparation

Background appetite is forbidden.

---

## 14.7 Away Mode Triggering and Scheduling

Away Mode should not be a permanent always-on drift state.

It must remain structured by triggers and windows.

### 14.7.1 Event-driven continuation

Away Mode should often be triggered by meaningful events such as:

* mission dependency change
* build completion
* validation failure
* scheduled batch readiness
* connector state change
* incident threshold crossing
* approval status change
* staging completion

This keeps activity tied to reality rather than constant churn.

### 14.7.2 Schedule windows

Away work often benefits from explicit schedule windows such as:

* nightly validation
* overnight report prep
* early morning briefing assembly
* maintenance windows
* low-traffic operation windows
* user-defined focus-away periods

Schedule windows help make Away intelligible and bounded.

### 14.7.3 Mission-linked continuation

Away runs should usually be attributable to a current Mission, maintenance objective, or specific continuity responsibility.

This keeps causality legible.

### 14.7.4 Away should return to stable state

Even while Away is enabled, individual Away runs should still:

* begin from a valid trigger
* operate in bounded steps
* produce receipts
* stop cleanly
* wait for the next event or window

Away Mode should not degrade into endless looping background activity.

---

## 14.8 Away Mode and Missions

Missions are one of the strongest organizing structures for Away.

### 14.8.1 Away should usually serve active missions

A mature Away Mode should be able to explain its work in mission terms:

* this advanced Mission A
* this prepared review for Mission B
* this detected blocker for Mission C
* this queued approval for Mission D
* this staged safe progress for Mission E

This is more useful than a generic list of background actions.

### 14.8.2 Away should preserve blocked truth

If a Mission cannot move, Away should not fake momentum.

It should be able to say:

* still blocked
* dependency unresolved
* approval still missing
* validation still failing
* nothing advanced because the stop condition remained active

That honesty matters.

### 14.8.3 Away can improve return-to-mission re-entry

One of the strongest values of Away is reducing re-entry cost.

The user should be able to step back into a Mission and immediately understand:

* what advanced
* what did not
* what changed
* what now needs them
* what can continue later
* what is ready for approval
* what remains uncertain

This is continuity made practical.

---

## 14.9 Away Mode and Telemetry

Away Mode depends on telemetry, but this is one of the most sensitive pairings in the whole product.

### 14.9.1 Away telemetry must remain bounded

User absence must never expand sensing rights.

Away telemetry should remain:

* scope-bound
* connector-specific where applicable
* policy-limited
* visibly inspectable on return
* minimal and purpose-linked

This prevents Away from drifting into ambient surveillance.

### 14.9.2 Away should sense for continuity, not curiosity

The reason to sense while away is to preserve momentum and detect relevant change, not to broadly “know what happened on the machine.”

That distinction is essential.

### 14.9.3 Away should not become more invasive because the user is gone

This must remain explicit.

If anything, the absence of live user supervision should strengthen the discipline of what Francis is willing to observe and act on—not weaken it.

---

## 14.10 Away Mode and Approvals

Approvals become more delicate in Away Mode because the user is not actively present to respond in real time.

### 14.10.1 Away should favor staging over crossing approval boundaries

When a continuation path approaches a meaningful approval threshold, the default behavior should usually be:

* stop
* preserve state
* queue approval
* prepare context
* wait for the user

This is one of the most important guardrails in the feature.

### 14.10.2 Approval-waiting is valid Away output

Away does not fail just because it stops at an approval boundary.

A strong Away result may be:

* progress made up to the approval edge
* context prepared well
* decision packet assembled
* next move ready for immediate human choice

That is high-value continuity.

### 14.10.3 Silence is not approval

User absence must never be interpreted as:

* implied consent
* approval through inaction
* permission to keep escalating
* authority to convert staged work into live work

This rule must stay absolute.

---

## 14.11 Away Mode and Trust Calibration

Away Mode must be especially honest because the user is not there to supervise moment-to-moment interpretation.

### 14.11.1 Away must not overclaim overnight progress

Francis should not come back with vague statements like:

* everything is handled
* all good
* fixed it
* no issues

unless those claims are actually supported by receipts and verification.

Away outputs must stay disciplined.

### 14.11.2 Partial progress should be surfaced clearly

A strong return briefing may say:

* validations completed; one remains failing
* three low-risk tasks advanced; one stopped at approval boundary
* patch staged and tested on branch; not promoted
* incident evidence collected; root cause still uncertain
* report prepared; final decision still needs you

That is much more trustworthy than inflated closure.

### 14.11.3 Uncertainty should be explicit on re-entry

Away reports should make it clear when something is:

* confirmed
* likely
* uncertain
* blocked
* staged but unverified
* changed but not yet reviewed

This helps the user re-enter with clarity rather than false confidence.

---

## 14.12 The Return Experience: Shift Report and Re-Entry

The return from Away Mode is one of the signature user-experience moments in Francis.

If done well, it is one of the strongest proofs that the system is real.

### 14.12.1 The user should return to a shift report, not a mystery
