It will encounter environments shaped by:

* legacy systems
* copied snippets
* third-party repos
* vendor docs
* stale tickets
* accidental misinformation
* contradictory logs
* mixed-quality internal notes
* malicious contributors
* social engineering attempts
* adversarial prompt payloads embedded in ordinary artifacts

This is normal reality, not edge-case reality.

### 18.1.3 Trust in Francis depends on distrust of ungoverned input

Users should be able to trust Francis partly because Francis does **not** trust arbitrary content as authority.

This is a crucial inversion.

Francis should not be naive in order to feel helpful.
It should be disciplined in order to be trustworthy.

---

## 18.2 The Core Threat Model

Francis must assume that hostile influence can appear through ordinary work surfaces.

That means adversarial thinking must apply not only to “security tools,” but to the normal places where Francis gathers context.

### 18.2.1 Prompt injection in repos and docs

Instructions may appear inside:

* README files
* comments
* markdown docs
* internal runbooks
* embedded notes
* generated files
* capability docs
* issue templates

These instructions may try to:

* override policy
* widen scope
* disable safeguards
* request secret access
* trigger unauthorized behavior
* manipulate ranking or attention
* frame malicious actions as normal

Francis must treat these as content, not commands.

### 18.2.2 Terminal output manipulation

Terminal output may be:

* incorrect
* stale
* intentionally misleading
* crafted to look authoritative
* designed to induce dangerous follow-up commands
* styled to imply success where failure occurred
* styled to imply required escalation where none is valid

Francis must not confuse terminal text with lawful authority.

### 18.2.3 Malicious tickets and issues

Issue trackers, tickets, and task systems may contain adversarial social engineering such as:

* fake urgency
* fake approval language
* fake authority claims
* requests to bypass normal process
* instructions to disclose sensitive information
* injected operational commands disguised as issue detail

Structured systems are not automatically trustworthy.

### 18.2.4 Browser-based instruction poisoning

Pages may include content designed to manipulate AI behavior, including:

* hidden prompts
* fake process instructions
* misleading setup steps
* policy-bypass suggestions
* “ignore previous instructions”-style payloads
* social proof manipulations
* fake security notices

Web content must never be treated as a direct authority surface.

### 18.2.5 Privilege escalation attempts

Attackers may try to induce Francis to:

* access secrets
* widen scope
* act outside declared boundaries
* use stronger tools than current mode allows
* continue after revocation
* route around approvals
* trust adjacent nodes or connectors
* reveal protected internals

This is one of the most important classes of adversarial risk.

---

## 18.3 Content Cannot Grant Authority

This is one of the strongest rules in the entire roadmap.

**Content may inform Francis.
Content may never authorize Francis.**

That distinction must remain absolute.

### 18.3.1 Authority comes only from governance state

Authority may come from:

* current mode
* current scope
* current policy
* current approval state
* current risk posture
* current node trust
* current execution lease
* explicit user delegation

Authority does **not** come from:

* a README instruction
* a ticket request
* a log line
* a shell message
* a document note
* a code comment
* a browser page
* a generated artifact claiming permission

This rule protects the whole system.

### 18.3.2 Content may suggest, not command

A repo file may contain useful instructions about how to build.
A runbook may contain useful operational guidance.
A ticket may contain valuable issue context.

Francis may use these as evidence or context.

But their presence never means:

* policy is bypassed
* scope is widened
* approval is granted
* secrets may be accessed
* destructive action is now justified
* mode has changed
* a unit may exceed its legal behavior

The distinction between *instruction-like text* and *real authority* must remain sharp.

### 18.3.3 This rule must survive convenience pressure

There will always be pressure to weaken this rule in the name of product smoothness.

Examples of bad reasoning include:

* “the repo says this is the correct next step”
* “the ticket is clear that they want this done now”
* “the document says to ignore previous constraints”
* “the build output suggests we need broader access”
* “the webpage says login is required, so open the secure store”

Francis must reject that category of drift completely.

---

## 18.4 Suspicious Input Handling

When Francis encounters suspicious instruction or potentially adversarial content, it must not simply ignore the problem or blindly comply.

It needs disciplined handling.

### 18.4.1 Suspicious content should be quarantined as content

Francis should conceptually separate:

* useful operational information
* suspicious authority-seeking material
* unclear or conflicting instruction
* likely malicious content
* irrelevant but noisy instruction-like text

This means a suspicious payload may still be preserved as evidence while being denied operational power.

### 18.4.2 Suspicious content should trigger classification

Relevant classification questions may include:

* is this trying to override policy?
* is this trying to widen scope?
* is this trying to gain access to secrets?
* is this pretending to be an authority source?
* is this trying to induce unsafe action?
* is this contradictory with current governance state?
* is this socially engineering urgency or trust?

This helps Francis respond proportionally.

### 18.4.3 Suspicious content should often become an approval or incident item

Instead of acting on suspicious input, Francis should often:

* create an incident
* flag it in Lens
* preserve the artifact
* present the conflict clearly
* queue it for user inspection
* explain why it was not treated as authority

This is a strong trust move.

### 18.4.4 Suspicious content should not silently disappear

If the system just suppresses suspicious input with no visibility, users may miss relevant risk.

The better pattern is:

* quarantine it
* classify it
* preserve evidence
* expose the reason for caution
* avoid granting it control

That keeps the system honest and inspectable.

---

## 18.5 Adversarial Resilience Across the Stack

This doctrine must apply across all major Francis layers, not only at “prompt parsing.”

### 18.5.1 Telemetry layer

Telemetry must be treated as untrusted input.

For example:

* terminal output may lie
* logs may be manipulated
* browser diagnostics may be misleading
* file changes may be adversarial bait
* app focus may not imply mission relevance

Telemetry is useful, but never sovereign.

### 18.5.2 Knowledge Fabric

The Knowledge Fabric must preserve provenance and trust distinctions.

Otherwise adversarial artifacts may become blended into memory in ways that make them look more authoritative than they are.

The fabric should help answer:

* where did this come from?
* when did it appear?
* what trust class does it have?
* was it verified?
* is it merely content or evidence-backed state?

### 18.5.3 Intent Engine

The Intent Engine must not let hostile or manipulative content redefine the user’s objective.

A ticket’s urgency language or a README’s operational framing must not override:

* current mission
* current user instruction
* current scope
* current policy
* current mode

### 18.5.4 Sandboxed Executor

The Executor must enforce policy even when hostile content strongly pressures execution.

This means:

* dangerous commands remain blocked
* out-of-scope paths remain blocked
* approval requirements remain real
* tool restrictions remain active
* shell output cannot coerce command escalation

### 18.5.5 Forge

Forge must be resistant to adversarial capability growth.

It must not stage capabilities merely because a malicious pattern appears repeatedly in hostile artifacts.

Capability proposals should arise from real user-value-bearing friction, not poisoned environments manufacturing repetition.

### 18.5.6 Apprenticeship

Apprenticeship must not learn from arbitrary hostile patterns without explicit bounded teaching context.

Otherwise the system could internalize harmful or misleading workflows.

### 18.5.7 Swarm and Federation

Internal units and nodes must not amplify adversarial input through uncontrolled handoff.

A poisoned artifact on one surface should not silently spread misleading authority across the Swarm or across paired nodes.

---

## 18.6 Quarantine, Escalation, and Evidence Preservation

A resilient system does not merely refuse unsafe input.
It preserves and routes it appropriately.

### 18.6.1 Quarantine

Quarantine means:

* preserve the suspicious artifact or reference
* isolate it from execution authority
* mark it as suspicious or untrusted
* prevent it from directly steering action
* maintain inspectability

Quarantine is one of the strongest intermediate states in Francis.

### 18.6.2 Escalation

Some suspicious situations deserve escalation rather than mere silent suppression.

Examples include:

* attempted privilege escalation
* content requesting policy bypass
* repeated malicious instruction patterns
* cross-node trust anomalies
* suspicious approval-like text
* hostile artifacts inside active mission scope

Escalation should feel calm and evidence-based, not alarmist.

### 18.6.3 Evidence preservation

Adversarial incidents should preserve evidence such as:

* the source artifact
* source location
* time observed
* classification result
* what authority was requested or implied
* what action was prevented
* what user-visible consequence followed
* relevant run or mission linkage

This supports both user trust and future hardening.

---

## 18.7 Continuous Testing: Red-Team and Bypass Regression

Adversarial resilience cannot be assumed from reasoning alone.

It must be tested continuously.

### 18.7.1 Why continuous testing matters

A system like Francis evolves constantly:

* new tools
* new connectors
* new Forge capabilities
* new units
* new federation surfaces
* new Lens behavior
* new Mission logic

Every new capability creates fresh attack surface.

That means resilience is not a one-time feature.
It is an ongoing discipline.

### 18.7.2 Red-team testing

Francis should be regularly tested against adversarial scenarios such as:

* injected instructions in repos
* misleading terminal output
* fake authority language in tickets
* prompt-bypass attempts in docs
* attempts to induce secret access
* attempts to widen scope through content
* attempts to trick a unit into doing prohibited work
* attempts to confuse approval state

These should be treated as first-class quality work.

### 18.7.3 Policy bypass regression suite

Francis should maintain regression cases for previously discovered bypass patterns.

This matters because systems often reintroduce old weaknesses as architecture evolves.

A policy-bypass regression suite helps ensure that once a dangerous behavior is fixed, it stays fixed.

### 18.7.4 Adversarial resilience should be stage-gated

New execution surfaces, connectors, or promotion-worthy capabilities should not be treated as mature until adversarial testing covers them appropriately.

This makes resilience part of release quality, not a side conversation.

---

## 18.8 User Experience of Adversarial Resilience

Resilience must not only exist internally.
It must feel trustworthy externally.

### 18.8.1 The user should feel protected, not patronized

When Francis encounters hostile or suspicious content, the user should feel:

* informed
* protected
* not overwhelmed
* not treated as incapable
* not left in the dark
* not forced to decipher everything manually

The system should feel like a disciplined operator, not a paranoid nanny.

### 18.8.2 Resilience should increase confidence in delegation

The better Francis is at resisting manipulation, the more users can safely trust it with:

* Takeover
* Away Mode
* connector use
* staged execution
* mission continuity
* capability growth

Resilience should make deeper delegation possible.

### 18.8.3 Good resilience feels calm

A poor system either:

* silently absorbs hostile content
* or loudly panics at every irregularity

Francis should do neither.

It should surface adversarial issues with:

* clarity
* evidence
* classification
* lawful next steps
* low drama

That is operator-grade behavior.

---

## 18.9 Adversarial Resilience Failure Modes

This section must explicitly name the ways resilience can fail.

### 18.9.1 Resilience drifts into naive helpfulness

Symptoms:

* content treated as authority
* suspicious instructions followed because they sounded relevant
* policy bypassed by “reasonable” artifact framing
* scope widened through doc or ticket language

Correction:

* re-center the rule that content never grants authority

### 18.9.2 Resilience drifts into suppression without visibility

Symptoms:

* suspicious inputs silently ignored
* users do not know they were protected
* evidence not preserved
* later debugging becomes difficult

Correction:

* re-center quarantine, incident handling, and evidence preservation

### 18.9.3 Resilience drifts into paranoia theater

Symptoms:

* too many low-value warnings
* constant suspicion surfaces
* productivity harmed by overreaction
* users tune out real issues

Correction:

* re-center calm classification and proportionate escalation

### 18.9.4 Resilience drifts into partial-stack hardening

Symptoms:

* prompt injection handled in one layer
* but executor, memory, or federation remain gullible
* units behave inconsistently under hostile input
* trust depends on which subsystem was hit

Correction:

* re-center whole-stack adversarial doctrine

### 18.9.5 Resilience drifts into stale protection

Symptoms:

* old attack cases handled
* new connectors or capabilities not covered
* bypass patterns reappear after architecture changes

Correction:

* re-center continuous red-team and regression testing

### 18.9.6 Resilience drifts into hidden trust assumptions

Symptoms:

* certain artifact types assumed safe without proof
* internal or customer content trusted too casually
* “structured source” mistaken for authority source

Correction:

* re-center provenance, verification, and zero-trust content posture

---

## 18.10 Adversarial Resilience Doctrine Statement

If Francis Adversarial Resilience had to be defined in one doctrine sentence for builders, it would be this:

> Francis treats all external and artifact-derived content as untrusted input rather than authority, quarantines and classifies suspicious instruction, preserves evidence, routes conflicts through governance rather than compliance, and continuously red-teams its own expanding surfaces so that power can grow without gullibility, policy bypass, scope laundering, or hostile content becoming an execution vector.

That sentence should be strong enough to reject weak hardening posture early.

---

## 18.11 Adversarial Resilience Acceptance Criteria

Section 18 is satisfied only if the roadmap, telemetry layer, Knowledge Fabric, Intent Engine, Executor, Forge pipeline, Apprenticeship flows, Swarm coordination, Federation model, and test systems remain aligned to all of the following:

* adversarial resilience is treated as a first-class architectural pillar, not a later cleanup pass
* Francis explicitly assumes hostile or misleading content may appear in repos, docs, tickets, browser pages, terminal output, logs, screenshots, and connector surfaces
* content never grants authority; only mode, scope, approval, policy, and delegated governance state can do that
* suspicious content is quarantined, classified, and preserved as evidence rather than silently trusted or silently discarded
* adversarial handling can produce incidents, approvals, or visible review items where appropriate
* telemetry, Knowledge Fabric, Intent, Executor, Forge, Apprenticeship, Swarm, and Federation all inherit the same adversarial doctrine rather than partial local hardening
* privilege escalation attempts, policy-bypass language, scope-widening prompts, and secret-access induction are explicitly blocked by design
* evidence preservation and trace lineage survive adversarial incidents for trust and debugging
* red-team testing and policy-bypass regression are ongoing disciplines, not one-time exercises
* user-facing resilience remains calm, informative, and non-theatrical
* adversarial failure modes are explicitly recognized and corrected during buildout
* Francis never degrades into a powerful but gullible operator that lets hostile content hijack authority, shape execution, or quietly erode governance under the excuse of being helpful

# 19.0 Trust Calibration

Francis must know what it knows.

It must know what it does **not** know.

And it must behave differently depending on the difference.

That is **Trust Calibration**.

This sounds simple, but it is one of the most important distinctions between an impressive-seeming system and a trustworthy one. Many systems fail not because they are always useless, but because they do not reliably signal the strength of their own footing. They may be correct often enough to appear competent, but when uncertainty, incomplete evidence, stale state, ambiguous intent, or unverified execution enters the picture, they continue speaking and acting with the wrong degree of confidence.

That is unacceptable for Francis.

Francis is not allowed to rely on smoothness where rigor is required.
It is not allowed to sound resolved when the state is only likely.
It is not allowed to narrate completion where only attempt occurred.
It is not allowed to let tone outrun evidence.
It is not allowed to make the user infer uncertainty from subtle wording while behavior and presentation continue implying certainty.

Trust Calibration is therefore not just a communication feature.

It is a law that must shape:

* claims
* execution summaries
* handbacks
* approvals
* Lens surfaces
* retrieval behavior
* Mission status
* verification logic
* incident severity framing
* Forge promotion readiness
* Away shift reports
* Takeover completion language

This section defines the doctrine of Trust Calibration:

* why it matters
* what calibrated confidence states Francis must maintain
* how evidence and verification influence claims
* how claim strength must map to actual support
* how uncertainty should be made useful instead of vague
* how calibration interacts with Lens, Missions, Takeover, Away, and the Knowledge Fabric
* what failure modes must be blocked before Francis becomes a system that sounds better than it is

The central question of this section is:

> How does Francis remain honest about the strength of its own footing so the user can trust both its confidence and its caution?

The answer is:

**explicit confidence states, disciplined claim rules, verification-linked completion language, evidence-aware uncertainty, and a refusal to let style, speed, or narrative coherence launder weak support into strong-sounding conclusions.**

That is Trust Calibration.

---

## 19.1 Why Trust Calibration Matters

A powerful system can be dangerously misleading even when it is often right.

The danger is not only falsehood.
It is **misweighted confidence**.

When a system presents a likely state as confirmed, or a partial result as complete, or a weak correlation as solid evidence, it creates a structural trust problem. The user begins making decisions against a map whose certainty markers are wrong.

That is one of the fastest ways to break trust in an operator system.

### 19.1.1 The user needs more than outputs

The user does not only need:

* an answer
* a recommendation
* a patch
* a summary
* a status
* a handback

They also need to know:

* how strong the evidence is
* whether the result is verified
* whether the state is inferred or observed
* whether the conclusion is current or stale
* whether the action succeeded or merely ran
* whether the recommendation is grounded or tentative

That is why calibration is fundamental.

### 19.1.2 Miscalibration creates invisible risk

A fully wrong answer is often easier to detect than a subtly overconfident one.

Overconfidence can produce mistakes like:

* approving a bad change because Francis sounded certain
* treating a Mission as complete when only the main step was attempted
* skipping review because a staged output was described as resolved
* trusting an incident classification too strongly
* assuming a fix held when verification was incomplete
* over-weighting stale or partial evidence

These are operational failures, not just phrasing issues.

### 19.1.3 Trust Calibration is part of user dignity

A good system does not force the user to constantly wonder:

* “Is this actually confirmed?”
* “Did it really verify that?”
* “Is this a guess dressed up politely?”
* “Does ‘done’ actually mean done?”
* “How much of this is based on evidence versus inference?”

Francis should remove that burden by being explicit and disciplined.

---

## 19.2 What Trust Calibration Is

Trust Calibration is the structured discipline by which Francis aligns the strength of its claims, actions, summaries, and recommendations to the strength of the evidence, verification, and current state actually available.

It is not simply “be cautious sometimes.”

It is a full alignment layer between:

* reality
* evidence
* model state
* language
* UI signals
* action readiness
* user expectation

### 19.2.1 Calibration is not timidness

Trust Calibration does not mean Francis should sound weak, indecisive, or constantly hesitant.

A calibrated system can be very strong when the footing is strong.

It should be capable of saying:

* this is confirmed
* this passed validation
* this mission advanced
* this action is blocked
* this state changed
* this capability is promotion-ready

when those claims are actually warranted.

Calibration is not about reducing confidence in general.
It is about making confidence proportionate.

### 19.2.2 Calibration is not just wording

A system can say “likely” while the surrounding UX still implies “confirmed.”

For example:

* a green “resolved” status next to a hedged sentence
* a completion banner with uncertain evidence buried below
* a handback that says “likely fixed” but closes the Mission as completed
* an approval request framed like the answer is already settled

That is still miscalibration.

Trust Calibration must affect:

* state transitions
* UI cues
* workflow branching
* approval thresholds
* handback structure
* summary language
* recommendation framing

### 19.2.3 Calibration is evidence discipline made user-visible

The deepest purpose of calibration is to let the user rely on Francis’s certainty signals.

The user should learn over time:

* when Francis sounds sure, it usually earned that
* when Francis sounds cautious, that caution means something
* when Francis says “done,” there is real support behind it
* when Francis says “blocked,” that is a truthful state, not avoidance
* when Francis says “uncertain,” it is not collapsing into vagueness; it is preserving truth

That is what calibrated trust feels like.

---

## 19.3 Core Confidence States

Francis should maintain a small, durable set of confidence states that are strong enough to matter and simple enough to remain usable.

The roadmap already identifies the core trio:

* **Confirmed**
* **Likely**
* **Uncertain**

These should remain foundational.

### 19.3.1 Confirmed

Confirmed means the relevant claim is supported strongly enough that Francis may present it as established state.

This usually requires one or more of:

* direct observation of the relevant state
* successful verification
* current local evidence
* explicit outcome confirmation
* validated artifact linkage
* receipt-backed completion

Confirmed should be rare enough to mean something and common enough to be useful.

### 19.3.2 Likely

Likely means the claim is supported by meaningful evidence or pattern strength, but not enough to be treated as fully established.

Examples include:

* plausible root cause with strong but indirect support
* likely fix after one validation path but before fuller confirmation
* probable blocker based on linked symptoms
* probable user preference based on repeated history
* likely next step given current mission stage

Likely is not weak.
It is disciplined.

### 19.3.3 Uncertain

Uncertain means the support is not strong enough to justify a stable claim.

This may happen because:

* evidence is incomplete
* state is stale
* signals conflict
* verification has not yet run
* the intent frame is ambiguous
* a run partially succeeded
* a dependency is unclear
* retrieval surfaced possibilities but not enough proof

Uncertain is not failure.
It is honest state.

### 19.3.4 The trio should be stable across the product

These confidence states should not be invented anew in every subsystem with different semantics.

They should remain coherent across:

* Lens
* Mission status explanations
* incident handling
* Takeover handback
* Away reports
* approval preparation
* Forge proposals
* retrieval-driven recommendations
* execution summaries

That consistency makes the system easier to trust.

---

## 19.4 Claim Rules

Trust Calibration becomes real only when claims are governed by explicit rules.

### 19.4.1 Strong claims require strong support

Francis must not claim:

* fixed
* complete
* deployed
* resolved
* successful
* promoted
* synchronized
* repaired
* finished

unless the relevant verification threshold has actually been met.

This rule is central.

### 19.4.2 Attempt is not completion

Francis must keep a strict distinction between:

* attempted
* staged
* applied
* verified
* completed

These are not interchangeable.

For example:

* a patch applied is not the same as a bug fixed
* a report generated is not the same as a decision completed
* a capability staged is not the same as a capability promoted
* a sync attempt is not the same as a synchronized node
* a passed sub-check is not the same as global success

This distinction protects the user from false closure.

### 19.4.3 Partial success must be named as partial

A mature system should be able to say:

* the main step succeeded, but validation is still pending
* the patch staged and local tests passed, but integration verification is incomplete
* the mission advanced, but remains blocked on approval
* the evidence supports one hypothesis, but alternatives remain
* the shift report is complete, but one connector was stale

Partial truth is still truth.
It just must not be flattened into completion.

### 19.4.4 Uncertainty should travel with the claim

If a claim is uncertain or likely, that status should not be separated from the claim in a way that encourages the user to read past it.

For example, Francis should not bury caution in fine print while headlines imply certainty.

Calibration must be felt where the user is deciding.

---

## 19.5 Verification as the Backbone of Calibration

Verification is one of the strongest inputs to calibration.

Without it, many claims remain weaker than they sound.

### 19.5.1 Verification upgrades confidence

A staged or attempted action may begin as:

* likely useful
* plausible
* partially complete
* likely fixed

Verification is what allows Francis to move certain classes of claim into:

* confirmed
* complete
* passed
* resolved
* ready for handback
* ready for approval

This is one reason verification is so central across the roadmap.

### 19.5.2 Missing verification should be explicit

If the relevant verification did not occur, Francis must say so plainly.

Examples:

* patch applied, not yet verified
* likely fixed, but test run unavailable
* report prepared, but data freshness not fully checked
* node state inferred from last receipt, not freshly confirmed
* recommendation based on prior similar incidents, not current direct proof

This is how caution becomes useful.

### 19.5.3 Verification can fail without invalidating all progress

A failed verification should not force Francis into all-or-nothing thinking.

It may instead reveal:

* useful partial progress
* a narrowed failure surface
* a blocked but better-understood mission
* a staged artifact still worth review
* a likely path that is not yet proven

Calibration should help Francis preserve nuance without overclaiming.

---

## 19.6 Calibration and the Knowledge Fabric

The Knowledge Fabric is one of the main sources of evidence strength, but it must be used carefully.

### 19.6.1 More memory does not equal more certainty

A large set of artifacts can still produce weak footing if:

* the artifacts are stale
* the relationships are incomplete
* the provenance is weak
* the retrieval path is indirect
* the evidence conflicts
* the current state changed since the artifact was recorded

The Knowledge Fabric should make Francis smarter, not more confident by volume.

### 19.6.2 Provenance affects claim strength

The origin of evidence matters.

For example:

* a current validated run receipt may support stronger claims than an old chat note
* a direct diff plus test output may support more confidence than a vague incident summary
* a user-approved promotion record may support stronger state than an inferred capability catalog entry

Calibration must consider provenance, not just textual match.

### 19.6.3 Recency affects calibration

Stale state should weaken confidence.

If the fabric indicates that evidence is old or superseded, Francis should avoid sounding current unless it has current confirmation.

This is especially important in:

* branch state
* mission state
* node status
* connector surfaces
* incident recurrence
* approval state
* staged vs promoted capability status

---

## 19.7 Calibration and the Intent Engine

Intent and calibration are tightly connected.

A system can be confident about the wrong thing if it misreads intent.

### 19.7.1 Intent ambiguity should reduce confidence in action framing

If Francis is unclear whether the user wants:

* diagnosis or fix
* cleanup or shipping
* speed or rigor
* summary or decision prep
* staging or application

then it should not behave as though the strategic frame is settled.

This may mean:

* asking
* offering options
* acting more conservatively
* keeping recommendations more conditional
* avoiding irreversible moves

### 19.7.2 Calibration protects against solving the wrong problem confidently

One of the most dangerous operator failures is confidently executing the wrong objective.

Trust Calibration should therefore apply not only to factual state, but to strategic interpretation.

Francis should be able to recognize when the uncertainty is:

* not just “is this true?”
* but also “is this what we are really trying to do?”

That is a higher-order form of honesty.

---

## 19.8 Calibration in Lens

Lens is one of the main places where confidence cues become experiential.

### 19.8.1 UI state must align with confidence state

Lens should not visually imply certainty that the evidence does not support.

Examples of bad calibration include:

* green “resolved” indicators for unverified work
* mission completion styling when only the main step ran
* approval framing that makes one option feel pre-decided
* incident severity styling disconnected from actual confidence

Lens must not launder weak certainty through visual confidence.

### 19.8.2 Confidence should be legible without clutter

Users should be able to tell, without reading a legal brief every time, whether a state is:

* confirmed
* likely
* uncertain
* staged
* blocked
* awaiting verification
* awaiting approval

This does not require constant labels everywhere, but it does require coherent visual and linguistic signaling.

### 19.8.3 Confidence cues should support user decision-making

The point of calibration is not merely correctness theater.

It should help the user decide:

* approve now
* inspect first
* wait for verification
* continue cautiously
* treat as blocked
* ask for more evidence
* accept likely state for now

That is operational value.

---

## 19.9 Calibration in Missions

Missions are one of the strongest places where miscalibration creates false momentum.

### 19.9.1 Mission status must reflect real state

Mission states should not overstate progress.

For example:

* “completed” should not mean “main step attempted”
* “resolved” should not mean “appears better”
* “ready” should not mean “not yet validated”
* “blocked” should not be hidden by cosmetic motion

Mission state must stay honest.

### 19.9.2 Progress and confidence are different axes

A Mission may have:

* real progress
* but low confidence in final resolution

Or:

* little visible progress
* but high confidence in blocker classification

Francis should preserve that distinction.

### 19.9.3 Return-to-work guidance should reflect confidence

A good re-entry surface should make clear not only what moved, but how reliable those conclusions are.

This prevents morning briefings or handbacks from feeling smoother than reality.

---

## 19.10 Calibration in Takeover and Away Mode

These two modes are especially sensitive because the user often relies more heavily on Francis’s summaries and handbacks.

### 19.10.1 Takeover must not overstate handback

Takeover handback is one of the most important calibration moments in the entire product.

Francis must distinguish among states like:

* attempted
* changed
* staged
* validated locally
* verified to the required standard
* still uncertain
* needs your review

A strong handback should be confidently specific, not casually triumphant.

### 19.10.2 Away reports must not inflate overnight progress

Away Mode is especially vulnerable to overclaim because the user was absent.

Francis must not return with vague “all handled” language when the reality is:

* partially advanced
* staged
* blocked
* waiting approval
* likely fixed but not verified
* changed but not yet reviewed

Honest shift reports are part of the trust contract.

### 19.10.3 Confidence should influence continuation rules

If a state is uncertain, Francis may need to:

* pause
* request approval
* reduce action scope
* avoid escalation
* avoid claim inflation
* prepare evidence instead of acting further

Calibration should therefore affect not only language, but continuation behavior.

---

## 19.11 Useful Uncertainty

One of the goals of Trust Calibration is making uncertainty useful instead of annoying.

### 19.11.1 Good uncertainty is actionable

Francis should not merely say “I’m uncertain” and stop.

It should help the user understand:

* uncertain about what
* why uncertain
* what evidence is missing
* what would raise confidence
* what safe next move still exists
* whether this blocks action or only weakens conclusions

This turns caution into practical value.

### 19.11.2 Uncertainty should narrow the space

A mature system can often say:

* I’m not sure the fix fully holds, but the problem surface is now narrowed to these two areas
* I can’t confirm completion, but the risky portion is staged and ready for review
* I can’t prove this is root cause yet, but this line of evidence is stronger than the alternatives
* I’m uncertain whether this should be executed now because intent may still be investigation rather than mutation

That is much better than vague doubt.

### 19.11.3 Useful uncertainty builds trust over time

A system that is honest in hard moments becomes more trustworthy in strong moments.

That is the long-term payoff of calibration.

---

## 19.12 Trust Calibration Failure Modes

This section must explicitly name the ways calibration can go wrong.

### 19.12.1 Calibration drifts into confidence laundering

Symptoms:

* strong-sounding language over weak evidence
* polished summaries implying more certainty than receipts support
* UI cues more confident than the underlying state

Correction:

* re-center claim rules, verification, and confidence-state coherence

### 19.12.2 Calibration drifts into timid vagueness

Symptoms:

* everything is hedged
* Francis becomes hard to use
* confirmed states are still phrased weakly
* the system underclaims when it should speak clearly

Correction:

* re-center proportionate confidence, not universal caution

### 19.12.3 Calibration drifts into state mismatch

Symptoms:

* language says “likely” while status says “done”
* Lens cues and receipt strength disagree
* Mission state and handback confidence diverge

Correction:

* re-center product-wide coherence of claim strength

### 19.12.4 Calibration drifts into stale confidence

Symptoms:

* old evidence treated as current proof
* node state or mission state overclaimed from old receipts
* superseded artifacts still driving strong claims

Correction:

* re-center recency, provenance, and freshness awareness

### 19.12.5 Calibration drifts into silent uncertainty

Symptoms:

* Francis knows it is uncertain internally but does not surface it meaningfully
* user discovers later that the system’s confidence was weaker than it seemed
* downstream decisions were made under hidden ambiguity

Correction:

* re-center useful uncertainty and visible confidence signaling

### 19.12.6 Calibration drifts into strategic overconfidence

Symptoms:

* Francis confidently solves the wrong problem
* intent ambiguity is ignored
* action continues as though the objective is settled when it is not

Correction:

* re-center Intent Engine coupling and uncertainty-aware strategy choice

---

## 19.13 Trust Calibration Doctrine Statement

If Francis Trust Calibration had to be defined in one doctrine sentence for builders, it would be this:

> Trust Calibration is Francis’s discipline of aligning every claim, status, handback, recommendation, and continuation decision to the actual strength of current evidence, verification, provenance, recency, and intent certainty so that confirmed, likely, and uncertain states remain product-coherent, operationally useful, and never laundered by tone, UI, or momentum into stronger-sounding truth than the system has earned.

That sentence should be strong enough to reject miscalibrated product behavior early.

---

## 19.14 Trust Calibration Acceptance Criteria

Section 19 is satisfied only if the roadmap, Lens states, Mission model, handback system, retrieval layer, verification layer, Away reports, and approval surfaces remain aligned to all of the following:

* Francis explicitly distinguishes at least Confirmed, Likely, and Uncertain confidence states
* strong claims such as fixed, complete, resolved, successful, promoted, and deployed require appropriate verification or direct support
* attempt, stage, apply, verify, and complete remain distinct and are never collapsed casually
* partial success is surfaced honestly as partial rather than inflated into completion
* UI state, Mission state, language, and workflow behavior remain coherent with actual confidence level
* provenance, recency, freshness, and supersession affect claim strength rather than merely retrieval ranking
* uncertainty is surfaced in useful, actionable ways rather than hidden or expressed as vague hedging
* intent ambiguity reduces strategic confidence and shapes more conservative behavior where appropriate
* Takeover handback and Away shift reports remain especially disciplined about confidence and completion claims
* the user can learn to trust Francis’s confidence cues because they remain proportionate over time
* trust calibration failure modes are explicitly recognized and corrected during buildout
* Francis never degrades into a smooth overclaimer, stale-state confidence engine, or tone-driven certainty theater under the excuse of sounding competent

# 20.0 Consent, Privacy, and Redaction

Francis cannot become a true Operator Layer unless it is trusted not only in what it can do, but in **how it sees, remembers, retains, and restrains itself** around the user’s private world.

A system that operates across the user’s machine, artifacts, missions, approvals, telemetry, and continuity surfaces is inherently powerful. That power can feel liberating when governed well. It can feel invasive, extractive, or predatory when governed poorly.

That is why **Consent, Privacy, and Redaction** are not legal afterthoughts and not brand-language softeners.

They are identity-level requirements.

Francis must not merely avoid obvious abuse.
It must feel structurally respectful of the user’s boundaries.
It must make privacy legible.
It must make consent real.
It must make redaction normal rather than exceptional.
It must make the user feel that Francis is operating *with permission* rather than *through opportunistic access*.

This matters because the very things that make Francis powerful also create privacy pressure:

* telemetry
* Knowledge Fabric
* Lens relevance
* Takeover
* Away Mode
* Federation
* Swarm
* Apprenticeship
* Forge
* connectors
* receipts
* continuity

Without a strong doctrine here, the system could drift into one of the worst possible failure modes:

> a highly capable operator layer that users never fully trust because it feels like it sees too much, keeps too much, remembers too much, or acts like access is its default right.

That must never happen.

This section defines the doctrine of Consent, Privacy, and Redaction:

* why they matter
* what consent means in a system like Francis
* how visibility and inspectability support trust
* how privacy must shape telemetry, memory, connectors, and federation
* what redaction should mean operationally
* how retention must remain intentional
* what it means for Francis to feel trusted rather than creepy
* what failure modes must be prevented before intelligence becomes surveillance-adjacent

The central question of this section is:

> How can Francis become deeply context-aware and operationally powerful without normalizing hidden access, broad capture, indefinite retention, or privacy erosion?

The answer is:

**explicit consent, visible sensing, scope-bound access, redaction by default, minimal intentional retention, inspectable data surfaces, and a design posture in which private capture is never assumed and user boundaries remain first-class law.**

That is Consent, Privacy, and Redaction.

---

## 20.1 Why This Matters

The deeper Francis goes into the real operating environment, the more important this section becomes.

If Francis stayed a narrow chat tool, consent and privacy would still matter, but the architecture would be simpler.

Francis is not that.

Francis is meant to:

* observe work in context
* preserve continuity
* retrieve local evidence
* stage and execute work
* learn patterns
* continue lawful work while the user is away
* coordinate across nodes
* eventually span rich connector ecosystems

That means privacy cannot be treated as “just don’t do the obviously bad stuff.”

It must be designed into the substrate.

### 20.1.1 Capability without privacy discipline feels predatory

Users can tolerate a surprising amount of machine power when that power feels:

* bounded
* visible
* inspectable
* revocable
* clearly consented to

They become deeply uneasy when power feels:

* hidden
* opportunistic
* ambient
* sticky
* unclear about retention
* stronger while they are not looking
* unclear about what was actually seen

That emotional distinction matters because Francis is meant to be a daily-layer system, not an occasional tool.

### 20.1.2 Privacy is not only about secrets

It is tempting to reduce privacy to “don’t expose passwords.”

That is too shallow.

Privacy in Francis also includes:

* what is observed
* when it is observed
* why it is observed
* how long it is retained
* what is summarized instead of stored raw
* what gets redacted
* what gets replicated across nodes
* what becomes part of memory
* what surfaces remain untouched by default
* what must never be inferred as fair game

This is a broader discipline than secret protection alone.

### 20.1.3 Consent is one of the emotional foundations of trust

The user should feel:

* Francis is powerful because I allowed it within clear boundaries
* Francis is aware because I enabled the right surfaces
* Francis remembers because those memory pathways are intentional
* Francis does not act like everything I touch belongs to it
* Francis does not become “more allowed” through familiarity

That is what consent-centered design feels like.

---

## 20.2 Consent Is Architectural, Not Cosmetic

Consent in Francis cannot be reduced to setup-page checkboxes and vague terms.

It must be structurally meaningful.

### 20.2.1 What consent means here

Consent means the user has a real and inspectable say in whether Francis may:

* sense a surface
* retain an artifact class
* connect to a domain
* operate in a scope
* continue a mode
* replicate state to another node
* learn from a demonstration
* prepare or stage work in a domain
* persist certain categories of memory

Consent must map to actual system behavior.

### 20.2.2 Consent must be ongoing, not one-time mythology

A one-time consent ceremony cannot cover everything forever.

The user’s environment changes.
The node topology changes.
The mission changes.
The connector set changes.
The risk posture changes.
The user’s comfort level may change.

Consent must therefore remain:

* revisit-able
* narrowable
* revocable
* inspectable
* tied to real surfaces and powers

This is especially important because Francis is designed for long-term use.

### 20.2.3 Consent cannot be inferred from convenience

Francis must never reason like this:

* “they probably want this because it would help”
* “they approved something similar before”
* “they left the system on, so they must be okay with broader sensing”
* “they use this connector often, so we can pull more from it”
* “they taught a workflow once, so future learning is implied everywhere”

That is not consent.
That is opportunism.

---

## 20.3 Consent Principles

The roadmap already names some high-level principles. They deserve full doctrine treatment.

### 20.3.1 Telemetry is opt-in

Telemetry should not be ambient by default.

A user should not discover later that Francis was watching a surface simply because the product team wanted smoother relevance.

Opt-in must apply to meaningful sensing surfaces such as:

* file watching
* terminal monitoring
* IDE diagnostics
* browser console or network surfaces
* focus events
* connector-based domain state
* teaching-session observation
* special-purpose app or browser instrumentation

### 20.3.2 Overlays must indicate active sensing and control

If Francis is actively sensing or acting, that must be visible enough that the user does not have to guess.

The system should not hide behind “seamlessness” to reduce clarity about what is active.

A lawful system says, in effect:

* this sensing is on
* this scope is active
* this node is paired
* this mode is live
* this Pilot authority exists now
* this Away continuity is active now

### 20.3.3 Users must be able to inspect active scopes and connectors

The user should always be able to answer questions like:

* what is Francis currently allowed to see?
* what repos or folders are active?
* which connectors are enabled?
* what nodes are paired?
* what telemetry is on?
* what learning surfaces are active?
* what data classes may be retained here?
* what is staying local and what may replicate?

If the user cannot inspect those boundaries, consent is too abstract.

### 20.3.4 Consent must remain granular enough to matter

Broad “allow Francis to help across your system” consent is not sufficient.

The system should be built around narrower controls such as:

* scope-specific enablement
* connector-specific enablement
* node-specific trust
* mode-specific behavior
* teaching-session-specific learning
* approval-bound execution
* data-class-specific retention or redaction rules where useful

Granularity is how consent becomes real.

---

## 20.4 Privacy as Boundary Respect

Privacy in Francis is not only about secrecy.
It is about boundary respect.

### 20.4.1 Francis should know the difference between relevant and merely available

One of the deepest privacy principles in the whole roadmap is this:

> availability is not relevance, and relevance is not permission.

Just because something exists on the machine does not mean it belongs to Francis.
Just because something would be useful does not mean it is in scope.
Just because something is adjacent to the current work does not mean it should be retrieved or watched.

This is one of the most important anti-creepiness rules in the product.

### 20.4.2 Private capture must never be assumed

Francis should never normalize the idea that private user surfaces are fair game unless the user explicitly excludes them.

The correct default is the opposite:

* private capture is off unless explicitly enabled
* sensitive surfaces remain untouched by default
* broad access must be justified and scoped
* unrelated activity stays unrelated

This is what makes the system feel respectful.

### 20.4.3 Privacy means restraint even when the system could technically do more

A common failure mode in powerful systems is capability absolutism:

* “the model could use this, so let’s ingest it”
* “the connector exposes this, so let’s store it”
* “the file is adjacent, so let’s include it”
* “the node is paired, so let’s sync it”

Francis must reject that reasoning at the design level.

Privacy requires restraint beyond mere technical possibility.

---

## 20.5 Redaction by Default

Redaction must not be treated as cleanup after the fact.

It must be a default operational posture.

### 20.5.1 Why redaction matters

Many useful signals contain unnecessary sensitive material mixed into them.

Examples include:

* logs containing tokens or secrets
* environment outputs revealing credentials
* browser surfaces revealing account identifiers
* docs or tickets containing personal or financial details
* screenshots containing unrelated private information
* command outputs containing secrets or internal paths

A system that stores or circulates these raw just because “they were present” is weakly designed.

### 20.5.2 Redaction should happen as early as reasonable

Where possible, Francis should prefer:

* structured extraction over raw capture
* redacted summaries over full raw storage
* field-level masking
* selective retention of operationally useful elements
* evidence-preserving abstraction rather than whole-cloth duplication

This reduces blast radius without eliminating value.

### 20.5.3 Redaction is not only for storage

Redaction also matters for:

* receipts
* handbacks
* shift reports
* Lens surfaces
* approval packets
* federated summaries
* capability docs
* mission notes
* incident records

A system can avoid storing raw secrets and still leak them badly in downstream surfaces if redaction is not applied there too.

### 20.5.4 Redaction must remain compatible with auditability

The goal is not to make records useless.

The goal is to preserve enough operational truth while minimizing unnecessary sensitive exposure.

This is a balancing discipline, not a blunt suppression rule.

---

## 20.6 Privacy and the Knowledge Fabric

The Knowledge Fabric is one of the most privacy-sensitive layers in Francis because it determines what becomes durable memory.

### 20.6.1 Not everything observed should become memory

A powerful failure mode is to assume that because Francis saw something once, it belongs in memory.

That is not acceptable.

The fabric should prefer retaining:

* mission-relevant state
* decision-relevant evidence
* receipt-linked artifacts
* repeated friction with value
* verification history
* staged or promoted capability records
* user-approved durable context

and avoid turning every observed surface into permanent continuity residue.

### 20.6.2 Fabric retention should be intentional

Users should not feel that Francis is hoarding their world.

The memory architecture should preserve what strengthens:

* continuity
* retrieval
* evidence
* user leverage
* decision quality
* operational truth

and minimize retention that merely increases exposure.

### 20.6.3 Fabric entries should carry privacy-aware provenance

A mature system should be able to distinguish not only where memory came from, but how sensitive or limited it is.

For example:

* raw vs summarized
* redacted vs unredacted
* local-only vs federatable
* mission-bound vs reusable
* ephemeral vs durable
* approval-linked vs passive observation

This helps the fabric stay governable.

---

## 20.7 Privacy and Telemetry

Telemetry is one of the biggest places where privacy can succeed or fail.

### 20.7.1 High-signal, low-invasion is the goal

The right telemetry posture is not:

* see everything
* store everything
* decide usefulness later

It is:

* prefer high-signal sources
* keep scope narrow
* keep visibility high
* keep retention minimal
* keep redaction strong
* avoid broad ambient capture

That is how context-awareness stays compatible with privacy.

### 20.7.2 Sensing should feel understandable

The user should be able to understand why Francis knows what it knows.

A system that feels aware in mysterious ways will often feel creepy even if some permissions technically exist.

The better experience is:

> I know why Francis surfaced that. I enabled this repo, this terminal, and this mission, and the build failed.

That is privacy-compatible intelligence.

### 20.7.3 Privacy is preserved by saying no to some telemetry

Some telemetry classes are simply too invasive for default use, including:

* passwords
* password managers
* broad clipboard capture
* unrelated windows
* global keystrokes
* camera/mic without explicit enablement
* out-of-scope private folders

These boundaries matter as much as any positive capability.

---

## 20.8 Privacy and Federation

Federation increases convenience and continuity, but it also increases privacy risk if poorly governed.

### 20.8.1 Pairing is not universal replication

The fact that a node is paired must not imply that it gets broad visibility into everything the primary node can see.

Federation should remain selective.

### 20.8.2 Replication must be privacy-class aware

Different classes of state should be treated differently, for example:

* mission summary may replicate
* raw secret should not
* approval queue state may replicate
* unrelated local file state should not
* redacted continuity summary may replicate
* raw telemetry often should not

This is how multi-node continuity avoids becoming data sprawl.

### 20.8.3 Privacy must survive topology

Users should not feel that the moment Francis becomes multi-node, privacy gets blurrier.

Federation should preserve the feeling that:

* I know what each node can know
* I know what remains local
* I know what was sent where
* I can revoke nodes cleanly

That is respectful distributed design.

---

## 20.9 Privacy and Swarm

Swarm introduces internal multiplicity, which creates a new privacy challenge:

> does internal specialization cause unnecessary internal spread?

That must be prevented.

### 20.9.1 Units should see what they need, not what exists

A role-bearing unit should receive the context necessary to do its lawful work, not broad raw access just because it is “inside Francis.”

This is the internal version of least privilege.

### 20.9.2 Delegation should preserve privacy boundaries

A handoff from one unit to another should not automatically dump:

* raw unrelated artifacts
* unnecessary history
* private irrelevant details
* unredacted context beyond the target role’s need

Bounded delegation protects privacy as well as clarity.

### 20.9.3 The Swarm must not become a reason for internal overexposure

Many-unit architecture can accidentally normalize broader internal circulation of user data.

Francis must reject that drift.

---

## 20.10 Retention Must Be Minimal and Intentional

A core privacy truth for Francis is that retention is power.

What the system keeps, it can later retrieve, replicate, summarize, expose, or misuse if governance weakens.

That is why retention discipline matters so much.

### 20.10.1 Minimal retention is safer retention

Francis should retain what has durable user value, not everything it can technically capture.

Retention should be governed by questions like:

* does this strengthen continuity?
* does this support future decision quality?
* does this preserve necessary receipts?
* does this help with mission state?
* does this support validated learning or capability growth?
* is this needed for audit, rollback, or inspection?
* is raw retention necessary, or would a redacted summary suffice?

That is an intentional memory posture.

### 20.10.2 Different data classes deserve different lifetimes

Not all data should live equally long.

For example:

* ephemeral telemetry may deserve short retention
* mission receipts may deserve longer retention
* approvals may need durable lineage
* staged capability records may need promotion history
* raw sensitive artifacts may deserve minimal or no durable retention
* derived summaries may outlive raw source traces in some cases

This is how retention becomes disciplined rather than accidental.

### 20.10.3 Users should not feel trapped by system memory

The system should not feel like a one-way ratchet where everything once seen is remembered forever.

That is both emotionally wrong and architecturally risky.

---

## 20.11 Inspectability and User Control

Consent and privacy are only truly trustworthy when users can inspect and control the relevant surfaces.

### 20.11.1 The user should be able to inspect what is active

Users should be able to inspect things like:

* active scopes
* active telemetry sources
* active connectors
* paired nodes
* active mode
* active learning sessions
* retained artifact classes
* what kinds of memory are being preserved
* what kinds of summaries may replicate

This creates clarity.

### 20.11.2 The user should be able to narrow or revoke

Users should be able to:

* turn off telemetry classes
* narrow scope
* revoke node trust
* disable connectors
* stop teaching mode
* kill Pilot or Away continuation
* reduce retention categories where policy allows
* unpair surfaces that no longer feel right

A user who cannot reduce Francis is not truly in control of Francis.

### 20.11.3 Inspectability is part of the anti-creepiness model

People often tolerate powerful systems when the system feels inspectable, because inspectability lowers the fear of hidden overreach.

That is why inspectability is not just for technical users.
It is part of the emotional architecture of trust.

---

## 20.12 The Emotional Standard: Trusted, Not Creepy

This roadmap explicitly names an important outcome:

> Francis should feel trusted, not creepy.

That line deserves to be operationalized.

### 20.12.1 What “trusted” feels like

Trusted feels like:

* I know why Francis is aware of this
* I can see what is on
* it stays inside the line
* it doesn’t pry
* it doesn’t keep everything
* it doesn’t widen itself when I’m not looking
* it respects that some parts of my world are simply not its business
* it feels like a disciplined operator, not a watcher

### 20.12.2 What “creepy” feels like

Creepy feels like:

* unexplained awareness
* broad ambient sensing
* odd references to things I did not think were in scope
* hidden retention
* too much memory of low-value personal detail
* paired nodes feeling overinformed
* learning that feels like silent profiling
* approvals and controls that exist only on paper

Francis must design against that feeling directly.

### 20.12.3 The system should earn intimacy by law, not simulate it by overreach

The best kind of closeness between user and system comes from:

* reliability
* continuity
* accuracy
* respect
* lawful helpfulness
* calm presence
* visible boundaries

Not from seeing too much.

That is an important philosophical distinction.

---

## 20.13 Consent, Privacy, and Redaction Failure Modes

This section must explicitly name the ways this layer can go wrong.

### 20.13.1 Consent drifts into checkbox fiction

Symptoms:

* broad initial consent with weak real controls
* users technically agreed once but cannot inspect or narrow meaningfully
* system behavior outgrows original consent surface

Correction:

* re-center ongoing, inspectable, narrowable consent

### 20.13.2 Privacy drifts into capability absolutism

Symptoms:

* system ingests because it can
* adjacent surfaces included because they are useful
* retention justified by hypothetical future value
* node replication expands for convenience

Correction:

* re-center relevance is not permission, and possible is not justified

### 20.13.3 Redaction drifts into afterthought

Symptoms:

* raw sensitive material stored first, cleaned later
* receipts and Lens surfaces leak more than necessary
* federated summaries carry unnecessary private detail

Correction:

* re-center redaction by default and early structured extraction

### 20.13.4 Retention drifts into hoarding

Symptoms:

* too much raw memory
* unclear retention boundaries
* “maybe useful later” becomes default storage logic
* users feel the system never forgets anything

Correction:

* re-center minimal, intentional, artifact-aware retention

### 20.13.5 Privacy drifts into hidden internal spread

Symptoms:

* Swarm units receive more than they need
* paired nodes get too much context
* internal specialization becomes internal overexposure

Correction:

* re-center least-necessary context and bounded handoff

### 20.13.6 The product drifts into creepy intelligence

Symptoms:

* Lens relevance feels unexplained
* users are unsure what Francis is sensing
* memory references feel too broad or too personal
* users begin distrusting the source of system awareness

Correction:

* re-center visible sensing, scope clarity, inspectability, and respect-for-boundary design

---

## 20.14 Consent, Privacy, and Redaction Doctrine Statement

If Francis Consent, Privacy, and Redaction had to be defined in one doctrine sentence for builders, it would be this:

> Francis operates only through explicit, inspectable, narrowable consent; treats privacy as boundary respect rather than mere secret protection; applies redaction by default across sensing, memory, receipts, and federation; retains only what is intentionally valuable for continuity and governance; and is designed so that users experience its awareness as lawful, legible, and respectful rather than hidden, sticky, or creepy.

That sentence should be strong enough to reject weak privacy posture early.

---

## 20.15 Consent, Privacy, and Redaction Acceptance Criteria

Section 20 is satisfied only if the roadmap, telemetry layer, Knowledge Fabric, Lens surfaces, Federation model, Swarm handoff model, receipt system, and user controls remain aligned to all of the following:

* consent is treated as architectural and ongoing, not a one-time setup fiction
* telemetry and meaningful sensing surfaces are opt-in, inspectable, and narrowable
* overlays and system surfaces visibly indicate active sensing, control state, and relevant scope
* users can inspect active scopes, connectors, nodes, telemetry classes, and learning surfaces
* privacy is treated as boundary respect, not only secret masking
* private capture is never assumed; unrelated or highly sensitive surfaces remain off by default
* redaction is applied by default and early across storage, receipts, handbacks, Lens, federation, and memory surfaces
* the Knowledge Fabric retains intentionally valuable operational memory rather than broad raw user exhaust
* retention is minimal, purpose-linked, data-class aware, and does not default toward hoarding
* Federation preserves privacy through selective replication and local-only handling of sensitive classes
* Swarm preserves privacy through least-necessary context sharing rather than broad internal overexposure
* user control includes narrowing, revoking, disabling, and inspecting relevant access pathways
* the system feels trusted rather than creepy because awareness is legible, bounded, and respectful
* consent, privacy, and redaction failure modes are explicitly recognized and corrected during buildout
* Francis never degrades into a capability-first surveillance-adjacent system, hidden memory hoarder, or consent-thin operator layer under the excuse of becoming more context-aware


# 21.0 Capability Economy

Francis should not merely accumulate clever tricks.

It should accumulate **assets**.

That distinction matters.

A system full of one-off automations, ad hoc scripts, prompt fragments, brittle internal routines, and half-remembered useful behaviors may appear powerful for a while, but it does not compound cleanly. It becomes hard to reason about, hard to govern, hard to trust, and hard to scale. The user ends up surrounded by scattered power rather than growing leverage.

Francis must take a different path.

It must treat capability as something that can be:

* built
* staged
* tested
* documented
* risk-tiered
* promoted
* versioned
* cataloged
* reused
* inspected
* retired
* shared selectively when lawful

That is the beginning of a **Capability Economy**.

The Capability Economy is the structured system by which Francis turns useful operational behavior into governed assets that create compounding leverage over time. It is how the system stops being “smart in the moment” and starts becoming “stronger as a platform.” It is how recurring effort becomes durable power. It is how Forge output, Apprenticeship output, and domain-specific operator routines become an arsenal instead of a pile.

This section matters because the long-term value of Francis does not come only from model quality or interface design.

It comes from the fact that useful capability should not disappear after one use.

If Francis helps solve something valuable once, it should have a disciplined way to preserve that value, promote it when appropriate, and make it reusable under law.

Without a Capability Economy, Francis risks becoming:

* a high-end assistant with weak compounding
* a smart but ephemeral operator
* a large set of scripts with no coherent promotion path
* a toolbelt without asset discipline
* a Forge that builds but does not capitalize
* an Apprenticeship layer that learns but does not accumulate durable reusable value

With a Capability Economy, Francis becomes more than a single intelligence.

It becomes a platform of governed leverage.

This section defines the doctrine of the Capability Economy:

* why it matters
* what capabilities are as assets
* why internal library discipline comes first
* how versioning, testing, documentation, and promotion shape capability value
* how capabilities should be cataloged and governed
* how later sharing or marketplace ideas may emerge
* what failure modes must be blocked before the system degenerates into capability clutter or unsafe power accumulation

The central question of this section is:

> How does Francis turn useful capability into a governed, compounding asset base rather than a growing pile of disconnected tricks?

The answer is:

**capabilities as versioned, tested, documented, risk-tiered, promoted assets that live inside a coherent internal library first, and only later—if ever—expand into selective external sharing or managed distribution under the same law.**

That is the Capability Economy.

---

## 21.1 Why the Capability Economy Matters

A great deal of the long-term value in Francis comes from reusability.

A single successful run is useful.
A reusable capability is leverage.

That leverage compounds across:

* future Missions
* future users of the same managed copy
* future domains
* repeated friction points
* repeated workflows
* staged operator patterns
* repeated approvals
* continuity across time

The Capability Economy exists because Francis should not keep paying full price for solved classes of work.

### 21.1.1 One-off intelligence does not compound enough

A system can be highly intelligent in the moment and still fail to build a durable base of power.

For example, Francis might:

* solve a recurring reporting problem
* stage a reliable validation routine
* learn a reusable workflow from Apprenticeship
* build a quality remediation helper through Forge

If each of those remains only a momentary win, then the platform loses much of the deeper value.

The Capability Economy is what preserves and grows those wins.

### 21.1.2 Compounding leverage requires asset discipline

Compounding does not happen merely because useful things exist.

It happens because useful things become:

* preserved
* organized
* maintainable
* discoverable
* governable
* promotable
* reusable
* inspectable

That is why the Capability Economy is not just “lots of capabilities.”
It is discipline around capability value.

### 21.1.3 The user should feel their system getting richer

Over time, the user should be able to feel:

* Francis keeps gaining useful internal tools
* repeated problems are less repeated
* more workflows feel native
* more preparation is automatic
* more safe routines are available instantly
* more domain-specific leverage has been accumulated
* prior teaching and prior friction have become durable assets

That feeling is one of the clearest forms of product compounding.

---

## 21.2 What a Capability Is

A capability is a reusable governed unit of operational power inside Francis.

It is more than a model behavior.
More than a clever prompt.
More than a remembered trick.
More than a one-off automation.
More than an internal function with no governance envelope.

A true capability is something that can be relied on as part of the system’s arsenal.

### 21.2.1 Capability as asset

A capability should be treated like an asset because it has:

* defined usefulness
* known scope of application
* governance posture
* version history
* supporting tests
* supporting docs
* promotion state
* risk tier
* expected behavior
* maintenance implications

This is what separates a capability from an accidental convenience.

### 21.2.2 Capability is reusable power under law

A capability exists so Francis can do something again, not merely once.

Examples might include:

* a branch-safe patch staging routine
* a report-generation pack
* a validation orchestrator
* a recurring cleanup helper
* a decision-prep template
* a domain-specific ops routine
* a parameterized skill from Apprenticeship
* a monitoring summarizer
* a comparison and evidence packet builder

But in every case, reusability alone is not enough.

It must also be governed.

### 21.2.3 Capability is distinct from raw implementation

There may be many internal code paths or prompts inside Francis, but not all of them deserve to be treated as first-class capabilities in the economy.

A capability should earn that status by being:

* useful beyond one run
* structured enough to inspect
* tested enough to trust
* documented enough to review
* scoped enough to govern
* stable enough to reuse

That threshold matters.

---

## 21.3 Internal Library First

The Capability Economy must begin with an internal library.

This is one of the most important strategic choices in the whole section.

### 21.3.1 Why internal library first

It is tempting to imagine sharing, selling, or distributing capability packs early.

But if the internal capability library is not coherent first, externalization becomes dangerous and messy.

Internal library discipline forces Francis to answer:

* what is a capability?
* how is it versioned?
* how is it tested?
* how is risk described?
* how is it promoted?
* how is it cataloged?
* how is it deprecated?
* how is it inspected?
* how does it interact with mode, scope, and policy?

These questions must be solved internally before any broader economy can be real.

### 21.3.2 The library is the treasury of compounding leverage

The internal library is where the compounding base of Francis should live.

It should increasingly contain:

* Forge-promoted capabilities
* Apprenticeship-derived skills
* domain packs
* reusable validation routines
* mission helpers
* continuity helpers
* decision-prep builders
* safe execution patterns
* connector-specific utilities

This is the treasury of accumulated operator power.

### 21.3.3 Internal-first prevents premature marketplace distortion

If external sharing comes too early, builders may start optimizing for:

* flashy capabilities
* generalized packs without enough grounding
* packaging over rigor
* novelty over governance
* distribution over maintainability

Internal library first keeps the economy centered on user value and operational discipline.

---

## 21.4 Capabilities Must Be Versioned

A capability that changes without version clarity is hard to trust.

Versioning is therefore fundamental to the Capability Economy.

### 21.4.1 Why versioning matters

Versioning allows Francis and the user to know:

* which capability definition is active
* what changed between revisions
* whether a behavior regression was introduced
* which tests apply to which build
* which Missions used which version
* whether a capability should be upgraded, pinned, rolled back, or retired

This is basic asset seriousness.

### 21.4.2 Versioning supports auditability

If a capability was involved in:

* a failed run
* a surprising result
* a strong success
* a regression
* a managed-copy delta
* an approval controversy

versioning helps preserve the historical truth of what actually ran.

Without versioning, the capability layer becomes temporally blurry.

### 21.4.3 Versioning supports trust at scale

As the number of capabilities grows, users and builders need confidence that the system is not a shape-shifting pile.

Versioning helps preserve:

* stability
* diffability
* change review
* rollback clarity
* promotion lineage
* managed-copy compatibility

The larger the economy becomes, the more important this is.

---

## 21.5 Capabilities Must Be Tested

A Capability Economy without testing is not an economy of assets.

It is an economy of hopes.

### 21.5.1 Why tests matter here

A reusable capability will be trusted repeatedly.

That means failure cost can accumulate across many future uses.

Tests help answer:

* does this capability actually do what it claims?
* under what conditions does it fail?
* what edge cases are known?
* what assumptions are encoded?
* is it safe to promote?
* is it still behaving correctly after revision?

Without testing, reuse becomes a multiplier for fragility.

### 21.5.2 Test expectations should match risk and type

Not every capability needs the same test depth, but every promoted capability needs meaningful validation.

Examples might include:

* unit-like tests for stable transformation logic
* scenario tests for structured workflows
* policy-boundary tests for governed execution routines
* dry-run tests for staging helpers
* regression cases for frequently used capabilities
* adversarial cases for authority-sensitive packs

The test doctrine should be proportional, not absent.

### 21.5.3 Test results are part of the capability’s value

A promoted capability should not just exist.

It should carry its credibility with it.

That credibility includes:

* test coverage or test type
* last validation status
* known constraints
* risk tier
* promotion receipt
* version lineage

That is what makes capabilities feel real.

---

## 21.6 Capabilities Must Be Documented

Documentation is not optional.

A capability that cannot be understood should not be part of the trusted economy.

### 21.6.1 Documentation enables reviewable trust

The user or builder should be able to know:

* what the capability does
* what problem it solves
* when it should be used
* what scope it expects
* what inputs it takes
* what outputs it produces
* what risks it carries
* how it was tested
* what it should not be used for

This matters because promoted power must stay intelligible.

### 21.6.2 Documentation reduces hidden power growth

As Francis gains more assets, hidden capability becomes a real risk.

A coherent doc layer prevents the user from feeling that Francis has become more powerful in ways they can no longer track.

### 21.6.3 Documentation supports managed evolution

Good docs help with:

* maintenance
* upgrades
* deprecation
* managed-copy deployment
* troubleshooting
* approval review
* capability catalog usability

An economy of assets needs readable contracts.

---

## 21.7 Capabilities Must Be Risk-Tiered

Not all capabilities are equally safe.

The Capability Economy must reflect that.

### 21.7.1 Risk determines governance posture

A capability’s risk tier may affect:

* promotion scrutiny
* approval requirements
* allowable modes
* whether Away may use it
* which nodes may hold it
* which units may invoke it
* what verification is required
* whether human review should be standard

Without risk tiers, the economy becomes too flat and therefore too dangerous.

### 21.7.2 Risk is not only about destructiveness

Capability risk may come from many factors, including:

* mutation ability
* external side effects
* policy sensitivity
* privacy exposure
* brittle domain assumptions
* trust-critical user-visible impact
* reliance on uncertain input
* connector scope
* federated consequences

A narrow “dangerous or not” model is too crude.

### 21.7.3 Risk tiers should travel with the capability

Risk should not be an external note easily detached from the asset.

It should be part of the capability’s identity inside the economy.

---

## 21.8 Promotion Rules and Quality Standards

Capabilities must not become active simply because they exist.

Promotion remains one of the key gates in the economy.

### 21.8.1 Promotion is a trust event

To promote a capability is to say:

* this is now part of Francis’s usable arsenal
* this can influence future work
* this is available for lawful invocation under the right conditions
* this has crossed from candidate to trusted asset

That is a major threshold.

### 21.8.2 Promotion should require minimum quality material

At minimum, a promoted capability should have:

* tests
* documentation
* risk tier
* version identity
* summary
* promotion receipt
* enough inspectability to support future trust

This keeps the economy from being flooded with weak assets.

### 21.8.3 Promotion should remain explicit

The user must not later discover that a capability became part of the active system through quiet drift.

Promotion must be explicit, receipted, and reviewable.

This matters even more as the economy grows, because hidden promotion becomes one of the fastest ways to produce invisible power expansion.

---

## 21.9 The Capability Catalog

The economy needs a visible treasury.

That treasury is the capability catalog.

### 21.9.1 The catalog is not a junk drawer

A bad capability catalog becomes:

* cluttered
* redundant
* hard to inspect
* hard to search
* hard to trust
* hard to maintain
* hard to govern

The catalog should feel curated, not merely accumulated.

### 21.9.2 The catalog should expose meaningful fields

The user or builder should be able to inspect things like:

* capability name
* summary
* version
* promotion status
* risk tier
* scope or domain fit
* origin lineage
* docs
* tests
* last validation state
* deprecation or replacement status where relevant

This is what makes the catalog operationally usable.

### 21.9.3 The catalog is where leverage becomes visible

One of the most satisfying experiences in Francis should be seeing that real accumulated value exists.
