* Francis never degrades into one-domain overfit, generic mush, governance inconsistency by environment, or fragmented multi-product identity under the excuse of being versatile


# 25.0 Stage-by-Stage Build Plan

Vision without sequencing turns into drift.

Francis is too large, too ambitious, too cross-cutting, and too law-sensitive to be built as a loose pile of parallel ideas. If the roadmap remains only descriptive—if it says what Francis should become but not how that becoming should be staged—then the project will constantly risk one of the most common failures in advanced systems work:

* building powerful pieces in the wrong order
* creating internals that do not yet have lawful surfaces
* shipping capability before receipts
* adding autonomy before bounded execution
* adding execution before policy
* adding product surfaces before core truth
* adding learning before inspectability
* adding scale before continuity discipline

That is why the build plan matters so much.

The build plan is where the vision becomes executable.
It is where philosophy becomes sequence.
It is where “north star” becomes “what we build next, and why.”

This section does not exist merely to list milestones.

It exists to enforce a principle:

**Francis must be built in vertical slices of lived experience, with law and receipts present from the beginning, and with each new stage making the system more real without making it less governed.**

That means the build plan must do several things at once:

* preserve the right dependency order
* protect the law while capability grows
* make each stage user-legible
* ensure each stage has a meaningful “done looks like”
* prevent seductive but dangerous shortcuts
* keep Codex and future builders aligned around the same execution cadence

The central question of this section is:

> In what order should Francis be built so that every layer rests on real foundations, every stage produces user-visible value, and the system becomes progressively more powerful without becoming progressively less trustworthy?

The answer is:

**build Francis in staged vertical slices that first establish law, traceability, and truth, then presence, observation, continuity, capability growth, bounded autonomy, OS-layer visibility, safe execution, delegated control, away continuity, learning, grounded memory, truth discipline, adversarial hardening, specialization, federation, capability compounding, managed copies, and finally full productization.**

That is the Stage-by-Stage Build Plan.

---

## 25.1 Why Staging Matters

A system like Francis cannot be built safely by following raw excitement.

There will always be temptations to jump ahead:

* build Takeover first because it feels magical
* build Forge early because self-extension is exciting
* build Swarm because multi-agent systems look advanced
* build Federation because distributed continuity sounds powerful
* build Lens before the system underneath it is truthful
* build Away before law, receipts, and stop conditions are stable

Those temptations must be resisted.

### 25.1.1 Wrong order creates fake progress

A project can look like it is moving fast while actually increasing future fragility.

For example:

* Lens without truthful observer state creates UI theater
* Takeover without receipt discipline creates dangerous opacity
* Away without bounded reactor logic creates runaway background behavior
* Forge without capability economy creates clutter
* Apprenticeship without reviewability creates creepy learning
* Federation without scoped trust creates cloud-vague power

This is why order matters as much as ingredients.

### 25.1.2 Early stages shape the permanent character of the system

The first layers of Francis will become its reflexes.

If early stages normalize:

* weak receipts
* hidden state
* unclear mode boundaries
* vague continuity
* builder-only assumptions
* “we’ll verify later”

then those habits become very expensive to remove later.

A correct stage plan establishes the right reflexes first.

### 25.1.3 Each stage should produce real lived experience

The build plan should not feel like infrastructure for ten stages followed by “real product” at the end.

Every stage should make Francis more experientially real.

That is what “vertical slices of lived experience” means.

---

## 25.2 The Build Doctrine for All Stages

Before listing stages, the system needs a unifying build doctrine.

### 25.2.1 Build vertical slices, not isolated subsystems

Each stage should create a real end-to-end experience, not just a backend primitive.

That means a stage should ideally connect:

* truth
* law
* state
* surface
* receipts
* user meaning

rather than only adding another hidden internal.

### 25.2.2 Governance must be present from stage zero

There is no “ungoverned prototype” phase if the prototype is teaching the architecture how to behave.

Even early stages should already reflect:

* run identity
* receipt discipline
* no hidden control
* truth claims tied to evidence
* user sovereignty
* scope awareness
* clear boundaries between observe, assist, execute, and continue

### 25.2.3 Each stage must have a visible user-experience objective

A stage is not done because code exists.

It is done when a user can feel a specific new property of Francis, such as:

* grounded presence
* observer truth
* mission continuity
* staged capability growth
* bounded autonomy
* OS-layer visibility
* safe execution
* delegated action
* night-shift value
* learned alignment
* grounded memory

That lived experience objective matters.

### 25.2.4 Each stage must preserve upgradeability

No stage should paint the later system into an incoherent corner.

The build plan should therefore bias toward:

* lawful abstractions
* versionable interfaces
* receipt-bearing transitions
* strong internal contracts
* reversible decisions where possible
* explicit “done looks like” criteria

---

## 25.3 Stage 0 — Foundation & Operating System Contracts

### Goal

Make Francis safe, predictable, and auditable from day one.

This is the stage where the system learns its first laws. If this stage is weak, every later stage inherits hidden instability.

### Why this stage comes first

Before Francis becomes visible, helpful, autonomous, or powerful, it must become structurally legible to itself and to the user.

That means this stage should establish:

* repo layout
* core docs
* governance rules
* workspace journaling
* run identity propagation
* no-surprises discipline
* the earliest receipt pathways

Without this, later stages will be forced to improvise law retroactively.

### What this stage is really building

This stage is not glamorous, but it is existential.

It creates the initial contracts for:

* what a run is
* what a receipt is
* what counts as meaningful action
* how state gets journaled
* how governance is represented
* how future modules relate to shared law

It is the stage where Francis begins to become more than “code.”

### Deliverables

Core deliverables for this stage should include:

* repo layout
* core governance docs
* workspace journaling
* run_id propagation
* early receipt structures
* foundational no-hidden-control principles in code and docs

### Done looks like

Stage 0 is done when:

* all meaningful actions are journaled
* no-surprises policy is documented
* user sovereignty is explicit everywhere foundational
* run identity can survive basic internal flow
* future stages have a lawful substrate to build on

### What failure looks like here

Failure would look like:

* building services without shared run identity
* hidden mutable actions without receipts
* governance written later instead of encoded now
* unclear repository structure
* no stable contract for later modules

That must be avoided.

---

## 25.4 Stage 1 — Grounded Presence

### Goal

Francis can speak truthfully and usefully without noise.

This is the stage where Francis begins to feel like a presence rather than just a set of internals—but only a grounded one.

### Why this stage comes early

Before Francis becomes highly capable, it must learn how to exist in a way that is:

* truthful
* useful
* calm
* evidence-linked
* non-fabricated

Presence built too early without grounding becomes personality theater.
Presence built on receipts becomes trust.

### What this stage is really building

This stage establishes:

* presence state
* presence briefing
* tone and voice contract
* receipt-linked briefing surfaces
* no fabricated state in user-facing summaries

This is where Francis first starts to feel “there,” but only through grounded continuity.

### Deliverables

* presence state model
* presence briefing system
* tone and voice contract
* receipt linkage for every briefing action
* early return-to-context presentation

### Done looks like

Stage 1 is done when:

* no fabricated state appears in briefings
* briefings are grounded in local evidence
* receipts exist for every briefing-producing action
* Francis feels calm and present without theatricality

### What failure looks like here

Failure would look like:

* vague status talk
* overconfident summaries without evidence
* presence created mostly through wording rather than truth
* tone outrunning state support

That would poison later trust.

---

## 25.5 Stage 2 — Observer

### Goal

Francis can see and structure the reality of the system and workspace.

This is where Francis first becomes operationally aware.

### Why this stage follows grounded presence

Presence without observation is shallow.
Observation without prior law would be creepy or unstable.

Now that foundational law and grounded briefings exist, Francis can begin lawful observation.

### What this stage is really building

The Observer stage builds:

* probes
* anomaly scoring
* incidents
* decisions log
* observer receipts
* first truthful state formation from system signals

This is the beginning of “Francis knows what is happening.”

### Deliverables

* probes
* anomaly scoring pipeline
* incident model
* decisions log
* observer receipts
* clear truth-state links into presence

### Done looks like

Stage 2 is done when:

* incidents are evidence-backed
* scans are traceable
* observer findings are receipted
* presence can cite observer truthfully
* the system is aware without being invasive

### What failure looks like here

Failure would look like:

* noisy false awareness
* weak provenance
* incidents without evidence
* telemetry treated as truth without calibration
* sensing that is useful technically but creepy experientially

---

## 25.6 Stage 3 — Missions

### Goal

Intent becomes progress.

This is where Francis stops being only reactive to current state and begins to carry structured objectives across time.

### Why this stage matters now

Observation tells Francis what is happening.
Missions tell Francis what matters over time.

Once Francis can see and report truthfully, it needs continuity-bearing containers for work.

### What this stage is really building

This stage introduces:

* mission model
* queue and deadletter
* status transitions
* mission history
* run-ledger event linkage
* next-step continuity

This is where the system begins carrying work instead of only reacting to it.

### Deliverables

* mission model
* queue/deadletter
* mission history
* visible status transitions
* run ledger events connected to missions

### Done looks like

Stage 3 is done when:

* mission ticks are idempotent
* failures deadletter cleanly
* mission status is visible in briefings or early Lens-like surfaces
* continuity across sessions becomes real
* the user no longer has to reconstruct everything manually

### What failure looks like here

Failure would look like:

* missions as just glorified tasks
* vague status changes
* no next-step clarity
* deadletter chaos
* mission existence without true continuity value

---

## 25.7 Stage 4 — Forge

### Goal

Francis can stage new capabilities to remove friction.

Now that Francis has law, presence, observation, and missions, it can begin compounding its usefulness.

### Why Forge comes here

Forge needs evidence of repeated friction.
That evidence comes from missions, runs, and observation.

Before this point, Francis does not yet know enough about recurring pain to build good capability proposals.

### What this stage is really building

This stage builds:

* capability proposals
* stage flow
* validation flow
* promotion flow
* capability catalog beginnings
* early capability economy structure

This is where Francis first starts to remove recurring burdens rather than only helping with them.

### Deliverables

* proposal system
* stage/validate/promote flow
* capability catalog
* test/docs/risk-tier requirements
* isolation of staged outputs

### Done looks like

Stage 4 is done when:

* staged outputs have tests, docs, and risk
* nothing auto-promotes
* forge outputs are receipted
* Francis can credibly propose removing recurring friction

### What failure looks like here

Failure would look like:

* speculative auto-tool generation
* under-documented proposals
* capability clutter
* hidden power growth
* proposals unlinked to real friction evidence

---

## 25.8 Stage 5 — Event-Driven Reactor

### Goal

Francis can do bounded autonomous work without becoming a generic loop bot.

This is one of the most important architecture thresholds in the entire roadmap.

### Why this stage comes after Missions and Forge

At this point Francis already has:

* truth
* observation
* continuity objects
* capability growth beginnings

Now it needs the correct motion model.

The reactor stage ensures that future autonomy is lawful, bounded, and event-driven rather than loop-shaped.

### What this stage is really building

This stage builds:

* event queue
* dispatch model
* budgets
* decision journal
* retries/backoff/deadletter semantics
* trigger discipline

This is where Francis learns how to move without drifting.

### Deliverables

* event queue
* dispatch engine
* execution budgets
* decision journaling
* retry and deadletter model

### Done looks like

Stage 5 is done when:

* no generic infinite loops exist
* dispatches are bounded and traceable
* blocked actions queue for approval or deadletter appropriately
* the system feels composed, not constantly churning

### What failure looks like here

Failure would look like:

* perpetual background self-initiation
* unclear stop conditions
* repeated hidden retries
* difficult interruptibility
* “agent mode” vibes instead of operator discipline

---

## 25.9 Stage 6 — Lens MVP

### Goal

Francis becomes visible as an OS-layer presence.

This is the moment the user starts feeling the 4th-wall promise in product form.

### Why Lens MVP comes now

Lens must not come before:

* truth
* observer state
* mission continuity
* bounded autonomy rules

Otherwise it becomes an overlay shell around weak internals.

Now the substrate is strong enough for visibility to become meaningful.

### What this stage is really building

This stage introduces:

* command palette
* basic HUD
* mode selector
* approvals view
* incident view
* visible Pilot indicator groundwork
* signature product surface beginnings

### Deliverables

* command palette
* basic HUD layer
* mode selector
* approvals view
* incident view
* visible Pilot indicator

### Done looks like

Stage 6 is done when:

* the user can summon Francis anywhere
* Lens is helpful, not noisy
* mode visibility becomes real
* Pilot visibility groundwork is obvious
* Francis begins to feel system-resident, not tab-trapped

### What failure looks like here

Failure would look like:

* floating chat widget syndrome
* dashboard wall syndrome
* noisy overlay spam
* vague authority rendering
* sci-fi styling without operational value

---

## 25.10 Stage 7 — Telemetry MVP

### Goal

Francis understands real work context.

This is where contextual intelligence starts becoming deeply practical.

### Why telemetry follows Lens MVP

The system now has a visible place to express relevance.
Telemetry can therefore be felt in-place rather than only hidden in backend logic.

It also arrives after law, so it can be scoped properly.

### What this stage is really building

This stage adds:

* terminal connector
* git watcher
* IDE diagnostics connector
* redaction
* visible telemetry indicators
* first meaningful work-context awareness

### Deliverables

* terminal connector
* git watcher
* IDE diagnostics connector
* redaction pipeline
* sensing indicators

### Done looks like

Stage 7 is done when:

* telemetry is useful, scoped, and redacted
* action quality improves because Francis has better context
* relevance feels lawful rather than mysterious
* the user does not feel spied on

### What failure looks like here

Failure would look like:

* weak signal usefulness
* mysterious awareness
* broad capture creep
* poor redaction
* telemetry that feels invasive instead of helpful

---

## 25.11 Stage 8 — Executor Substrate

### Goal

Francis gains safe hands.

This is where Francis stops being only suggestive and becomes truly operational.

### Why this stage comes after law, missions, reactor, Lens, and telemetry

Execution is too consequential to arrive early.

By this point the system already has:

* governance foundation
* receipts
* observation
* missions
* event discipline
* visible surfaces
* contextual awareness

Now it can safely gain action.

### What this stage is really building

This stage adds:

* toolbelt
* allowlists
* branch-first workflows
* leases and idempotency
* verification hooks
* substrate-level scope enforcement

### Deliverables

* execution toolbelt
* allowlists/policy filters
* branch-first code workflows
* leases/idempotency
* verification hooks

### Done looks like

Stage 8 is done when:

* Francis can safely execute in scope
* every action leaves receipts
* dangerous commands are blocked by policy
* execution feels calm, bounded, and reviewable

### What failure looks like here

Failure would look like:

* raw shell wrapper behavior
* hidden mutation
* weak scope enforcement
* action without proof
* fragile retries and poor reversibility

---

## 25.12 Stage 9 — Takeover (Pilot Mode)

### Goal

Francis can take the wheel on command.

This is one of the most important product threshold moments in the roadmap.

### Why Takeover comes after the Executor

Takeover without a safe Executor would be dangerous theater.
Takeover without Lens would be hidden.
Takeover without receipts would be untrustworthy.

Now the system can finally become the user’s hands in a lawful way.

### What this stage is really building

This stage introduces:

* control transfer ritual
* live action feed
* panic stop
* handback summary
* visible Pilot law in product form

### Deliverables

* control transfer flow
* live action feed
* panic stop
* handback summary
* strong Pilot visibility

### Done looks like

Stage 9 is done when:

* takeover never happens implicitly
* handback always includes proof
* panic revokes control instantly
* the user experiences real delegated action without fear of hidden control

### What failure looks like here

Failure would look like:

* implicit execution drift
* sticky authority
* poor handback
* black-box action
* weak panic semantics

---

## 25.13 Stage 10 — Away Mode

### Goal

Francis keeps moving work forward while the user is away.

This is where continuity becomes one of the product’s strongest differentiators.

### Why Away comes after Takeover

Away requires:

* event-driven discipline
* missions
* execution substrate
* receipts
* bounded autonomy
* strong trust law

Without those, Away becomes runaway background behavior.

### What this stage is really building

This stage adds:

* away-safe task classes
* shift reports
* approvals queue refinement
* autonomy budgets for absence
* mission-centered return briefings

### Deliverables

* away-safe tasks
* shift reports
* approvals queue
* autonomy budgets
* return briefing flow

### Done looks like

Stage 10 is done when:

* Away creates progress, not chaos
* risky actions remain gated
* the user returns to meaningful shift reports
* the system feels like a disciplined night shift operator

### What failure looks like here

Failure would look like:

* mysterious overnight action
* silent approval erosion
* weak re-entry clarity
* lots of background work, little user value

---

## 25.14 Stage 11 — Apprenticeship

### Goal

Francis learns from how the user actually works.

Now that Francis can observe, carry missions, stage capabilities, and act safely, it can begin learning reusable patterns from demonstration.

### Why Apprenticeship comes here

Before this point, Francis lacked enough:

* lawful observation
* continuity substrate
* Forge destination
* review surfaces
* execution semantics

Now it can learn in a way that compounds into real leverage.

### What this stage is really building

This stage adds:

* teaching session UX
* replay
* generalization
* skillization
* Forge handoff from demonstrations

### Deliverables

* teaching session UX
* replay/generalization flow
* skillization artifacts
* Forge-ready outputs from demonstration

### Done looks like

Stage 11 is done when:

* the user can teach once and keep forever
* outputs are staged and governed
* learned skills are reusable and inspectable
* Apprenticeship feels collaborative, not creepy

### What failure looks like here

Failure would look like:

* macro-recorder brittleness
* vague learning
* hidden behavior adaptation
* surveillance-like passive capture

---

## 25.15 Stage 12 — Knowledge Fabric

### Goal

Francis can reason across the user’s own artifacts with citations.

This is where memory becomes operational rather than conversational.

### Why this stage comes after Missions, Forge, and Apprenticeship

By now Francis has enough meaningful artifact classes to weave into a real fabric:

* receipts
* missions
* incidents
* staged capabilities
* observations
* teaching outputs
* execution traces

The fabric now has something real to connect.

### What this stage is really building

This stage adds:

* artifact indexing
* retrieval
* local evidence citations
* retention model
* cross-artifact reasoning substrate

### Deliverables

* artifact indexing
* retrieval layer
* local evidence citation support
* retention model

### Done looks like

Stage 12 is done when:

* Francis cites local evidence
* memory becomes operational
* recommendations and summaries are grounded
* cross-artifact continuity becomes real

### What failure looks like here

Failure would look like:

* search-only behavior
* vague memory theater
* data hoarding
* weak provenance
* stale-state overclaiming

---

## 25.16 Stage 13 — Trust Calibration

### Goal

Francis knows what it knows and proves what it claims.

This stage hardens one of the deepest truth disciplines in the whole system.

### Why this stage comes now

By now Francis has many surfaces where confidence matters:

* missions
* handbacks
* Away reports
* Lens cues
* retrieval-backed claims
* capability promotion
* incident handling

Now it needs product-wide calibration coherence.

### What this stage is really building

This stage adds:

* confidence rules
* verification gates
* anti-hallucination and anti-overclaim policy
* UI and state coherence around confidence

### Deliverables

* confidence rules
* verification gates
* anti-hallucination policy
* calibrated claim logic

### Done looks like

Stage 13 is done when:

* no false “done”
* no fake certainty
* uncertainty is explicit and useful
* strong claims truly mean something

### What failure looks like here

Failure would look like:

* smooth overclaiming
* likely/confirmed mismatch
* UI confidence laundering
* stale evidence treated as current proof

---

## 25.17 Stage 14 — Adversarial Hardening

### Goal

Francis stays safe in hostile input environments.

This is where resilience becomes explicit and continuously tested.

### Why this stage follows the earlier power stages

Now that Francis can:

* retrieve
* execute
* learn
* continue
* coordinate

it must be hardened against hostile content across all those surfaces.

### What this stage is really building

This stage adds:

* injection containment
* quarantine model
* red-team tests
* policy-bypass regression suite
* whole-stack adversarial posture

### Deliverables

* injection containment
* quarantine model
* red-team suite
* bypass regressions

### Done looks like

Stage 14 is done when:

* content cannot grant authority
* policy bypasses are tested continuously
* suspicious input becomes evidence-backed review items
* the system stays governed even in hostile environments

### What failure looks like here

Failure would look like:

* content-driven authority drift
* repo or ticket prompt injection success
* hostile output steering execution
* resilience present only in one subsystem

---

## 25.18 Stage 15 — Swarm

### Goal

Francis becomes a coordinated multi-unit system.

This is where internal specialization begins to scale precision without fracturing identity.

### Why Swarm comes here

Specialization should arrive after the core laws, execution, learning, memory, and resilience are already coherent.

Otherwise multi-unit architecture just multiplies confusion.

### What this stage is really building

This stage adds:

* unit roles
* messaging envelopes
* delegation etiquette
* trace continuity
* deadletter/retry semantics across units

### Deliverables

* unit roles
* messaging model
* delegation etiquette
* trace continuity
* failure semantics

### Done looks like

Stage 15 is done when:

* units collaborate safely
* handoffs are visible and auditable
* one Francis presence is preserved
* specialization adds precision instead of chaos

### What failure looks like here

Failure would look like:

* agent zoo dynamics
* authority multiplication
* trace fragmentation
* personality fragmentation
* specialization theater

---

## 25.19 Stage 16 — Federation

### Goal

Francis can coordinate across devices and always-on nodes.

This is where continuity escapes the single machine.

### Why Federation comes after Swarm

Federation is hard enough on its own.
Combining it with internal specialization requires first having clean unit discipline and trace law.

Now the system can expand across topology without losing coherence.

### What this stage is really building

This stage adds:

* pairing
* scoped trust
* sync model
* remote approvals
* revocation
* node-attributed continuity

### Deliverables

* pairing flow
* scoped trust model
* sync model
* remote approval support
* revocation surfaces

### Done looks like

Stage 16 is done when:

* workstation sleep does not destroy continuity
* remote approval is safe and traceable
* raw private data does not leak across nodes
* multi-device Francis still feels like one governed system

### What failure looks like here

Failure would look like:

* cloud vagueness
* hidden trust expansion
* over-replication
* stale-state confusion
* node-action ambiguity

---

## 25.20 Stage 17 — Capability Economy

### Goal

Capabilities become compounding leverage.

This is where Francis stops merely having generated capabilities and starts managing them as a true asset base.

### Why this stage comes after Forge, Apprenticeship, and Federation maturity

By now Francis has enough capability-producing pathways and enough platform structure that a real asset economy can emerge coherently.

### What this stage is really building

This stage adds:

* versioned packs
* promotion rules
* quality standards
* internal library discipline
* optional future marketplace architecture

### Deliverables

* versioned packs
* promotion rules
* quality standards
* capability library coherence

### Done looks like

Stage 17 is done when:

* packs are reusable assets
* governance travels with the pack
* the catalog feels like an arsenal, not a junk drawer
* compounding leverage becomes visible to the user

### What failure looks like here

Failure would look like:

* script pile growth
* hidden capability sprawl
* weak versioning
* capability clutter
* no durable compounding feel

---

## 25.21 Stage 18 — Managed Copies Platform

### Goal

Turn Francis into a service business without giving away the core.

This is where the business model becomes concrete in architecture.

### Why this stage comes after core platform maturity

Managed copies require mature:

* governance
* Federation
* capability economy
* rogue handling
* versioning
* supportable product surfaces

Without those, commercialization would force bad shortcuts.

### What this stage is really building

This stage adds:

* copy creation process
* isolation rules
* federated delta handling
* rogue kill/replace flows
* SLA framework beginnings

### Deliverables

* copy creation
* isolation rules
* safe delta model
* rogue recovery
* SLA framework

### Done looks like

Stage 18 is done when:

* customer instances are isolated
* global core improves through safe signals
* rogue instances can be detected and replaced
* the business model is structurally aligned to the product law

### What failure looks like here

Failure would look like:

* core surrender
* privacy-weak pooling
* uncontrolled forks
* support chaos
* invisible vendor power

---

## 25.22 Stage 19 — Productization

### Goal

Francis becomes a stable daily-driver OS layer.

This is the final stage of making the platform feel truly machine-resident and product-real.

### Why Productization is last, not first

Productization before core maturity becomes a polished shell over unstable truth.

Now that the architecture is real, the product can embody it fully.

### What this stage is really building

This stage adds or solidifies:

* startup/install/update behavior
* tray icon
* hotkeys
* crash recovery
* export/import
* machine-resident lifecycle dignity

### Deliverables

* startup/install/update flows
* tray icon
* hotkey summon
* crash recovery
* export/import

### Done looks like

Stage 19 is done when:

* Francis feels native
* reliability is boring and strong
* the user can turn it on or off without fear
* the system no longer feels like a build artifact

### What failure looks like here

Failure would look like:

* polished app shell over weak internals
* brittle updates
* shaky startup
* poor recovery
* builder-only usability hidden under UI polish

---

## 25.23 Why This Sequence Works

The stages above are not arbitrary.

They create a specific dependency chain:

* law before power
* truth before presence
* observation before continuity
* continuity before compounding
* reactor before autonomy
* visibility before deep delegation
* execution before takeover
* boundedness before away work
* learning before durable reuse
* memory before grounded synthesis
* calibration before trust-sensitive scale
* hardening before expansion
* specialization before topology
* topology before commercialization
* productization after the system is actually worthy of embodiment

That order is one of the strongest strategic parts of the roadmap.

---

## 25.24 Stage-by-Stage Build Plan Doctrine Statement

If the Francis Stage-by-Stage Build Plan had to be defined in one doctrine sentence for builders, it would be this:

> Francis must be built in governance-first vertical slices that establish law, truth, receipts, and continuity before progressively adding observation, missions, capability growth, bounded autonomy, OS-layer presence, safe execution, delegated control, away continuity, learning, grounded memory, calibration, hardening, specialization, federation, compounding capability assets, managed-copy business structure, and final machine-resident productization—so each stage makes the system more real without making it less trustworthy.

That sentence should be strong enough to reject seductive but dangerous build-order shortcuts.

---

## 25.25 Stage-by-Stage Build Plan Acceptance Criteria

Section 25 is satisfied only if the roadmap, build cadence, Codex instructions, architectural sequencing, and release planning remain aligned to all of the following:

* Francis is built in staged vertical slices of lived experience rather than disconnected infrastructure piles
* governance, run identity, journaling, and receipt discipline are established first and remain present throughout all stages
* each stage has a clear user-experience objective in addition to technical deliverables
* the sequence preserves the dependency order of law before power, truth before presence, boundedness before autonomy, and safe execution before delegated control
* every stage has an explicit “done looks like” definition grounded in real system behavior
* Lens is introduced only after the underlying truth and continuity layers are strong enough to support meaningful visibility
* Takeover and Away are introduced only after bounded execution, receipts, verification, and control discipline are real
* Apprenticeship, Forge, Knowledge Fabric, and Capability Economy build on one another instead of arriving as isolated novelty layers
* Swarm and Federation arrive only after core governance, trace continuity, and operator law are strong enough to survive specialization and topology
* Managed Copies are introduced only after the platform is mature enough to commercialize without surrendering the core or weakening trust
* Productization is treated as the embodiment of a real system, not as superficial polish over fragile internals
* the build plan actively resists stage-order shortcuts that would create fake progress, hidden fragility, or trust debt

# 26.0 Quality Gates Across All Stages

A roadmap this ambitious cannot rely on taste, optimism, or “it feels pretty good” as its standard of readiness.

Francis is too powerful, too cross-cutting, too governance-sensitive, and too trust-dependent for that.

Every stage in the build plan must pass through explicit **Quality Gates**.

These gates are not bureaucratic ceremony.
They are not process theater.
They are not a checklist culture imported from generic enterprise habits.

They are the discipline that prevents Francis from becoming:

* powerful but sloppy
* polished but ungrounded
* autonomous but unbounded
* helpful but unverified
* impressive but unsafe
* productized but untrustworthy

Quality Gates are how the roadmap protects itself from its own ambition.

Because Francis is not just another software project. It is a system that aims to:

* observe real work
* preserve continuity
* act in the environment
* carry missions
* stage capabilities
* learn from demonstration
* continue while the user is away
* coordinate across units and nodes
* become a daily-layer operator

A system with that kind of reach cannot be allowed to “mostly work.”

It must earn readiness in multiple dimensions at once.

That is why every stage must satisfy three broad categories of gates:

* **Code Quality**
* **Safety Quality**
* **Product Quality**

These categories matter because a stage can fail in very different ways:

* the code may be broken
* the code may run but violate law
* the law may hold but the user experience may still be misleading
* the feature may work locally but fail as a product surface
* the feature may look polished but be weakly auditable
* the feature may feel magical but be dangerously under-tested

Quality Gates exist so none of those failure modes can quietly pass as progress.

The central question of this section is:

> What must every Francis stage prove before it can be considered real, trustworthy progress instead of unstable accumulation?

The answer is:

**every stage must satisfy code integrity, safety integrity, and product integrity, with explicit checks for correctness, scope discipline, adversarial resilience, receipt completeness, and user-legible behavior.**

That is the Quality Gates doctrine.

---

## 26.1 Why Quality Gates Matter

The more ambitious the system, the more dangerous vague readiness becomes.

A small script can sometimes get away with soft standards.
Francis cannot.

### 26.1.1 Without gates, momentum becomes deceptive

A roadmap like this can create a strong feeling of progress even when the underlying system is accumulating hidden debt.

For example:

* a new Lens surface appears, but its backing state is weak
* a mission system exists, but deadletter behavior is broken
* a takeover flow looks impressive, but handback receipts are incomplete
* a capability gets promoted, but tests are stale
* a node pairs successfully, but trust narrowing is weak
* Away Mode generates output, but approval boundaries are porous

Without gates, these problems become easy to rationalize away because the system “basically works.”

That is how trust debt forms.

### 26.1.2 Gates protect the character of the system

Quality gates are not only about preventing bugs.

They preserve Francis’s identity.

They keep the build aligned with:

* truthfulness
* bounded execution
* explicit authority
* user sovereignty
* no hidden control
* no fabricated state
* no unreceipted action
* no silent scope expansion

Without gates, those principles become aspirations instead of enforced build realities.

### 26.1.3 Gates make stage completion meaningful

A stage is not done because the code merged.

A stage is done because it passed the right standards.

This matters because the roadmap is not trying to produce:

* lots of code
* lots of services
* lots of screens
* lots of capabilities

It is trying to produce **a trustworthy operator layer**.

Quality Gates are what make “done” mean something.

---

## 26.2 The Three Gate Families

Every stage must satisfy three quality families:

* Code Quality
* Safety Quality
* Product Quality

These are complementary, not interchangeable.

### 26.2.1 Code Quality

Code Quality answers:

* does it work mechanically?
* is it structurally sound?
* does it pass basic correctness checks?
* is it maintainable enough to support future growth?

This is necessary, but not sufficient.

A feature can be technically correct and still be unsafe or misleading.

### 26.2.2 Safety Quality

Safety Quality answers:

* does it obey law?
* does it stay in scope?
* does it resist adversarial misuse?
* does it preserve receipts and control boundaries?
* does it avoid hidden power?

This is what keeps Francis from becoming dangerous as it becomes more capable.

### 26.2.3 Product Quality

Product Quality answers:

* does the user actually experience the intended value?
* is the feature legible?
* are control boundaries understandable?
* is the system honest in the way it presents itself?
* does the surface match the doctrine?

This is what keeps Francis from becoming technically rich but experientially wrong.

---

## 26.3 Code Quality Gates

Code Quality must be strong enough that later growth does not rest on brittle foundations.

The roadmap already names several baseline gates. They deserve fuller framing.

### 26.3.1 Lint and static hygiene

Francis should maintain baseline lint integrity, including checks such as:

* `ruff check .`
* no broken imports
* consistent code hygiene
* workspace/runtime exclusions where appropriate

Linting matters because it reduces:

* silent mistakes
* inconsistency
* readability decay
* accidental fragility

For a growing platform, this is basic discipline.

### 26.3.2 Test execution

At minimum, the project should pass its relevant automated test suite, such as:

* `pytest`
* targeted scenario or integration tests
* stage-specific regression tests

This is foundational because Francis’s behavior will become increasingly interconnected.

A broken assumption in one stage can quietly poison later stages without broad testing discipline.

### 26.3.3 Type checks where configured

Type checking matters more as the system grows in:

* orchestration complexity
* capability catalog size
* event routing
* mission state propagation
* Federation and Swarm interactions
* receipt structure richness

Where type checking is configured, it should pass cleanly.

This is especially important in systems that carry structured contracts across many layers.

### 26.3.4 Runtime integrity

Code Quality also includes making sure the stage actually functions in its intended runtime context.

That means avoiding a situation where:

* tests pass in abstraction
* but runtime assumptions break
* environment handling is brittle
* service boundaries drift
* packaging or startup assumptions fail silently

A stage is not code-quality complete if it only passes in a lab-like context.

### 26.3.5 Internal contract stability

As Francis grows, internal contracts become increasingly important.

Code Quality must therefore include the stability of interfaces such as:

* run and receipt models
* mission state contracts
* capability metadata contracts
* node trust or pairing contracts
* Lens-facing state surfaces
* event queue envelopes
* handoff trace structures

This is not just about syntax.
It is about whether the stage deepens the architecture cleanly.

---

## 26.4 Safety Quality Gates

Safety Quality is one of the defining features of Francis as a category.

It must be tested explicitly, not assumed from good intentions.

### 26.4.1 Policy tests

Every stage that touches decision or action should prove that core policy law still holds.

Examples include verifying that:

* mode boundaries remain real
* approvals are still required where expected
* policy-denied actions do not quietly proceed
* promoted capabilities still obey invocation law
* node trust rules still apply
* content does not gain authority

This is the backbone of safety validation.

### 26.4.2 Scope escape tests

Scope is one of the strongest laws in the roadmap, so it must be tested like one.

Stage completion should include proving that Francis cannot silently:

* touch out-of-scope files
* cross into adjacent repos
* widen app automation surfaces
* leak across unapproved nodes
* route around scope through delegation
* infer permission from relevance

Scope escape tests should exist anywhere scope-bearing logic exists.

### 26.4.3 Adversarial tests

As the system becomes richer, adversarial testing becomes non-negotiable.

Relevant tests may include:

* prompt injection attempts in repos/docs
* malicious ticket language
* misleading terminal output
* content-based policy-bypass attempts
* injected fake authority
* attempts to induce secret access
* attempts to widen scope through artifact content

A stage that adds new input surfaces should expand adversarial coverage accordingly.

### 26.4.4 Receipt completeness checks

A powerful system without receipts is not governable.

Safety Quality must therefore verify that meaningful actions produce the required traceability such as:

* run_id
* action lineage
* verification outcome
* artifacts
* mission linkage where relevant
* approval linkage where relevant
* summary state

If receipts are incomplete, the system is claiming more than it can responsibly prove.

### 26.4.5 Control-path integrity

Safety also includes verifying that users can still:

* stop
* revoke
* narrow
* inspect
* deny
* kill active authority
* collapse Pilot or Away into safer state

This is especially important for:

* Takeover
* Away Mode
* Federation
* node trust
* execution substrate changes
* Swarm coordination

A feature is not safe merely because it runs inside law; it must also remain interruptible and reversible where the doctrine requires.

---

## 26.5 Product Quality Gates

A technically correct and policy-compliant system can still fail if the user experience is misleading, noisy, or hard to trust.

That is why Product Quality must be gated independently.

### 26.5.1 The user-experience objective must be visible

Every stage in the roadmap has a lived-experience goal.

Product Quality must verify that the stage actually makes that new property visible to the user.

Examples:

* Grounded Presence should feel grounded
* Missions should reduce reconstruction cost
* Lens should feel in-place rather than detached
* Takeover should feel visible and interruptible
* Away should feel like a disciplined night shift
* Apprenticeship should feel like teach-once leverage, not silent recording

A stage that only exists internally has not fully landed.

### 26.5.2 No hidden control

The product surface must never hide meaningful power in the name of elegance or simplicity.

That means Product Quality must check whether the user can actually tell:

* what mode is active
* what sensing is active
* whether Pilot is active
* whether Away is active
* what needs approval
* what changed
* what is staged versus complete

If those truths are blurred, the product surface is misrepresenting the system.

### 26.5.3 No ambiguity about what Francis can and cannot do

One of the most dangerous UX failures is capability ambiguity.

The user should not feel unsure about:

* whether Francis is only observing or actively acting
* whether a step was staged or applied
* whether a capability is active or only proposed
* whether a mission is blocked or complete
* whether a node is paired and trusted
* whether a shift report reflects confirmed or likely state

Product Quality must verify clarity around these edges.

### 26.5.4 Calmness and discipline of surface

Francis should feel:

* calm
* competent
* bounded
* non-theatrical
* easy to inspect
* easy to correct

A stage that technically works but feels:

* noisy
* magical in the wrong way
* overconfident
* creepy
* confusing
* overwhelming

has failed product quality even if the code and policy tests pass.

### 26.5.5 Recovery and re-entry quality

For stages involving continuity, product quality must also assess re-entry:

* does the user understand what happened?
* can they pick up work quickly?
* do they receive meaningful summary and next-step clarity?
* does the system reduce cognitive drag rather than add it?

This is especially important for:

* Missions
* Away Mode
* crash recovery
* Takeover handback
* Federation

---

## 26.6 Quality Gates Must Scale with Stage Power

Not every stage requires the same intensity of every check, but every stage requires all three families.

The depth of gating should scale with the amount of power introduced.

### 26.6.1 Early stages emphasize structural truth

In early stages like Foundation, Presence, and Observer, the gates may emphasize:

* contract correctness
* journaling
* receipt integrity
* truthfulness
* no fabricated state
* clarity of briefings
* observation provenance

These are the earliest forms of product law.

### 26.6.2 Middle stages emphasize continuity and boundedness

In stages like Missions, Forge, Reactor, Lens, and Telemetry, the gates may emphasize:

* state transitions
* idempotency
* deadletter behavior
* mode visibility
* scope discipline
* redaction
* proposal quality
* relevance without creepiness

This is where power begins becoming more experiential.

### 26.6.3 Later stages emphasize execution trust

In stages like Executor, Takeover, Away, Apprenticeship, and Federation, gates must become even stricter around:

* interruptibility
* verification
* authority visibility
* stale-state handling
* node trust
* approval preservation
* live action clarity
* replay and inspectability
* adversarial behavior
* cross-surface trace continuity

The more real the power, the stronger the gates.

---

## 26.7 Quality Gates as Release Culture

Quality Gates should not be a one-time checklist before “launch.”

They should become part of Francis’s build culture.

### 26.7.1 Every stage should inherit the gate discipline

Builders should expect that a stage is not complete until it passes:

* code quality
* safety quality
* product quality

That expectation should become normal.

### 26.7.2 Gates should resist charisma and builder excitement

A common failure mode in ambitious projects is falling in love with the new thing:

* the cool overlay
* the clever Forge demo
* the smooth takeover video
* the autonomous-feeling behavior
* the multi-node orchestration

Quality Gates exist partly to resist charisma.

A feature should not pass because it feels impressive.
It should pass because it is real, safe, and product-honest.

### 26.7.3 Gates should produce builder humility

One of the best side effects of strong gates is that they teach the team not to overclaim.

That matters because Francis itself is supposed to model calibrated truth.

The build culture should mirror that discipline.

---

## 26.8 How Quality Gates Interact with Receipts

Receipts are not just one more artifact.
They are one of the core validation tools for the gates themselves.

### 26.8.1 Receipts prove work happened

A stage that claims:

* action occurred
* progress occurred
* validation occurred
* promotion occurred
* mission advanced
* handback completed
* Away work happened

must be able to support those claims through receipts.

This is true at both runtime and build-quality level.

### 26.8.2 Receipt discipline is itself a gate

A stage that introduces meaningful action but cannot produce complete receipts has not passed quality.

That is especially true for:

* execution
* Takeover
* Away
* capability promotion
* Federation handoff
* Swarm delegation

### 26.8.3 Receipt quality supports debugging and trust

Good receipts are not only for audits.

They are also how builders debug failures and how users trust what Francis says happened.

A weak receipt layer weakens all three gate families at once.

---

## 26.9 How Quality Gates Interact with Codex Build Cadence

Because the roadmap is intended to guide actual build execution, the gates must fit the implementation cadence.

### 26.9.1 The cadence should always include gate completion

Every implementation cycle should naturally end with something like:

1. restate the user-experience objective
2. name the affected modules/files
3. implement coherent full-file changes
4. run quality gates
5. return receipts

That final structure matters because it keeps quality from becoming optional.

### 26.9.2 “Return receipts” is part of gate closure

The gate is not fully passed until the implementation returns evidence of what changed and what passed.

This keeps builder behavior aligned with Francis’s own product law.

### 26.9.3 Codex work must avoid shipping gate debt forward

A dangerous habit would be:

* “we’ll fix the tests later”
* “we’ll add scope tests next stage”
* “we’ll tighten receipts after the demo”
* “we’ll improve product clarity after the core behavior works”

That kind of debt compounds badly in a system like Francis.

The cadence must resist it.

---

## 26.10 Quality Gate Failure Modes

This section must explicitly name the ways gate culture can go wrong.

### 26.10.1 Gates drift into checkbox theater

Symptoms:

* tests run, but do not meaningfully validate the feature
* gates exist as ritual, not as protection
* everyone assumes “green” means “good” even when the wrong things were tested

Correction:

* re-center gates around real doctrine-bearing risk and user experience

### 26.10.2 Gates drift into code-only validation

Symptoms:

* lint/tests pass
* but the feature still weakens governance or confuses users
* product-surface truth is not being checked

Correction:

* re-center Safety Quality and Product Quality as equal gate families

### 26.10.3 Gates drift into slow bureaucracy

Symptoms:

* gates become heavy process
* builders fear iteration
* quality becomes paperwork rather than useful protection

Correction:

* re-center gates as sharp, meaningful checks tied directly to system law and stage outcomes

### 26.10.4 Gates drift into uneven enforcement

Symptoms:

* glamorous features get looser gates
* boring infrastructure gets held to higher standards
* power-heavy stages slip through on charisma

Correction:

* re-center stronger gates for stronger power, not weaker

### 26.10.5 Gates drift into post-hoc rationalization

Symptoms:

* feature already treated as done
* gates run only to justify it afterward
* failing signals are explained away to preserve momentum

Correction:

* re-center gates as real decision points, not retrospective decoration

### 26.10.6 Gates drift into non-product thinking

Symptoms:

* internals pass
* doctrine passes on paper
* but the user cannot actually feel the intended new property
* the system is growing, but not becoming more experientially real

Correction:

* re-center lived-experience objectives in Product Quality

---

## 26.11 Quality Gates Doctrine Statement

If Francis Quality Gates had to be defined in one doctrine sentence for builders, it would be this:

> Every Francis stage must pass explicit Code Quality, Safety Quality, and Product Quality gates—covering correctness, scope and policy discipline, adversarial and receipt integrity, and user-legible lived experience—so that no new power, visibility, continuity, or autonomy enters the system without being mechanically sound, lawfully bounded, and honestly expressed to the user.

That sentence should be strong enough to reject weak “good enough for now” culture early.

---

## 26.12 Quality Gates Acceptance Criteria

Section 26 is satisfied only if the roadmap, build cadence, CI discipline, runtime testing, product review, and release readiness model remain aligned to all of the following:

* every stage is gated by Code Quality, Safety Quality, and Product Quality rather than code checks alone
* code quality includes lint, test execution, type checks where configured, runtime integrity, and stable internal contracts
* safety quality includes policy tests, scope escape tests, adversarial tests where relevant, receipt completeness, and control-path integrity
* product quality verifies that the user-experience objective of the stage is actually visible, understandable, and aligned with the doctrine
* no stage is considered complete merely because code merged or demos well
* stronger stages with greater power receive proportionally stronger gate expectations
* receipt discipline is treated as a cross-cutting quality requirement rather than an optional audit add-on
* gate culture resists charisma, novelty, and schedule pressure when they conflict with operator law
* the build cadence always includes running gates and returning receipts
* gate failure modes are explicitly recognized and corrected during buildout
* Francis never degrades into a system that is technically impressive but weakly verified, under-governed, or product-misleading under the excuse of moving fast


# 26.0 Quality Gates Across All Stages

A roadmap this ambitious cannot rely on taste, optimism, or “it feels pretty good” as its standard of readiness.

Francis is too powerful, too cross-cutting, too governance-sensitive, and too trust-dependent for that.

Every stage in the build plan must pass through explicit **Quality Gates**.

These gates are not bureaucratic ceremony.
They are not process theater.
They are not a checklist culture imported from generic enterprise habits.

They are the discipline that prevents Francis from becoming:

* powerful but sloppy
* polished but ungrounded
* autonomous but unbounded
* helpful but unverified
* impressive but unsafe
* productized but untrustworthy

Quality Gates are how the roadmap protects itself from its own ambition.

Because Francis is not just another software project. It is a system that aims to:

* observe real work
* preserve continuity
* act in the environment
* carry missions
* stage capabilities
* learn from demonstration
* continue while the user is away
* coordinate across units and nodes
* become a daily-layer operator

A system with that kind of reach cannot be allowed to “mostly work.”

It must earn readiness in multiple dimensions at once.

That is why every stage must satisfy three broad categories of gates:

* **Code Quality**
* **Safety Quality**
* **Product Quality**

These categories matter because a stage can fail in very different ways:

* the code may be broken
* the code may run but violate law
* the law may hold but the user experience may still be misleading
* the feature may work locally but fail as a product surface
* the feature may look polished but be weakly auditable
* the feature may feel magical but be dangerously under-tested

Quality Gates exist so none of those failure modes can quietly pass as progress.

The central question of this section is:

> What must every Francis stage prove before it can be considered real, trustworthy progress instead of unstable accumulation?

The answer is:

**every stage must satisfy code integrity, safety integrity, and product integrity, with explicit checks for correctness, scope discipline, adversarial resilience, receipt completeness, and user-legible behavior.**

That is the Quality Gates doctrine.

---

## 26.1 Why Quality Gates Matter

The more ambitious the system, the more dangerous vague readiness becomes.

A small script can sometimes get away with soft standards.
Francis cannot.

### 26.1.1 Without gates, momentum becomes deceptive

A roadmap like this can create a strong feeling of progress even when the underlying system is accumulating hidden debt.

For example:

* a new Lens surface appears, but its backing state is weak
* a mission system exists, but deadletter behavior is broken
* a takeover flow looks impressive, but handback receipts are incomplete
* a capability gets promoted, but tests are stale
* a node pairs successfully, but trust narrowing is weak
* Away Mode generates output, but approval boundaries are porous

Without gates, these problems become easy to rationalize away because the system “basically works.”

That is how trust debt forms.

### 26.1.2 Gates protect the character of the system

Quality gates are not only about preventing bugs.

They preserve Francis’s identity.

They keep the build aligned with:

* truthfulness
* bounded execution
* explicit authority
* user sovereignty
* no hidden control
* no fabricated state
* no unreceipted action
* no silent scope expansion

Without gates, those principles become aspirations instead of enforced build realities.

### 26.1.3 Gates make stage completion meaningful

A stage is not done because the code merged.

A stage is done because it passed the right standards.

This matters because the roadmap is not trying to produce:

* lots of code
* lots of services
* lots of screens
* lots of capabilities

It is trying to produce **a trustworthy operator layer**.

Quality Gates are what make “done” mean something.

---

## 26.2 The Three Gate Families

Every stage must satisfy three quality families:

* Code Quality
* Safety Quality
* Product Quality

These are complementary, not interchangeable.

### 26.2.1 Code Quality

Code Quality answers:

* does it work mechanically?
* is it structurally sound?
* does it pass basic correctness checks?
* is it maintainable enough to support future growth?

This is necessary, but not sufficient.

A feature can be technically correct and still be unsafe or misleading.

### 26.2.2 Safety Quality

Safety Quality answers:

* does it obey law?
* does it stay in scope?
* does it resist adversarial misuse?
* does it preserve receipts and control boundaries?
* does it avoid hidden power?

This is what keeps Francis from becoming dangerous as it becomes more capable.

### 26.2.3 Product Quality

Product Quality answers:

* does the user actually experience the intended value?
* is the feature legible?
* are control boundaries understandable?
* is the system honest in the way it presents itself?
* does the surface match the doctrine?

This is what keeps Francis from becoming technically rich but experientially wrong.

---

## 26.3 Code Quality Gates

Code Quality must be strong enough that later growth does not rest on brittle foundations.

The roadmap already names several baseline gates. They deserve fuller framing.

### 26.3.1 Lint and static hygiene

Francis should maintain baseline lint integrity, including checks such as:

* `ruff check .`
* no broken imports
* consistent code hygiene
* workspace/runtime exclusions where appropriate

Linting matters because it reduces:

* silent mistakes
* inconsistency
* readability decay
* accidental fragility

For a growing platform, this is basic discipline.

### 26.3.2 Test execution

At minimum, the project should pass its relevant automated test suite, such as:

* `pytest`
* targeted scenario or integration tests
* stage-specific regression tests

This is foundational because Francis’s behavior will become increasingly interconnected.

A broken assumption in one stage can quietly poison later stages without broad testing discipline.

### 26.3.3 Type checks where configured

Type checking matters more as the system grows in:

* orchestration complexity
* capability catalog size
* event routing
* mission state propagation
* Federation and Swarm interactions
* receipt structure richness

Where type checking is configured, it should pass cleanly.

This is especially important in systems that carry structured contracts across many layers.

### 26.3.4 Runtime integrity

Code Quality also includes making sure the stage actually functions in its intended runtime context.

That means avoiding a situation where:

* tests pass in abstraction
* but runtime assumptions break
* environment handling is brittle
* service boundaries drift
* packaging or startup assumptions fail silently

A stage is not code-quality complete if it only passes in a lab-like context.

### 26.3.5 Internal contract stability

As Francis grows, internal contracts become increasingly important.

Code Quality must therefore include the stability of interfaces such as:

* run and receipt models
* mission state contracts
* capability metadata contracts
* node trust or pairing contracts
* Lens-facing state surfaces
* event queue envelopes
* handoff trace structures

This is not just about syntax.
It is about whether the stage deepens the architecture cleanly.

---

## 26.4 Safety Quality Gates

Safety Quality is one of the defining features of Francis as a category.

It must be tested explicitly, not assumed from good intentions.

### 26.4.1 Policy tests

Every stage that touches decision or action should prove that core policy law still holds.

Examples include verifying that:

* mode boundaries remain real
* approvals are still required where expected
* policy-denied actions do not quietly proceed
* promoted capabilities still obey invocation law
* node trust rules still apply
* content does not gain authority

This is the backbone of safety validation.

### 26.4.2 Scope escape tests

Scope is one of the strongest laws in the roadmap, so it must be tested like one.

Stage completion should include proving that Francis cannot silently:

* touch out-of-scope files
* cross into adjacent repos
* widen app automation surfaces
* leak across unapproved nodes
* route around scope through delegation
* infer permission from relevance

Scope escape tests should exist anywhere scope-bearing logic exists.

### 26.4.3 Adversarial tests

As the system becomes richer, adversarial testing becomes non-negotiable.

Relevant tests may include:

* prompt injection attempts in repos/docs
* malicious ticket language
* misleading terminal output
* content-based policy-bypass attempts
* injected fake authority
* attempts to induce secret access
* attempts to widen scope through artifact content

A stage that adds new input surfaces should expand adversarial coverage accordingly.

### 26.4.4 Receipt completeness checks

A powerful system without receipts is not governable.

Safety Quality must therefore verify that meaningful actions produce the required traceability such as:

* run_id
* action lineage
* verification outcome
* artifacts
* mission linkage where relevant
* approval linkage where relevant
* summary state

If receipts are incomplete, the system is claiming more than it can responsibly prove.

### 26.4.5 Control-path integrity

Safety also includes verifying that users can still:

* stop
* revoke
* narrow
* inspect
* deny
* kill active authority
* collapse Pilot or Away into safer state

This is especially important for:

* Takeover
* Away Mode
* Federation
* node trust
* execution substrate changes
* Swarm coordination

A feature is not safe merely because it runs inside law; it must also remain interruptible and reversible where the doctrine requires.

---

## 26.5 Product Quality Gates

A technically correct and policy-compliant system can still fail if the user experience is misleading, noisy, or hard to trust.

That is why Product Quality must be gated independently.

### 26.5.1 The user-experience objective must be visible

Every stage in the roadmap has a lived-experience goal.

Product Quality must verify that the stage actually makes that new property visible to the user.

Examples:

* Grounded Presence should feel grounded
* Missions should reduce reconstruction cost
* Lens should feel in-place rather than detached
* Takeover should feel visible and interruptible
* Away should feel like a disciplined night shift
* Apprenticeship should feel like teach-once leverage, not silent recording

A stage that only exists internally has not fully landed.

### 26.5.2 No hidden control

The product surface must never hide meaningful power in the name of elegance or simplicity.

That means Product Quality must check whether the user can actually tell:

* what mode is active
* what sensing is active
* whether Pilot is active
* whether Away is active
* what needs approval
* what changed
* what is staged versus complete

If those truths are blurred, the product surface is misrepresenting the system.

### 26.5.3 No ambiguity about what Francis can and cannot do

One of the most dangerous UX failures is capability ambiguity.

The user should not feel unsure about:

* whether Francis is only observing or actively acting
* whether a step was staged or applied
* whether a capability is active or only proposed
* whether a mission is blocked or complete
* whether a node is paired and trusted
* whether a shift report reflects confirmed or likely state

Product Quality must verify clarity around these edges.

### 26.5.4 Calmness and discipline of surface

Francis should feel:

* calm
* competent
* bounded
* non-theatrical
* easy to inspect
* easy to correct

A stage that technically works but feels:

* noisy
* magical in the wrong way
* overconfident
* creepy
* confusing
* overwhelming

has failed product quality even if the code and policy tests pass.

### 26.5.5 Recovery and re-entry quality

For stages involving continuity, product quality must also assess re-entry:

* does the user understand what happened?
* can they pick up work quickly?
* do they receive meaningful summary and next-step clarity?
* does the system reduce cognitive drag rather than add it?

This is especially important for:

* Missions
* Away Mode
* crash recovery
* Takeover handback
* Federation

---

## 26.6 Quality Gates Must Scale with Stage Power

Not every stage requires the same intensity of every check, but every stage requires all three families.

The depth of gating should scale with the amount of power introduced.

### 26.6.1 Early stages emphasize structural truth

In early stages like Foundation, Presence, and Observer, the gates may emphasize:

* contract correctness
* journaling
* receipt integrity
* truthfulness
* no fabricated state
* clarity of briefings
* observation provenance

These are the earliest forms of product law.

### 26.6.2 Middle stages emphasize continuity and boundedness

In stages like Missions, Forge, Reactor, Lens, and Telemetry, the gates may emphasize:

* state transitions
* idempotency
* deadletter behavior
* mode visibility
* scope discipline
* redaction
* proposal quality
* relevance without creepiness

This is where power begins becoming more experiential.

### 26.6.3 Later stages emphasize execution trust

In stages like Executor, Takeover, Away, Apprenticeship, and Federation, gates must become even stricter around:

* interruptibility
* verification
* authority visibility
* stale-state handling
* node trust
* approval preservation
* live action clarity
* replay and inspectability
* adversarial behavior
* cross-surface trace continuity

The more real the power, the stronger the gates.

---

## 26.7 Quality Gates as Release Culture

Quality Gates should not be a one-time checklist before “launch.”

They should become part of Francis’s build culture.

### 26.7.1 Every stage should inherit the gate discipline

Builders should expect that a stage is not complete until it passes:

* code quality
* safety quality
* product quality

That expectation should become normal.

### 26.7.2 Gates should resist charisma and builder excitement

A common failure mode in ambitious projects is falling in love with the new thing:

* the cool overlay
* the clever Forge demo
* the smooth takeover video
* the autonomous-feeling behavior
* the multi-node orchestration

Quality Gates exist partly to resist charisma.

A feature should not pass because it feels impressive.
It should pass because it is real, safe, and product-honest.

### 26.7.3 Gates should produce builder humility

One of the best side effects of strong gates is that they teach the team not to overclaim.

That matters because Francis itself is supposed to model calibrated truth.

The build culture should mirror that discipline.

---

## 26.8 How Quality Gates Interact with Receipts

Receipts are not just one more artifact.
They are one of the core validation tools for the gates themselves.

### 26.8.1 Receipts prove work happened

A stage that claims:

* action occurred
* progress occurred
* validation occurred
* promotion occurred
* mission advanced
* handback completed
* Away work happened

must be able to support those claims through receipts.

This is true at both runtime and build-quality level.

### 26.8.2 Receipt discipline is itself a gate

A stage that introduces meaningful action but cannot produce complete receipts has not passed quality.

That is especially true for:

* execution
* Takeover
* Away
* capability promotion
* Federation handoff
* Swarm delegation

### 26.8.3 Receipt quality supports debugging and trust

Good receipts are not only for audits.

They are also how builders debug failures and how users trust what Francis says happened.

A weak receipt layer weakens all three gate families at once.

---

## 26.9 How Quality Gates Interact with Codex Build Cadence

Because the roadmap is intended to guide actual build execution, the gates must fit the implementation cadence.

### 26.9.1 The cadence should always include gate completion

Every implementation cycle should naturally end with something like:

1. restate the user-experience objective
2. name the affected modules/files
3. implement coherent full-file changes
4. run quality gates
5. return receipts

That final structure matters because it keeps quality from becoming optional.

### 26.9.2 “Return receipts” is part of gate closure

The gate is not fully passed until the implementation returns evidence of what changed and what passed.

This keeps builder behavior aligned with Francis’s own product law.

### 26.9.3 Codex work must avoid shipping gate debt forward

A dangerous habit would be:

* “we’ll fix the tests later”
* “we’ll add scope tests next stage”
* “we’ll tighten receipts after the demo”
* “we’ll improve product clarity after the core behavior works”

That kind of debt compounds badly in a system like Francis.

The cadence must resist it.

---

## 26.10 Quality Gate Failure Modes

This section must explicitly name the ways gate culture can go wrong.

### 26.10.1 Gates drift into checkbox theater

Symptoms:

* tests run, but do not meaningfully validate the feature
* gates exist as ritual, not as protection
* everyone assumes “green” means “good” even when the wrong things were tested

Correction:

* re-center gates around real doctrine-bearing risk and user experience

### 26.10.2 Gates drift into code-only validation

Symptoms:

* lint/tests pass
* but the feature still weakens governance or confuses users
* product-surface truth is not being checked

Correction:

* re-center Safety Quality and Product Quality as equal gate families

### 26.10.3 Gates drift into slow bureaucracy

Symptoms:

* gates become heavy process
* builders fear iteration
* quality becomes paperwork rather than useful protection

Correction:

* re-center gates as sharp, meaningful checks tied directly to system law and stage outcomes

### 26.10.4 Gates drift into uneven enforcement

Symptoms:

* glamorous features get looser gates
* boring infrastructure gets held to higher standards
* power-heavy stages slip through on charisma

* re-center stronger gates for stronger power, not weaker

## 26.11 Quality Gates Doctrine Statement

If Francis Quality Gates had to be defined in one doctrine sentence for builders, it would be this:

> Every Francis stage must pass explicit Code Quality, Safety Quality, and Product Quality gates—covering correctness, scope and policy discipline, adversarial and receipt integrity, and user-legible lived experience—so that no new power, visibility, continuity, or autonomy enters the system without being mechanically sound, lawfully bounded, and honestly expressed to the user.

That sentence should be strong enough to reject weak “good enough for now” culture early.

---

## 26.12 Quality Gates Acceptance Criteria

Section 26 is satisfied only if the roadmap, build cadence, CI discipline, runtime testing, product review, and release readiness model remain aligned to all of the following:

* every stage is gated by Code Quality, Safety Quality, and Product Quality rather than code checks alone
* code quality includes lint, test execution, type checks where configured, runtime integrity, and stable internal contracts
* safety quality includes policy tests, scope escape tests, adversarial tests where relevant, receipt completeness, and control-path integrity
* product quality verifies that the user-experience objective of the stage is actually visible, understandable, and aligned with the doctrine
* no stage is considered complete merely because code merged or demos well
* stronger stages with greater power receive proportionally stronger gate expectations
* receipt discipline is treated as a cross-cutting quality requirement rather than an optional audit add-on
* gate culture resists charisma, novelty, and schedule pressure when they conflict with operator law
* the build cadence always includes running gates and returning receipts
* gate failure modes are explicitly recognized and corrected during buildout
* Francis never degrades into a system that is technically impressive but weakly verified, under-governed, or product-misleading under the excuse of moving fast

Send the final section and I’ll finish the roadmap in the same cadence.
